Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Fourth Madhhab: Known vs. Unknown Specifying Evidence
Presents a fourth opinion on 'amm after takhsis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fourth madhhab's position on the status of 'amm after takhsis, and apply it to distinguish between known and unknown specifying evidence.

Core Idea
The effect of specification on 'amm depends on whether the specifying evidence is known or unknown.

In this lesson, we encounter a fourth position within Hanafi usul that synthesizes earlier debates. The author of al-Tawdih proposed that the reliability of a general text after specification hinges on the specifying evidence itself: if that evidence is well-known, the general remains definitive; if unknown, the general becomes unreliable. This nuanced view bridges the second and third madhahib.

Guided Path
1
Recall the second and third madhahib
The second madhhab (Karkhi, 'Isa ibn Aban) held that 'amm after takhsis becomes zanni and cannot be used for inference. The third madhhab (some Hanafis) held it remains qat'i. Briefly review these opposing positions.
These are the two poles.
2
Identify the fourth madhhab's distinction
The fourth madhhab, attributed to Sahib al-Tawdih, introduces a condition: it considers whether the specifying evidence (dalil al-khusus) is known (ma'lum) or unknown (majhul). If unknown, 'amm is like the second madhhab; if known, it is like the third.
3
Apply the distinction to a test case
Take the general command 'اقتلوا المشركين' (kill the polytheists) and specification by 'لا تقتلوا النساء والصبيان' (do not kill women and children). The specifying evidence is known and clear, so 'amm remains definitive per this view. Contrast with a vague exception like 'إلا بعضهم' (except some of them) where the specifying evidence is unknown, making 'amm unreliable.
This shows practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذہب رابع: یہاں ایک چوتھا مذہب بھی ہے جس کو صاحب توضیح نے ذکر کیا ہے وہ یہ ہے کہ دلیل خصوص پر مدار ہے لہذا جب دلیل خصوص مجہول ہو تو عام سے استدلال کرنا ساقط ہو گا کما قال اصحاب المذہب الثانی اور اگر دلیل خصوص معلوم ہو تو عام خصوص کے بعد بھی ایسا ہی قطعی رہے گا جیسکہ پہلے تھا کما قال اصحاب المذهب الثالث اور اس سے استدلال کرنا درست ہوگا ۔ قوله : والعموم اما أن يكون بالصيغة والمعنى أو بالمعنى لا غير كرجال وقوم .
Fourth madhhab: There is also a fourth madhhab which the author of al-Tawdih mentioned. It is that the [effect] depends on the specifying evidence. Therefore, when the specifying evidence is unknown, inference from the general is invalid, as the proponents of the second madhhab said. And if the specifying evidence is known, then the general after specification remains definitive as it was before, as the proponents of the third madhhab said, and inference from it is valid. His saying: And generality is either by wording and meaning, or by meaning only, like rijal and qawm.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This brief passage introduces a fourth position on the status of 'amm after takhsis, attributed to the author of al-Tawdih (Sadr al-Shari'ah). This position synthesizes the second and third madhahib by distinguishing between two types of specifying evidence: known (معلوم) and unknown (مجہول). If the evidence specifying the general is unknown in its extent or identity, the general becomes unreliable and cannot be used for inference — this aligns with the second madhhab of Karkhi and 'Isa ibn Aban. However, if the specifying evidence is known and well-defined, the general remains definitive and valid for inference, just as the third madhhab holds. The passage ends by quoting the matn which begins a new topic: the division of 'amm into types based on how generality is indicated — by wording and meaning, or by meaning alone, with examples like rijal (men, a plural) and qawm (a group, singular in form but plural in meaning). This marks a transition from discussing the effect of specification to the nature of generality itself.

Key Terms
دلیل خصوص (Dalil al-Khusus): The evidence that specifies a general statement, indicating which individuals are excluded
مجہول (Majhul): Unknown; here referring to a specifying evidence whose scope or identity is not clearly known
معلوم (Ma'lum): Known; a specifying evidence that is clearly defined and understood

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth madhhab mentioned by Sahib al-Tawdih, when can a general text still be used for inference after specification?
A) Only when the specification is unknown.
B) Only when the specification is known.
C) Never, because specification always nullifies the general.
D) Always, because specification does not affect the general's strength.

Key Takeaway
The applicability of 'amm after takhsis depends on the certainty of the specifying evidence: known preserves qat'iyyah, unknown invalidates inference.

Classification of 'Amm: By Lafz and Ma'na vs. By Ma'na Only
Classifies 'amm into two types based on how generality is indicated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a general term (amm) as either 'amm by wording and meaning' or 'amm by meaning only,' and identify the scholarly disagreement regarding indefinite plurals.

Core Idea
'Amm can arise from word form or from collective meaning; scholars differ on whether indefinite plurals are truly general.

In this lesson, we break down a key classification from Hanafi usul: how do we know when a term is general ('amm)? The answer depends on whether the wording itself shows generality, or only the meaning. The passage presents two types and highlights a subtle but important disagreement among Hanafi scholars about indefinite plurals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first type of 'amm
Find the type where both wording (sigha) and meaning (ma'na) indicate generality. Note the examples given: rijal and nisa (plurals). Understand that this applies to both definite and indefinite plurals according to Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi.
Bazdawi does not require complete comprehensiveness (isti'ab).
2
Identify the second type of 'amm
Find the type where the wording is singular but the meaning is plural, like qawm and raht. Note their specific numerical ranges.
These terms require the individuals to be gathered together (for qawm).
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
The passage contrasts Bazdawi's view (no condition of isti'ab) with the view of Sahib al-Tawdih (isti'ab required). Under Tawdih's view, indefinite plurals are not true 'amm but an intermediate category.
This affects how we interpret general statements in legal texts.
4
Apply the classification to new terms
Given a new Arabic term (e.g., a collective noun or a plural), determine which type of 'amm it belongs to according to each scholar's criteria.
Practice with examples from Quran and Hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) باعتبار صیغہ و معنی عام کی تقسیم

عام کی دو قسمیں ہیں:
(1) صیغہ اور معنی دونوں عموم پر دلالت کرتے ہوں اور افراد پر مشتمل ہوں جیسے کہ رجال جمع رجل اور نساء جمع امراة وغیرہ خواہ جمع معرف ہو یا جمع منکر اور خواہ قلت ہو یا کثرت لیکن یہ فخر الاسلام رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک ہے کیونکہ اس کے نزدیک عام کے معنی میں استیعاب اور استغراق شرط نہیں ہے لیکن جن لوگوں کے نزدیک استیعاب شرط ہے جیسا کہ صاحب توضیح نے اشارہ کیا ہے وہ جمع منکر کو عام اور خاص کے درمیان واسطہ کہتے ہیں
(2) دوسری قسم یہ ہے کہ اس عام کا صیغہ تو عموم پر دلالت نہ کرتا ہو لیکن معنی عموم پر دلالت کرتا ہو جیسے (قوم) اور (رہ) یہ دونوں لفظا مفرد میں ہیں لیکن باعتبار معنی جمع ہیں کیونکہ قوم کا اطلاق تین سے لیکر دس تک ہوتا ہے اور رحط کا اطلاق تین سے نو تک ہوتا ہے۔ لیکن قوم کا اطلاق وہیں پر ہو گا جہاں افراد مجتمع ہوں۔
(1) Division of 'amm according to wording and meaning

'Amm has two types:
(1) Both the wording and meaning indicate generality and include individuals, such as rijal (plural of rajul) and nisa (plural of imra'a), etc., whether the plural is definite or indefinite, and whether it indicates few or many. But this is according to Fakhr al-Islam, may Allah have mercy on him, because in his view, comprehensiveness and inclusion (isti'ab and istighraq) are not conditions for the meaning of 'amm. However, those who consider comprehensiveness as a condition, as indicated by the author of al-Tawdih, call the indefinite plural an intermediary between 'amm and khass.
(2) The second type is that the wording of this 'amm does not indicate generality, but the meaning does indicate generality, like (qawm) and (rah). These two words are singular in form, but in terms of meaning they are plural, because qawm is applied from three to ten, and raht is applied from three to nine. However, qawm is applied only where individuals are gathered together.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of 'amm (general term) from the perspective of how generality is expressed: through the wording (صیغہ) and/or the meaning (معنی). The first type includes words where both the morphological form (like the plural pattern) and the lexical meaning indicate generality and encompass all individuals of the category. Examples include رجال (men, the plural of رجل) and نساء (women, the plural of امرأة). This holds whether the plural is definite or indefinite, and whether it denotes few or many. The commentator notes that this classification follows Fakhr al-Islam (al-Bazdawi), who does not require the general to include every single individual (استیعاب واستغراق). According to scholars who do require such comprehensiveness — as alluded to by Sahib al-Tawdih — the indefinite plural is considered an intermediate category between 'amm and khass. The second type consists of words that are singular in form (صیغہ مفرد) but plural in meaning (معنی جمع). Examples are قوم (qawm, a group of people) and رہ (raht, a group up to ten). Both words refer to collectives: qawm applies to groups of three to ten individuals, and raht to three to nine. However, qawm specifically requires the individuals to be assembled together. This classification is important for identifying when a term can be considered general and how it should be interpreted in legal texts.

Key Terms
عام ('Amm): A term that indicates generality, either by its form or its meaning, encompassing multiple individuals
صیغہ (Sigha): The morphological or grammatical form of a word
معنی (Ma'na): The meaning or semantic content of a word
قوم (Qawm): A group of people, applied to three to ten individuals; singular in form but plural in meaning
رہط (Raht): A group of up to ten, specifically from three to nine; singular in form but plural in meaning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Fakhr al-Islam (al-Bazdawi), what is NOT a condition for a term to be considered 'amm?
A) The term must be a plural form.
B) The term must include all individuals comprehensively (isti'ab).
C) The term must indicate generality by both wording and meaning.
D) The term can be definite or indefinite.

Key Takeaway
The classification of 'amm into two types (by wording and meaning vs. by meaning only) hinges on how generality is expressed, with a key dispute over whether indefinite plurals qualify as 'amm.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Fourth Madhhab: When Does Takhsis Invalidate the General?
Stating the fourth madhhab regarding the effect of known vs unknown specific evidence on generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fourth madhhab on the effect of known vs unknown specific evidence on generality and apply it to identify when al-'amm can be used as proof.

Core Idea
The authority of a general text depends on whether the specifying evidence is known or unknown.

In Hanafi usul, one key debate is whether a general text (al-'amm) retains its probative force after the discovery of a specific evidence (al-khass) that excepts some of its applications. The fourth madhhab, reported by the author of al-Tawdih, offers a nuanced middle ground: it all depends on whether the specific evidence is known or unknown. This lesson unpacks that position and its practical implications for istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two extreme positions
Recall that the second madhhab holds that takhsis always removes the authority of the 'amm, while the third madhhab holds that 'amm remains decisive even after takhsis. State these in one sentence each.
These are referenced but not named in the passage.
2
State the fourth madhhab's position
According to the fourth madhhab, the ruling depends on the specific evidence itself (daleel al-khass). If the khass is unknown (majhool), the 'amm cannot be used for deduction. If the khass is known (ma'loom), the 'amm remains qat'i and usable.
This synthesizes the two extremes.
3
Explain the rationale
When the khass is unknown, we cannot determine the extent of the exception, so the 'amm becomes ambiguous (mujmal). When the khass is known, we can precisely apply the general to the remaining individuals, so it retains its decisive character.
This logic is implied but not explicit in the text.
4
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
Give a concrete example: if a hadith specifying a minimum threshold for theft is unknown to a jurist, he cannot use the general verse 'cut the hand' as proof. If the hadith is known, he can use the verse for cases that meet the condition.
This shows how the ruling affects actual istinbat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذہب رابع : یہاں ایک چوتھا مذ ہب بھی ہے جس کو صاحب توضیح نے ذکر کیا ہے وہ یہ ہے کہ دلیل خصوص پر مدار ہے لہذا جب دلیل خصوص مجہول ہو تو عام سے استدلال کرنا ساقط ہو گا کما قال اصحاب المذہب الثانی اور اگر دلیل خصوص معلوم ہو تو عام خصوص کے بعد بھی ایسا ہی قطعی رہے گا جیسکہ پہلے تھا کما قال اصحاب المذهب الثالث اور اس سے استدلال کرنا درست ہوگا ۔
Fourth madhhab: There is also a fourth madhhab mentioned by the author of al-Tawdih, and it is that the ruling depends on the specific evidence (daleel khas). Therefore, when the specific evidence is unknown, using the general (al-'amm) for deduction is invalid, as said by the adherents of the second madhhab. And if the specific evidence is known, then the general remains as decisive after the specific as it was before, as said by the adherents of the third madhhab, and using it for deduction is valid.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth opinion (madhhab) in a usul dispute about how the existence of a specific evidence (takhsis) affects the generality of a term. The author of al-Tawdih reports that according to this view, the validity of using 'Amm as evidence depends on whether the specific evidence is known or unknown. If the specific evidence is unknown (majhool), then the 'Amm cannot be relied upon because we don't know the extent of the exception. If it is known (ma'loom), then the 'Amm retains its decisive (qat'i) status even after the exception, because we can properly apply it to the remaining individuals. This is a middle position between the second madhhab (which likely held that 'Amm is always dropped with takhsis) and the third madhhab (which held that 'Amm remains intact). It highlights the nuance in Hanafi usul regarding the interaction of general and specific texts.

Key Terms
عام (aamm): A general term that applies to all individuals of its category without restriction.
خاص (khaass): A specific term or evidence that restricts the application of a general term.
دلیل خصوص (daleel al-khass): Evidence that specifies or excepts from a general ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth madhhab, when is it valid to use a general ('amm) as evidence?
A) Only when no specific evidence exists
B) Only when specific evidence is unknown
C) When specific evidence is known
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The fourth madhhab conditions the use of 'amm on the knowledge of the khass: unknown khass invalidates, known khass preserves the general's decisiveness.

The Particles 'Man' and 'Ma': Generality and Default Usage
Usul definition of particles 'man' and 'ma'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the original ruling of generality for 'man' and 'ma', identify their default usage for rational/non-rational beings, and explain how a context (qarina) can override this default.

Core Idea
'Man' and 'ma' are originally general; 'man' defaults to rational, 'ma' to non-rational, but context can specify.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding the particles 'man' (who) and 'ma' (what) is crucial for interpreting legal texts from the Quran and Hadith. This passage establishes that both particles are inherently general (amm) in their origin, meaning they encompass all individuals within their category unless a specific indicator narrows them. It also clarifies their default domains: 'man' primarily refers to rational beings (humans, angels, jinn), while 'ma' refers to non-rational beings (objects, animals, concepts). Recognizing this default helps us accurately apply texts like 'man shahida minkumu sh-shahra' (whoever witnesses the month) and 'ma malakat aymanukum' (what your right hands possess).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two particles and their default usage
The passage introduces 'man' and 'ma'. Note that 'man' is for rational beings (dhawat man ya'qil) and 'ma' is for non-rational beings (dhawat ma la ya'qil). This is the default ('asl') assignment.
'Man' includes humans and other rational agents; 'ma' covers everything else.
2
Understand the concept of 'original generality'
Both particles are originally general (amm), meaning they indicate all members of their category. The passage says 'asluhuma al-umum' (their origin is generality). For example, 'man' in a command includes every rational being unless specified otherwise.
Generality is the default – a qarina (context) is needed to make them specific.
3
Recognize the role of context (qarina)
The passage notes that sometimes, due to a context, 'man' can be used for non-rational beings and 'ma' for rational beings. This is an exception. For instance, in 'ma malakat aymanukum', 'ma' is used for slaves (who are rational), so the default shifted because of the linguistic context.
In legal interpretation, first assume generality; then check for any contextual specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ومن وما يحتملان العموم والخصوص واصلهما العموم ومن في ذوات من يعقل كما فى ذوات ما لا يعقل . من اور ما کا مفہوم اور وجہ فرق من اور ما اصل کے اعتبار سے عموم کے لئے ہیں خصوص کا احتمال بھی رکھتے ہیں اور وجد فرق یہ ہے کہ من ذوی العقول کے لئے استعمال ہوتا ہے اور کبھی کبھی کسی قرینہ کی وجہ سے غیر ذوی العقول کے لئے بھی استعمال ہوتا ہے اور مامیں اس کا بر عکس ہے۔
He said: And 'man' and 'ma' are susceptible to both generality and specificity, and their origin is generality. 'Man' is for rational beings (dhawat man ya'qil) just as 'ma' is for non-rational beings (dhawat ma la ya'qil). The meaning of 'man' and 'ma' and the reason for the difference: 'Man' and 'ma' are originally for generality, but they also carry the possibility of specificity. The difference is that 'man' is used for rational beings, and sometimes due to a context it is used for non-rational beings, while 'ma' is the opposite.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This short passage explains the usul of the particles 'man' (who) and 'ma' (what) in Arabic. In Hanafi usul, these particles are originally assumed to be general (implies all individuals of their category) unless there is evidence to specify them. The key difference is that 'man' is primarily used for rational beings (people), while 'ma' is for non-rational beings (objects, animals, etc.). However, this is not absolute; with a clear context (qarina), 'man' can be used for non-rational and 'ma' for rational. This is important when interpreting legal texts: e.g., 'man shā'a' (whoever wishes) refers to rational agents like slaves, while 'mā malakat aymānukum' (what your right hands possess) refers to non-rational property.

Key Terms
من (man): Particle originally general, used for rational beings.
ما (ma): Particle originally general, used for non-rational beings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the original (asl) status of 'man' and 'ma'?
A) Both are specific (khass)
B) Both are general (amm)
C) 'Man' is general, 'ma' is specific
D) 'Ma' is general, 'man' is specific

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the particles 'man' and 'ma' are originally general, with 'man' defaulting to rational beings and 'ma' to non-rational, but both can be specified by a contextual qarina.

Linguistic Analysis of 'Man' with Fatha vs Kasra in Manumission
Applying 'man' vowel principle to manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that 'man' with fatha indicates generality and with kasra indicates partiality to determine the legal effect in manumission statements.

Core Idea
A single vowel difference on 'man' changes manumission from all becoming free to a choice.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, subtle linguistic features carry immense legal weight. This passage examines how the vowel on the particle 'man'—fatha vs kasra—determines whether a manumission statement frees all slaves or leaves a choice. Understanding this distinction refines your ability to read 'amm and khass in commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source texts
Locate the two hypothetical manumission statements: one with 'man shā'a' (fatha) and one with 'man shi'ta' (kasra). Note how each is used in a different context.
The first statement is a condition ('whoever wishes'), the second is a delegation ('whomever you wish').
2
Classify the particle 'man' in each
In the first, 'man' is with fatha, indicating generality (umum) and explanation (bayan). In the second, 'man' is with kasra, indicating partiality (tab'id).
Vowel alone changes the meaning in Hanafi usul.
3
Apply the Hanafi usul principle
For 'man' with fatha, the principle 'al-amm yufidu al-umum' applies: the word covers all instances. For 'man' with kasra, the principle 'al-kasr yufidu al-tab'id' applies: the word indicates a part, not the whole.
These principles are derived from classical Arabic usage.
4
Derive the ruling
With fatha: if all slaves wish freedom, all become free. With kasra: the addressee may free any number but must leave at least one slave unfree.
The ruling is the legal effect (athar) of the linguistic form.
5
Note the alternative explanation (al-Tawdih)
The author of al-Tawdih suggests that in both cases 'man' is for partiality, but in the first the attribute of 'wishing' distributes generally, causing all to be included.
This shows depth and flexibility in Hanafi tafsir al-alfaz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله فاذا قال من شاء من عبيدى العتق فهو حر فشاء واعتقوا من کے عموم پر تفریح : چنانچہ اگر کسی نے یوں کہا من شاء من عبيدى العتق فهو حر پر تمام غلاموں نے آزادی کو چاہ لیا تو سب آزاد ہو جائیں گے اس لئے کہ کلہ من عام ہے اور موصوف ہے صفت عامہ کے ساتھ یعنی مشعیت اور من ”بالکہ " یہاں بیان کیلئے ہے لیکن اگر کسی نے کہا من شئت من عبيدی عنقه فاعتقه تو اس صورت میں مخاطب کو ایک کے علاوہ سب کو آزاد کرنے کا اختیار ہے اس لئے کہ کلمہ من عام ہے اور من "بالکسر تبعیض کیلئے ہے اور ان دونوں عموم اور تبعیض ) کے متقضی پر اس وقت عمل ہو سکتا ہے جب کہ ایک کے علاوہ باقی سب آزاد ہو جائیں۔ صاحب توضیح کی توجیہ: - صاحب توضیح نے اس مسئلے کی دوسری توجیہ بیان فرماتے ہوئے تحریر کیا ہے کہ دونوں مثالوں میں من جارو تبعیض ہی کیلئے ہے مگر وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ پہلی مثال میں (من شاء من عبيدى العنق فهو حر ) میں من کے عموم کے تحت ہر غلام داخل ہے جو اپنی آزادی کا متمنی ہو
He said: For example, if one says, 'Whoever among my slaves wishes to be free, he is free,' and they all wished and they (were) freed. Elaboration on the generality of 'man': So if someone said, 'Whoever among my slaves wishes to be free, he is free,' and all the slaves desired freedom, then all become free because the word 'man' is general and is described with a general attribute, i.e., will, and 'man' (with fatha) here is for explanation. But if someone said, 'Whomever you wish among my slaves to free, so free him,' then in that case the addressee has the option to free all except one, because the word 'man' is general and 'man' (with kasra) is for partiality. And the implication of both generality and partiality can only be acted upon when all except one are freed. Explanation of the author of al-Tawdih: The author of al-Tawdih, in another explanation of this issue, wrote that in both examples, 'man' is a particle for partiality, but the difference is that in the first example ('man shā'a min 'abīdī al-'itq fa huwa hurr'), every slave who desires his freedom is included under the generality of 'man'.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage illustrates how the slightest change in vowel (fatha vs kasra) on the particle 'man' can alter the legal effect in Hanafi fiqh. When the master says 'man shā'a min 'abīdī al-'itq fa huwa hurr' (whoever among my slaves wishes freedom, he is free), 'man' is with fatha, indicating generality and explanation (bayān). If all slaves wish freedom, all become free. When the master says 'man shi'ta min 'abīdī 'itq' (whomever you wish among my slaves to free), 'man' is with kasra, indicating partiality (tab'īd), so the addressee has the discretion to free up to all but one (i.e., must leave at least one unfree). The author of al-Tawdih offers an alternative explanation: in both cases 'man' is for partiality, but in the first, the generality is due to the attribute of will which applies to all slaves. This shows the depth of Hanafi linguistic analysis in deriving rulings.

Key Terms
من (بالفتح) (man (bil-fath)): 'Man' with fatha indicates generality and explanation (bayān).
من (بالكسر) (man (bil-kasr)): 'Man' with kasra indicates partiality (tab'īd) and restriction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All slaves become free
B) The addressee can free any number he wishes
C) The addressee must free all except one
D) No slaves are freed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the vowel on 'man' determines whether the ruling is general or partial, changing manumission from automatic freedom to a discretionary choice.

Default Generality of 'Man' and 'Ma' and Contextual Restriction
Explaining the default generality of 'man' and 'ma' and their metaphorical crossover.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the particles 'man' and 'ma' in Arabic legal texts, classify their default status as general or specific, and demonstrate how contextual indicators (qarina) can override the default generality.

Core Idea
'man' and 'ma' intrinsically indicate generality; context alone specifies.

Understanding the default generality of particles like 'man' and 'ma' is foundational in Hanafi usul. This passage from the Sharh explains that both particles originally denote generality, with 'man' for rational beings and 'ma' for non-rational. However, due to contextual indicators (qarina), they can be used metaphorically for the other category. This principle is crucial for interpreting legal language, as demonstrated by the examples of slave manumission and conditional freeing of a slave-girl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling for 'man' and 'ma'
The text states 'their original [meaning] is generality' (واصلهما العموم). Recognize that this is the default position unless a qarina indicates otherwise.
This is a key principle in Hanafi usul: particles are general until specified.
2
Distinguish the domains of 'man' and 'ma'
'man' is primarily used for rational beings (ذوات من يعقل), while 'ma' is for non-rational beings (ذوات ما لا يعقل).
This distinction is based on linguistic convention, not a revealed ruling.
3
Apply the rule to the first example
In 'من شاء من عبيدى العتق فهو حر', 'man' is used generally. Therefore, all slaves who will freedom become free; the ruling applies to every individual slave.
The generality is clear because there is no qarina restricting it to a specific slave.
4
Apply the rule to the second example
In 'ان كان ما في بطنك غلاما فأنت حرة', 'ma' is used generally for the contents of the womb. The condition requires the entire content to be male; since she gives birth to both male and female, the condition fails, and she is not freed.
Here, the generality of 'ma' is restricted by the conditional phrase 'ghulaman' (a male), but the actual outcome shows the condition not fully met.
5
Recognize metaphorical crossover
The passage concludes that 'ma' can be used metaphorically like 'man' and can apply to rational beings as well.
This flexibility allows for interpretation when context demands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ومن وما يحتملان العموم والخصوص واصلهما العموم ومن في ذوات من يعقل كما في ذوات ما لا يعقل . قوله فاذا قال من شاء من عبيدى العتق فهو حر فشاء واعتقوا. قوله فان قال لامته ان كان ما في بطنك غلاما فأنت حرة فولدت غلاما وجارية لم تعتق. وما يجنى بمعنى من مجازا ويدخل في صفات من يعقل أيضا .
He said: 'man' and 'ma' both imply generality and specificity, and their original [meaning] is generality. 'man' is used for beings with intellect, as is 'ma' for beings without intellect. He said: So if he says 'Whoever among my slaves wills freedom, he is free' and they will and are freed [they become free]. He said: If he says to his slave-girl 'If what is in your womb is a male, then you are free' and she gives birth to a male and a female, she is not freed. And 'ma' is used metaphorically in the meaning of 'man' and enters into the attributes of beings with intellect as well.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh discusses the particles 'man' (whoever) and 'ma' (whatever) in Usul al-Fiqh. The base matn states that both originally indicate generality (عموم), but can also be used for specificity (خصوص). The distinction: 'man' is primarily for rational beings (ذوات من يعقل) and 'ma' for non-rational (ذوات ما لا يعقل). However, due to a qarina (contextual indicator), each can be used for the other metaphorically. The examples clarify: first, 'man shaa min 'abidi' (whoever among my slaves wills) shows 'man' in its general sense, so if all slaves will freedom, all become free. Second, 'ma fi batnik' (what is in your womb) uses 'ma' generally, so if the contents are not entirely male, the condition fails. The last phrase notes that 'ma' can be used metaphorically like 'man' and even applied to rational beings. This is a standard Hanafi position that particles have a default meaning but can shift with context.

Key Terms
من (man): A particle used primarily for rational beings, originally indicating generality.
ما (ma): A particle used primarily for non-rational beings, originally indicating generality.
عموم (umum): Generality, covering all individuals of the category without exception.
خصوص (khusus): Specificity, restriction to a subset.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The master may choose which to free.

Key Takeaway
The default generality of 'man' and 'ma' is a foundational principle in Hanafi usul; only a clear contextual indicator (qarina) can restrict them.

The Generality of 'Ma' and Its Contextual Restriction
Explains 'ma' in generalities with a fiqh example and its metaphorical use for rational beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the generality of the particle 'ma' in Arabic, apply the Hanafi principle of 'umum to scriptural texts, and explain how contextual qualification can restrict its scope.

Core Idea
Context can restrict the generality of 'ma' in Arabic.

This lesson explores how the simple Arabic word 'ma' carries a default generality in Hanafi usul. Using a fiqh example and a Quranic verse, we will see how that generality operates and when it can be limited by accompanying words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base principle
In Hanafi usul, the particle 'ma' indicates generality ('umum) over all individuals of its referent unless restricted by context.
This is a default rule: 'ma' is considered 'amm.
2
Apply to the fiqh example
In the example, 'ma fi batniki' (what is in your womb) requires all contents to be male for the condition to be fulfilled. Since the slave woman gives birth to both a male and a female, the condition fails.
The generality of 'ma' distributes over each individual in the womb.
3
Recognize the objection
If 'ma' always implies generality, then the Quranic verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Quran' would obligate reciting the entire Quran, which is impractical.
This objection challenges the apparent generality of 'ma' in that verse.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The word 'tayassara' (is easy) restricts the generality. It is derived from 'taysir' (ease), so the verse means to recite whatever is easy, not the entire Quran. Thus, context can limit 'ma'.
The restriction comes from a verbal qualifier, not from rejecting 'umum.
5
Note the metaphorical extension
'Ma' is sometimes used metaphorically in place of 'man' for rational beings, as in Quranic examples: 'wa ma banaha' (by the sky and what built it) referring to Allah, and 'fankihu ma taba lakum' (marry what is good for you) referring to women.
This shows that 'ma' can denote rational beings by way of majaz (metaphor).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فان قال لامته ان كان ما في بطنك غلاما فأنت حرة فولدت غلاما وجارية لم تعتق. ما کے عموم کی مثال .... مام محمد رحمہ اللہ نے ایک مثال ذکر کی ہے کہ اگر کوئی شخص اپنی باندی سے کہے ان كان ما في بطنك غلاماً فأنت حرة اور دہ باندی ایک بچہ اور ایک بچی بنے تو وہ آزاد نہیں ہوگی کیونکہ ما کا عموم تقاضا کرتا ہے کہ جمیع مافی البطن بچہ ( نظام ہو ) خواہ جتنے بھی ہوں اعتراض اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ اگر ما میں تقسیم کو لازمی مان لیا جائے تو پھر ضروری ہے کہ نماز میں نمازی کیلئے سارا قرآن پڑھنا واجب ہو، جتنا کوئی پڑھ سکتا ہو کیونکہ ما تیسر من القرآن میں بھی ما عموم کیلئے ہے۔ جواب ہم جواب دیتے ہیں کہ ما کا عموم تو اسی کا متقاضی ہے مگر اگلا لفظ تیر مشتق ہے تیسیر سے اور تیسر کا معنی یہ ہے کہ اتنا قرآن پڑھے جتنا آسانی سے پڑھ سکے اور سارے قرآن کا پڑھنا آسانی کے منافی ہے۔ قوله : وما يجنى بمعنى من مجاراً ويدخل في صفات من يعقل أيضا . مجھنے... ما اور من مجاز ایک دوسرے کی جگہ استعمال ہوتے ہیں ۔ جیسے اللہ تعالی کا فرمان ہے والسماء وما بناها ( یہاں ، ہمعنی من ہے ( اسی طرح فانكحوا ما طاب لكم ہے۔ ما ذوی العقول کیلئے آجاتا ہے مثلاً ( ما زید) کے جواب میں (الکریم ) کہنا۔
He said: If he says to his slave woman, 'If what is in your womb is a male, you are free,' and she gives birth to a male and a female, she is not freed. Example of the generality of 'ma'... Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned an example: If a man says to his slave woman, 'If what is in your womb is a male, you are free,' and she gives birth to a male and a female, she will not be freed, because the generality of 'ma' requires that all that is in the womb be male (system), however many there are. Objection: If it is necessary to consider distribution in 'ma', then it would be obligatory for a person in prayer to recite all of the Quran, as much as one can recite, because in 'ma tayassara min al-Quran' (whatever is easy of the Quran) 'ma' is for generality. Reply: We reply that the generality of 'ma' indeed requires that, but the following word 'tayassara' is derived from 'taysir', and 'taysir' means to recite as much as is easy, and reciting the entire Quran is contrary to ease. He said: And 'ma' is used metaphorically in place of 'man' and enters into attributes of rational beings as well. 'Ma' and 'man' are used metaphorically in place of each other. For example, Allah's saying, 'And the sky and what built it' – here 'ma' is in the meaning of 'man'. Similarly, 'marry what is good for you' – 'ma' comes for rational beings. For example, in response to 'What is Zaid?', one says 'The generous one.'
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage discusses the particle 'ma' in Arabic and its usage for generality (umum) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author first provides a fiqh example: a man says to his slave woman, 'If what is in your womb is a male, you are free.' She gives birth to both a male and a female. The ruling is that she is not freed because 'ma' (what) implies generality, requiring all the contents of the womb to be male. Since one is female, the condition is not met. The passage then addresses an objection: if 'ma' always implies generality, then the Quranic verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Quran' (recite what is easy of the Quran) would require reciting the entire Quran, which is impossible. The reply is that the word 'tayassara' (is easy) itself restricts the generality, as it is derived from 'taysir' (ease). Therefore, the ruling in prayer is to recite whatever is easy, not the entire Quran. Finally, the passage notes that 'ma' can be used metaphorically in place of 'man' for rational beings, citing Quranic examples: 'wal-sama'i wa ma banaha' (by the sky and what built it) where 'ma' refers to Allah, and 'fankihu ma taba lakum' (marry what is good for you) where 'ma' refers to women. This shows that 'ma' is sometimes used for rational beings.

Key Terms
ما (ma): A particle used for generality (umum) in Arabic, often indicating a universal scope over its object.
عموم (umum): Generality; a term covering all individuals or parts of its referent.
تبعيض (tab'id): Distribution; implying that a ruling applies to each individual separately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The particle 'ma' defaults to generality in Hanafi usul, but can be restricted by contextual qualifiers like 'tayassara', and may be used metaphorically for rational beings.

The Rule of 'Kull': Indefinite vs Definite Nouns
Explains the Hanafi rule of 'kull' for generality and its distinction between individuals and parts based on noun definiteness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of 'kull' to determine whether generality applies to individuals or parts based on the definiteness of the noun.

Core Idea
When 'kull' precedes an indefinite noun, it generalizes over individuals; when before a definite noun, it generalizes over parts.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the word 'kull' (every/all) is a key tool for expressing generality (umum). However, its scope changes depending on whether the noun it modifies is indefinite (nakirah) or definite (ma'rifah). This lesson unpacks that distinction using fiqh examples from divorce and everyday logic, enabling you to correctly interpret statements containing 'kull'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the noun after 'kull'
Look at the noun following 'kull'. Determine if it is indefinite (e.g., without 'al-') or definite (with 'al-' or a possessive).
Indefinite nouns refer to any member of a class; definite nouns refer to a specific entity or the whole class as a unit.
2
Apply the Hanafi rule
If the noun is indefinite, 'kull' generalizes over all individuals (afrad) of that noun. If the noun is definite, 'kull' generalizes over all parts (ajza') of that noun.
This is the core rule: individuals vs. parts.
3
Test with a fiqh example
Consider a husband saying to his wife 'anti taliqun kulla talaqatin' (indefinite 'talaqatin'). Here 'kull' triggers each individual divorce, resulting in three divorces. If he says 'kulla al-talaqati' (definite), only one divorce occurs because the definite 'al-talaqati' is a single entity without divisible parts.
The practical outcome differs significantly.
4
Test with a logical example
Examine the statements 'kullu rummanin ma'kul' (indefinite: every pomegranate is edible) — true because each individual pomegranate is edible; and 'kullu al-rummani ma'kul' (definite: all the pomegranate is edible) — false because the pomegranate as a whole includes the inedible peel.
This demonstrates the rule's truth-conditional impact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وكل اللاحاطة على سبيل الافراد وهي تصحب الاسماء فتعمها . (۳) لفظ کل اور اسکے اخوات : عموم کا تیسرا صیغہ لفظ (کل ) ہے یہ حاملہ کیلئے استعمال ہوتا ہے علی سبیل الافراد یعنی ہر فردا ایسا ہوتا ہے گویا کہ اس کے ساتھ دوسرا فرد نہیں ہے اور لفظ کل اسماء پر داخل ہوتا ہے اور ان میں عموم پیدا کرتا ہے اور افعال پر اسلئے داخل نہیں ہوتا کیونکہ کل لازم الاضافہ ہے اور افعال مضاف الیہ نہیں بنتے ۔ قوله فان دخلت على المنكر او جبت عموم افراده وان دخلت على المعرف اوجبت عموم أجزاءه حتى فرقو ابين قولهم كل رمان ماكول وكل البرمان ماكول بالصدق والكذب - پھر اگر یہ لفظ منکہ پر داخل ہو تو یہ افراد کے عموم کو ثابت کرتا ہے اور اگر معرفہ پر داخل ہو تو اجز آ کے عموم کو ٹا بہ کرتا ہے۔ چنانچہ اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی سے انت طالق كل تطليقة ( نكره ) تو تین طلاقیں واقع ہو جائیں گے اور اگر كل التطليقة (معرفه ) کہا تو صرف ایک طلاق واقع ہوگی۔ اسی طرح كل رمان ماكول یہ بیچ ہے کیونکہ انار کا ہر فرد کھایا جاتا ہے اور ( كل الرمان ماكول ) جھوٹ ہے اس لئے کہ انار کے سارے اجز انہیں کھائے جاتے بلکہ اس کا چھلکا پھینکا جاتا ہے
He said: And 'kull' is for encompassing on the basis of individuals, and it accompanies nouns and generalizes them. (3) The word 'kull' and its sisters: The third form of generality is the word 'kull'. It is used for encompassing on the basis of individuals, i.e., every individual is such that there is no other individual with it. And the word 'kull' enters upon nouns and creates generality in them, and it does not enter upon verbs because 'kull' requires annexation and verbs cannot be annexed. He said: If it enters upon an indefinite noun, it necessitates generality of its individuals; if it enters upon a definite noun, it necessitates generality of its parts. This is why they distinguished between their saying 'kullu rummanin ma'kul' (every pomegranate is edible) and 'kullu al-rummani ma'kul' (all the pomegranate is edible) in terms of truth and falsehood. Then, if this word enters upon an indefinite, it establishes generality of individuals; if upon a definite, it establishes generality of parts. For example, if a man says to his wife, 'You are divorced by every divorce' (indefinite 'kullu talaqatin'), three divorces occur; if he says 'al-talaqata' (definite 'kullu al-talaqati'), only one divorce occurs. Similarly, 'kullu rummanin ma'kul' (every pomegranate is edible) is true because every individual pomegranate is eaten; but 'kullu al-rummani ma'kul' (all the pomegranate is edible) is false because not all parts of the pomegranate are eaten; its peel is thrown away.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage explains the word 'kull' (every/all) as a tool for generality in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author states that 'kull' is the third form of 'umum (generality), used for encompassing individuals (al-afrad) such that each individual is considered separately. It only enters upon nouns (asma'), not verbs, because 'kull' requires idafa (annexation) and verbs do not function as the second term of an idafa. A key distinction is made: when 'kull' is followed by an indefinite noun (nakirah), it generalizes over all individuals of that noun; when followed by a definite noun (ma'rifah), it generalizes over all parts of that noun. Two fiqh examples illustrate the practical difference. First, in divorce: if a husband says to his wife 'anti taliqun kulla talaqatin' (indefinite), the word 'kull' generalizes each individual divorce, resulting in three divorces (because the indefinite 'talaqatin' implies one, and 'kull' generalizes over all such individuals, i.e., three). If he says 'anti taliqun kulla al-talaqati' (definite), 'al-talaqati' (the divorce) is a single entity, and 'kull' generalizes over its parts, which is absurd since a single divorce has no parts; thus only one divorce occurs. Second, a logical example: 'kullu rummanin ma'kul' (every pomegranate is edible) is true because each individual pomegranate is edible; but 'kullu al-rummani ma'kul' (all the pomegranate is edible) is false because the pomegranate as a whole includes the peel, which is not eaten. This demonstrates the linguistic rule's application.

Key Terms
کل (kull): A word denoting encompassing (ihata) of individuals or parts, used for generality.
نکرہ (nakirah): Indefinite noun; when preceded by 'kull', it generalizes over all individuals of that noun.
معرفہ (ma'rifah): Definite noun; when preceded by 'kull', it generalizes over all parts of that noun.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule for 'kull' distinguishes between indefinite nouns (generalizing over individuals) and definite nouns (generalizing over parts), a crucial distinction for interpreting legal statements.

The Word Kull: Generality over Individuals vs Parts
Defines 'kull' and its rule for generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the effect of 'kull' when attached to a nakira vs a ma'rifa, and apply it to determine the ruling of a given phrase.

Core Idea
When 'kull' is attached to a definite noun, it refers to parts; when attached to an indefinite, it refers to individuals.

In Hanafi usul, the word 'kull' is one of the key tools for expressing generality, but its meaning shifts based on the definiteness of the noun it modifies. This passage clearly distinguishes between 'kull' with an indefinite noun (generalizing over each individual) and with a definite noun (generalizing over parts). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting contractual language, oaths, and legal statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the noun after 'kull'
Look at the noun that follows 'kull'. Determine if it is indefinite (nakira) or definite (ma'rifa). In the source, the examples are 'kullu tatlīqatin' (indefinite) vs 'kullu al-tatlīqati' (definite).
The definiteness can be indicated by al- (the definite article) or idafa.
2
Determine the type of generality
If the noun is indefinite, 'kull' gives generality over individuals (each separate instance). If the noun is definite, 'kull' gives generality over parts (the whole).
This rule is specific to Hanafi usul. For example, 'kullu rummānin' means every pomegranate (each fruit), while 'kullu al-rummāni' means the entire pomegranate (all its parts).
3
Apply to a legal scenario
Use the rule to derive the ruling. In the talaq example, 'anti tāliqun kulla tatlīqatin' (indefinite) results in three divorces because each individual divorce is intended. 'Kulla al-tatlīqati' (definite) results in one divorce because the whole divorce is meant as a single act.
The same logic applies to contracts, oaths, and other statements where generality matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وكل اللاحاطة على سبيل الافراد وهي تصحب الا سماء فتعمها . (۳) لفظ کل اور اسکے اخوات : عموم کا تیسرا صیغہ لفظ (کل ) ہے یہ حاملہ کیلئے استعمال ہوتا ہے علی سبیل الافراد یعنی ہر فردا ایسا ہوتا ہے گویا کہ اس کے ساتھ دوسرا فرد نہیں ہے اور لفظ کل اسماء پر داخل ہوتا ہے اور ان میں عموم پیدا کرتا ہے اور افعال پر اسلئے داخل نہیں ہوتا کیونکہ کل لازم الاضافہ ہے اور افعال مضاف الیہ نہیں بنتے ۔ قوله فان دخلت على المنكر او جبت عموم افراده وان دخلت على المعرف اوجبت عموم أجزاءه حتى فرقو ابين قولهم كل رمان ماكول وكل البرمان ماكول بالصدق والكذب - پھر اگر یہ لفظ منکہ پر داخل ہو تو یہ افراد کے عموم کو ثابت کرتا ہے اور اگر معرفہ پر داخل ہو تو اجز آ کے عموم کو ٹا بہ کرتا ہے۔ چنانچہ اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی سے انت طالق كل تطليقة ( نكره ) تو تین طلاقیں واقع ہو جائیں گے اور اگر كل التطليقة (معرفه ) کہا تو صرف ایک طلاق واقع ہوگی۔ اسی طرح کل رمان ماکول یہ بیچ ہے کیونکہ انار کا ہر فرد کھایا جاتا ہے اور ( کل الرمان ماکول ) جھوٹ ہے اس لئے کہ انار کے سارے اجز انہیں کھائے جاتے بلکہ اس کا چھلکا پھینکا جاتا ہے
He said: And the word 'kull' denotes encompassing in the manner of individuals, and it accompanies nouns and generalizes them. (3) The word 'kull' and its sisters: The third form of generality is the word 'kull'. It is used for individuals in the manner of each single one, i.e., every individual is such that there is no other individual with it. The word 'kull' enters upon nouns and creates generality in them, but does not enter upon verbs because 'kull' requires annexation and verbs cannot be annexed to. He said: If it enters upon an indefinite noun, it necessitates generality of its individuals; if it enters upon a definite noun, it necessitates generality of its parts. So they distinguished between their statement 'every pomegranate is edible' and 'the whole pomegranate is edible' in terms of truth and falsehood. Then if this word enters upon an indefinite, it establishes generality of individuals, and if upon a definite, it establishes generality of parts. For instance, if someone says to his wife 'You are divorced with every divorce' (indefinite), three divorces occur; and if he says 'with the whole divorce' (definite), only one divorce occurs. Similarly, 'every pomegranate is edible' is true because every individual pomegranate is eaten; and 'the whole pomegranate is edible' is false because not all parts are eaten; rather its peel is thrown away.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a definition and rule concerning the word 'kull' as one of the tools of 'umum (generalization) in Hanafi usul. The author states that 'kull' is the third form of generality and it is used for individuals one by one. Importantly, 'kull' only enters upon nouns, not verbs, because it requires an idafa (annexation) and verbs cannot be annexed. Then a crucial distinction is made: when 'kull' is attached to a nakira (indefinite) noun, it gives generality over individuals (e.g., 'kullu tatlīqatin' means each individual divorce, so three divorces occur if said after a triple talaq). When attached to a ma'rifa (definite) noun, it gives generality over parts (e.g., 'kullu al-tatlīqati' means the whole divorce, so only one divorce). The example of pomegranate illustrates: 'kullu rummānin' (every pomegranate) is true because each fruit is edible; 'kullu al-rummāni' (the whole pomegranate) is false because its peel is not eaten. This distinction is essential in Hanafi law for understanding contractual and oath language.

Key Terms
كُلّ (kull): A word indicating generality in the manner of individuals (istighrāq afrādī) when attached to a noun.
نَكِرَة (nakira): An indefinite noun; when 'kull' enters upon it, it gives generality over each individual.
مَعْرِفَة (ma'rifa): A definite noun; when 'kull' enters upon it, it gives generality over parts (ajzā').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The word 'kull' with an indefinite noun generalizes over individuals; with a definite noun, it generalizes over parts.

Kullama: Generality over Verbs and Implicit Generality over Nouns
Definition and contrast of 'kullama' with 'kull'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how 'kullama' gives generality to verbs and how nouns are implicitly included, and distinguish it from the operation of 'kull'.

Core Idea
Kullama generalizes actions; nouns are included implicitly because the action implies its object.

In this lesson, we examine the usul al-fiqh term 'kullama', a compound of 'kull' and 'ma'. Unlike 'kull' alone, which primarily generalizes nouns, 'kullama' enters upon verbs and generalizes the action. However, the nouns associated with that action are necessarily included by implication. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting legal statements to which 'kullama' is attached.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound
Recognize that 'kullama' is formed from 'kull' + 'ma'. This transformation changes the scope of generality.
'Kull' alone enters upon nouns; adding 'ma' allows it to enter upon verbs.
2
Determine primary generality
Understand that 'kullama' primarily gives generality over verbs, meaning it covers every occurrence of the action.
The example 'kullamā tazawwajtu...' shows that the act of marriage is generalized.
3
Recognize implicit inclusion
The generality of verbs implies generality of the objects involved, because the action logically requires an object.
In the example, the noun 'imra'ah' (woman) is implicitly included; each marriage necessarily involves a woman.
4
Contrast with 'kull'
Recall that 'kull' alone explicitly generalizes nouns, with verbs generalized only implicitly.
For instance, 'kullu imra'atin' explicitly generalizes every woman, and any verb attached is implicitly included.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : واذا وصلت ما اوجبت عموم الافعال ويثبت عموم الاسماء فيه ضمنا كعموم الافعال في كل . كلما : جب لفظ کل کو ما کیسا تھ ملایا جائے تو پھر وہ افعال پر داخل ہو سکتا ہے۔ اور وہ افعال کے عموم کو ثابت کرتا ہے اور اسماء کا مو ضمنا اس میں ثابت ہوتا ہے جیسے كلما تزوجت امرا بھی طالق ( جن اوقات میں میں شادی کروں ہمہ وقت وہ عورت مطلقہ ہوگی ) لہذا مقصد اصلی یہ ہو گا کہ ہر فعل تزویج میں طلاق واقع ہو لیکن اسم کو ضمنا شامل ہے کیونکہ تموم تزوج بغیر عموم زوجہ کے نہیں ہو سکتا اس لئے اس کے برعکس (کل) میں صراحتہ عموم اسماء ہوتا ہے اور عموم افعال ضمنا ہو کر آتا ہے۔
He said: And when 'ma' is attached, it necessitates generality of verbs, and generality of nouns is established implicitly, like generality of verbs in 'kull'. Kullama: When the word 'kull' is combined with 'ma', then it can enter upon verbs. And it establishes generality of verbs, and the generality of nouns is established implicitly. For example, 'kullamā tazawwajtu imra'atan fahiya tāliqun' (Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced). Therefore, the primary intent is that divorce occurs with every act of marriage, but the noun is implicitly included because the generality of 'tazawwajtu' cannot occur without generality of the wife. Conversely, in 'kull' itself, there is explicit generality of nouns and implicit generality of verbs.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage discusses the compound 'kullama' (كلما), which is derived from 'kull' attached to the particle 'ma'. Unlike 'kull' alone, which can only enter upon nouns, 'kullama' can enter upon verbs and gives generality of verbs (i.e., every occurrence of the action). However, the generality of nouns involved in the verb is included implicitly. The author gives the example: 'Kullamā tazawwajtu imra'atan fahiya tāliqun' – whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced. The ruling is that each time the husband marries any woman, that woman is divorced. The main ruling is on the verb (marrying), but the noun (woman) is implicitly generalized because the act of marrying necessarily involves a woman. The passage contrasts this with 'kull' alone, where the noun is explicitly generalized and verbs are only implicitly included.

Key Terms
كلما (kullamā): A compound word from 'kull' and 'ma' that gives generality over actions (verbs) and implicitly over nouns.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The compound 'kullama' primarily generalizes verbs; nouns are included by implication, contrasting with 'kull' which primarily generalizes nouns.

Jami': Generality through Togetherness
Defines the word 'jami‘' as a tool for collective generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'jami‘' in Hanafi usul, apply its literal and metaphorical usage, and defend the Hanafi position against the objection of combining haqiqa and majaz.

Core Idea
Jami‘ gives collective generality — the group as one, not each individually.

The word 'jami‘' is the fourth form of 'umum' (generality) in Hanafi usul. Unlike 'kull' which distributes to every individual, 'jami‘' covers the set as a whole — like a bundle. Understanding this distinction is crucial for deriving correct rulings from general statements. This lesson walks through the definition, the example of a commander's reward, and the subtle interplay between literal (haqiqa) and metaphorical (majaz) usage when entry is sequential.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'jami‘'
State that 'jami‘' gives umum al-ijtima‘ — generality through togetherness, not individual distribution.
Memorize: 'jami‘' = collective whole.
2
Apply the ruling to the together entry scenario
If a commander says 'Jami‘ man dakhala hadha al-hisn falahu alf dirham' and ten enter together, the thousand is divided among them because the collective whole applies.
3
Analyze the sequential entry scenario
If the ten enter one by one, only the first receives the reward. Here 'jami‘' is interpreted metaphorically as 'kull' (each) because the condition 'awwalan' (first) selects an individual.
This is a majaz usage, not a mix of haqiqa and majaz.
4
Resolve the objection
The objection claims the interpretation combines haqiqa and majaz. The Hanafi response: in sequential entry, the generality of the metaphor is acted upon — the first entrant is intended, whether one or a group.
No combination; only one meaning is operative in each scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وكلمة الجميع توجب عموم الاجتماع دون الانفراد حتى اذاقال جميع من خل في هذا الحصن أولاً فله من النقل كذا فدخل عشرة معا أن لهم نفلاً واحداً بينهم . (۴) جميع : عموم کا چوتھا صیغہ لفظ (جميع ) ہے یہ عموم کو ثابت کرتا ہے علی سبیل الاجتماع چنانچہ اگر امیر لشکر نے کہا ( جميع من دخل هذا الحصن فله الف درهم ) پھرا کھٹے دس آدمی داخل ہو گئے تو ہزار ان کے درمیان تقسیم کیا جائے گا اور اگر یہی دس آدمی اکیلے اکیلے داخل ہوئے تو سب سے پہلے داخل ہونے والے کو انعام دیا جائیگا ، پہلی صورت میں کلمہ جمع کی حقیقت پر عمل تھا اور دوسری صورت میں کلمہ ' جمیع کے مجاز پر عمل کیا گیا کیونکہ اس وقت یہ جمیع کل کے معنی میں مستعمل ہوگا ۔ اعتراض... اعتراض یہ ہوتا ہے کہ تم نے حقیقت اور مجاز کو جمع کر دیا ہے جو کہ جائز نہیں ہے۔ پہلا جواب ہم نے ایسا نہیں کیا بلکہ عموم مجاز پر عمل کیا ہے یعنی اول سے سابق فی الد خول مراد لیا ہے خواہ وہ ایک ہو یا جماعت ہو لہذ اعمل عموم مجاز پر ہوگا۔
He said: And the word 'jami'' necessitates generality of togetherness, not individually, such that if one says 'jami' man dakhala hadha al-hisn awwalan falahu min al-naql kadha' (all who enter this fort first have such-and-such reward), then if ten enter together, they have one reward among them. (4) Jami': The fourth form of generality is the word 'jami''. It establishes generality in the manner of togetherness. For example, if the army commander says 'Jami' man dakhala hadha al-hisn falahu alf dirham' (all who enter this fort have a thousand dirhams), then if ten men enter together, the thousand is divided among them. And if these ten enter one by one, then the first to enter is given the reward. In the first case, the literal meaning of 'jami'' is acted upon; in the second case, the metaphorical meaning of 'jami'' is acted upon because then 'jami'' is used in the sense of 'kull'. Objection: The objection is that you have combined literal and metaphorical meanings, which is not permissible. First answer: We have not done so; rather we have acted upon the generality of the metaphor, meaning the first to enter is intended, whether one or a group. So the action is upon the general metaphorical meaning.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth tool of 'umum: the word 'jami'' (all/whole). Unlike 'kull', which gives individual generality, 'jami'' gives collective generality — meaning the group as a whole, not each individual separately. The author provides a concrete example: a commander says 'Jami' man dakhala hadha al-hisn falahu alf dirham' (All who enter this fort have a thousand dirhams). If ten men enter together, the thousand is divided among them, because the word 'jami'' implies collective sharing. If they enter one by one, only the first gets the prize, because then 'jami'' is understood metaphorically to mean 'kull' (each individual) and the condition of being first applies. An objection is raised: this interpretation seems to combine literal (togetherness) and metaphorical (individual) meanings, which is not allowed in usul. The answer is that they are not combining; rather, in the case of sequential entry, they act upon the general metaphorical meaning of 'jami'' as 'kull' (each) and take the first entrant due to the word 'awwal' (first) in the statement. The ruling is based on the metaphorical generality, not a mix.

Key Terms
جميع (jami‘): A word indicating generality in the manner of togetherness (ijtimā‘), not individually.
حقيقة (ḥaqīqa): Literal meaning of a word when used as originally intended.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical meaning when a word is used for something other than its original meaning due to a connection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Jami‘ is a tool of umum that indicates collective generality; its metaphorical use as 'kull' applies only when context necessitates individual selection.

The Sighat 'Kullama': Generality of Actions
Explaining the sighat 'kullama' for generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify 'kullama' as a sighat of umum for actions and apply it to derive rulings from Arabic legal texts.

Core Idea
'Kullama' explicitly generalizes actions; nouns are only implicitly included.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding the precise function of different words of generality is crucial. The compound 'kullama' is a specific sighat that primarily operates on verbs, making the action general in every instance. This lesson unpacks its function, distinguishing it from other tools of umum like 'kull'.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the sighat
Identify the compound 'kullama' (كلما) and recall that it is formed by attaching 'ma' to 'kull'.
This construction is a distinct sighat for actions.
2
Determine explicit generality
Understand that 'kullama' explicitly generalizes the action (fi'l) it precedes. The ruling applies to every occurrence of that action.
The noun is included only as an implicit consequence.
3
Illustrate with the example
Analyze the example: 'kullama tazawajtu imra'atan fahiya taliq'. Here, 'tazawwaj' (marrying) is generalized, so divorce occurs with every single marriage.
The wife (imra'ah) is not explicitly generalized; she is included because marriage requires a wife.
4
Contrast with 'kull'
Compare 'kullama' with 'kull' alone: 'kull' explicitly generalizes nouns and implicitly actions, the reverse of 'kullama'.
This distinction is key in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : واذا وصلت ما اوجبت عموم الافعال ويثبت عموم الاسماء فيه ضمنا كعموم الافعال في كل .
كلما : جب لفظ کل کو ما کیسا تھ ملایا جائے تو پھر وہ افعال پر داخل ہو سکتا ہے۔ اور وہ افعال کے عموم کو ثابت کرتا ہے اور اسماء کا مو ضمنا اس میں ثابت ہوتا ہے جیسے کلما تزوجت امرا بھی طالق ( جن اوقات میں میں شادی کروں ہمہ وقت وہ عورت مطلقہ ہوگی ) لہذا مقصد اصلی یہ ہو گا کہ ہر فعل تزویج میں طلاق واقع ہو لیکن اسم کو ضمنا شامل ہے کیونکہ تموم تزوج بغیر عموم زوجہ کے نہیں ہو سکتا اس لئے اس کے برعکس (کل) میں صراحتہ عموم اسماء ہوتا ہے اور عموم افعال ضمنا ہو کر آتا ہے۔
He said: And when 'ma' is attached, it necessitates generality of actions, and generality of nouns is established therein implicitly, like the generality of actions in 'kull'. 'Kullama': When the word 'kull' is joined with 'ma', then it can enter upon actions. And it establishes generality of actions, and generality of nouns is implicitly established in it, like: 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced.' So the main purpose is that divorce occurs in every act of marriage, but the noun is included implicitly because the generality of 'tazawwaj' cannot be without the generality of wife. Therefore, conversely, in 'kull' there is explicit generality of nouns and generality of actions comes implicitly.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage explains the linguistic form 'kullama' (كلما) as a sighat of 'umum (generality). The author clarifies that when the particle 'ma' is attached to 'kull', the compound word operates primarily on verbs (af'al), making them general. For example, 'kullama tazawwajtu imra'atan fahiya taliq' means that every act of marriage results in divorce immediately. The noun (the wife) is included implicitly because the action of marriage cannot occur without a wife. This is contrasted with the word 'kull' alone, which explicitly gives generality to nouns and implicitly to actions. The discussion is part of the Hanafi usul al-fiqh analysis of words of generality, showing how different constructions affect the scope of the ruling.

Key Terms
كلما (kullama): A compound word that makes the following verb general in time or occurrence.
عموم الأفعال (umum al-af'al): Generality of actions: the ruling applies to every instance of the action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Generality of actions

Key Takeaway
'Kullama' is a sighat that explicitly generalizes actions, with nouns included implicitly, distinct from 'kull' which does the reverse.

The Generality of 'Jami'': Collective vs Distributive
Explaining the sighat 'jami'' for collective generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between collective and distributive generality in the Hanafi usul, apply the literal meaning of 'jami'' to a collective action context, and explain the metaphorical shift when action is sequential.

Core Idea
'Jami'' means collective — a group enters together, the reward is shared; sequential entry shifts it to distributive via metaphor.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the fourth form of 'umum (generality) is the word 'jami'' (جميع). Unlike other sighat that imply distributive generality (each individual), 'jami'' intrinsically denotes collective inclusion. This lesson unpacks this unique feature through a concrete example from a commander's reward announcement, and addresses a subtle objection about mixing literal and metaphorical usage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sighat and its literal meaning
Recognize that 'jami'' is a sighat of umum, and its literal meaning indicates generality on the basis of collective gathering (ijtima'), not individual distribution.
This is the core distinction from words like 'kull' or 'jami'' in ordinary language.
2
Analyze the example: simultaneous entry
In the example 'Jami' man dakhala hadha al-hisn falahu alfu dirham' (All who enter this fort have a thousand dirhams), if ten men enter simultaneously, the reward is divided among them. This is the literal application of 'jami''.
Collective generality applies when the action is performed together.
3
Analyze the example: sequential entry
If the ten men enter one by one, the first entrant receives the whole reward. Here 'jami'' is interpreted metaphorically as 'kull' (each) to refer to the first entrant only.
The metaphorical reading is necessary because sequential entry contradicts the collective literal sense.
4
Address the objection of combining literal and metaphorical
An objection claims that the Hanafi position combines literal and metaphorical usage in one statement. The response clarifies that in the sequential scenario, only the metaphorical meaning is used; there is no combination.
The objection is resolved by showing that the word's meaning shifts based on context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وكلمة الجميع توجب عموم الاجتماع دون الانفراد حتى اذاقال جميع من خل في هذا الحصن أولاً فله من النقل كذا فدخل عشرة معا أن لهم نفلاً واحداً بينهم .
(۴) جميع :
عموم کا چوتھا صیغہ لفظ (جميع ) ہے یہ عموم کو ثابت کرتا ہے علی سبیل الاجتماع چنانچہ اگر امیر لشکر نے کہا ( جميع من دخل هذا الحصن فله الف درهم ) پھرا کھٹے دس آدمی داخل ہو گئے تو ہزار ان کے درمیان تقسیم کیا جائے گا اور اگر یہی دس آدمی اکیلے اکیلے داخل ہوئے تو سب سے پہلے داخل ہونے والے کو انعام دیا جائیگا ، پہلی صورت میں کلمہ جمع کی حقیقت پر عمل تھا اور دوسری صورت میں کلمہ ' جمیع کے مجاز پر عمل کیا گیا کیونکہ اس وقت یہ جمیع کل کے معنی میں مستعمل ہوگا ۔
اعتراض... اعتراض یہ ہوتا ہے کہ تم نے حقیقت اور مجاز کو جمع کر دیا ہے جو کہ جائز نہیں ہے۔ پہلا جواب ہم نے ایسا نہیں کیا بلکہ عموم مجاز پر عمل کیا ہے یعنی اول سے سابق فی الد خول مراد لیا ہے خواہ وہ ایک ہو یا جماعت ہو لہذ اعمل عموم مجاز پر ہوگا۔
He said: And the word 'jami'' necessitates generality of gathering/collective, not individual, so if he says 'All who enter this fort first - for him such and such reward', then ten people entered together - that they have one reward among them. (4) 'Jami'': The fourth form of generality is the word 'jami''. It establishes generality on the basis of collectivity. For example, if an army commander says 'All who enter this fort - for him a thousand dirhams', then ten men entered together, the thousand is divided among them. And if the same ten men entered individually, then the reward is given to the first entrant. In the first case, the literal meaning of 'jami'' was acted upon; in the second case, the metaphorical meaning of 'jami'' was acted upon because at that time 'jami'' is used in the meaning of 'kull' (each). Objection: The objection is that you have combined literal and metaphorical usage, which is not permissible. First answer: We did not do that; rather we acted upon the metaphorical generality, meaning the first entrant is intended, whether he is one or a group, so the action is upon metaphorical generality.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage discusses the sighat 'jami'' (جميع) as the fourth form of 'umum. The key point is that 'jami'' implies generality as a collective group ('ala sabi al-ijtima'), not individually. The example given is a military commander's statement: 'Jami' man dakhala hadha al-hisn falahu alfu dirham' (All who enter this fort have a thousand dirhams). If ten men enter simultaneously, the reward is shared. If they enter one by one, the first entrant gets the full reward, as 'jami'' is then interpreted metaphorically as 'kull' (each). An objection arises about combining literal and metaphorical usage in one statement. The answer clarifies that they are not combining; rather, in the sequential entry scenario, the word 'jami'' is used metaphorically to mean the first entrant, so the ruling is based on metaphorical generality alone. This shows Hanafi precision in analyzing linguistic forms for legal rulings.

Key Terms
جميع (jami'): A word of generality that indicates collective inclusion (jam') rather than individual.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The thousand is divided among them

D) No one gets the reward

Key Takeaway
'Jami'' indicates collective generality; the ruling differs if entry is simultaneous (shared) vs sequential (first only).

Nakirah in Negation and Affirmation
Rule establishing generality of nakirah in negation with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule regarding nakirah in negation and affirmation, apply it to the proof from 'La ilaha illallah', and identify the key madhhab difference with al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Indefinite nouns under negation are general; under affirmation, specific—proven by the consensus on 'La ilaha illallah'.

This passage from Khulāṣat al-Anwār establishes a foundational usul al-fiqh rule: an indefinite noun (nakirah) that appears in a negative context becomes general (amm), whereas in an affirmative context it remains specific (khass) but unrestricted (mutlaq). The author supports this with the linguistic consensus on the kalimah 'La ilaha illallah' and contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i. Understanding this rule is essential for deriving rulings from legislative texts correctly.

Guided Path
1
State the rule and its two contexts
Identify the two contexts: negation (nafi) and affirmation (ithbat). The rule: nakirah in negation is general; in affirmation it is specific.
The passage also differentiates between nakirah that includes 'min istighraqiyyah' (decisive in generality) and those that do not (apparent in generality).
2
Understand the linguistic reasoning
Analyze why negation transforms the indefinite noun. The original placement of nakirah is for essence (mahiyyah) or an unspecified individual. Negation must negate all possible instances, otherwise the essence or individual would not be fully negated.
This is the core Hanafi argument: negation requires comprehensive denial.
3
Examine the proof from linguistic consensus
The proof is the unanimous agreement of Arabic linguists that 'La ilaha illallah' is the word of tawhid (monotheism). If 'ilaha' were not general under negation, oneness would not be established.
The kalimah is used as a decisive evidence (dalil qaṭ‘ī) for the rule.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that nakirah in affirmation can also be general, as he argued for the generality of 'raqabah' (slave) in the verse of zihar. Understand the basis of his view.
The hanafi_response: the nakirah in affirmation remains specific but unrestricted (mutlaq); any generality comes from external evidence, not the word itself.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
For example, in zihar expiation, Hanafis say freeing any one slave fulfills the obligation; Shafi'is may require a specific type (e.g., believing slave) due to their reading of 'raqabah' as general.
The practical difference arises from the underlying usul rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : النكرة في موضع التقى تعم و في الاثبات تخص لكنها مطلقة وعند الشافعي رحمه الله تعم حتى قال بعموم الرقبة المذكورة في الظهار.
قاعده :
نکرہ جب تحت الھی واقع ہو تو عام ہوتا ہے اس لئے کہ نکرہ اصل وضع کے اعتبار سے یا تو ماہیت ہوتا ہے یا فرد واحد غیر متعین کیلئے ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا جب اس پر نھی داخل ہوگی تو وہ عام ہو جائیگا کیونکہ اگر نفی عام نہ ہو تو پھر ما بیت یا فرد غیر معین کی نفی نہیں ہو سکے گی پھر اگر نکرہ من استغراقیہ کے معنی کو شامل ہو تو وہ عموم میں نص ہوگا اور اگر من استغراقیہ کے معنی کو شامل نہ ہو تو عموم میں ظاہر ہو گا اگر چہ خصوص کا بھی احتمال رکھتا ہے۔
اور اس عموم کی دلیل اجتماع اہل لغت ہے کہ (لا اله الا الله ) بالا جماع کلمہ توحید ہے پس اگر یہ کلمہ لا عموم یعنی تمام معبود برحق کی نفی کیلئے نہ ہوتا تو اس کلمہ سے وحدت باری تعالیٰ کسی طرح ثابت ہوتی ؟
He said: The indefinite noun in the context of negation becomes general, and in affirmation it is specific, but it is unrestricted. And according to al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him), it is general, so he said regarding the generality of the slave mentioned in zihar. Rule: The indefinite noun when it occurs under negation becomes general, because the indefinite noun in its original placement is either for essence or for a single unspecified individual. Therefore, when negation enters upon it, it becomes general, because if the negation were not general, then it would not be possible to negate the essence or the unspecified individual. Then, if the indefinite noun includes the meaning of istighraq, it is decisive (nass) in generality, and if it does not include the meaning of istighraq, it is apparent (zahir) in generality, although it also has the possibility of specificity. And the proof of this generality is the consensus of the people of language that 'La ilaha illallah' is by consensus the word of tawhid. So if this word were not for generality, i.e., the negation of all true deities, how would the oneness of Allah be established from this word?
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental rule in usul al-fiqh: an indefinite noun (nakirah) in the context of negation (nafi) becomes general, whereas in the context of affirmation (ithbat) it remains specific. The author explains the reasoning: the indefinite noun is originally placed to denote either the essence (mahiyyah) or an unspecified individual. When negation applies, it must negate all instances, otherwise the essence or individual would not be fully negated. If the nakirah includes the sense of istighraq (encompassing all), it is considered nass (decisive) in generality; otherwise, it is zahir (apparent) and may allow specificity. A strong proof is the kalimah 'La ilaha illallah', which by consensus of linguists is the word of tawhid. If 'ilaha' were not general under negation, it would not negate all false gods to establish the oneness of Allah. The passage also notes that Imam al-Shafi'i differed, holding that the indefinite noun in affirmation is also general, as he argued for the generality of the slave mentioned in the verse of zihar. This shows a key madhhab difference: Hanafis restrict generality to negation context, while Shafi'is extend it.

Key Terms
النكرة في النفي (al-nakirah fi al-nafi): An indefinite noun occurring in a negative context, which becomes general in the Hanafi view.
من استغراقیہ (min istighraqiyyah): A particle 'min' that implies inclusiveness, making the indefinite noun decisive (nass) in generality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It remains specific
B) It becomes general
C) It becomes ambiguous
D) It depends on the context

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, an indefinite noun under negation is general (amm); in affirmation it remains specific (khass) but unrestricted (mutlaq).
