Understanding 'Kull' and 'Man' in Conditional Rewards
Textual analysis of 'kull' and 'man' in conditional rewards.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi usuli treatment of 'kull' and 'man' in conditional statements and apply the appropriate ruling based on the form of the condition.

Core Idea
'Kull' distributes rewards individually; 'man' requires a unique first — multiple entrants invalidate the reward.

This passage explores how the choice of words in a conditional reward statement affects the legal outcome. Understanding 'kull' and 'man' is crucial for deriving rulings from general and conditional texts in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key word
Look at the conditional phrase: does it use 'kull' (كل) or 'man' (من)? This determines the initial ruling.
'Kull' indicates encompassment; 'man' indicates a specific individual.
2
Analyze the scenario for 'kull'
If 'kull' is used, check if multiple people perform the act sequentially or simultaneously. Hanafi: 'kull' gives each an independent reward, but if only one enters first, that one gets the reward because 'kull' can be restricted by context.
The word 'kull' is general but not absolutely qat'i; it admits takhsis.
3
Analyze the scenario for 'man'
If 'man' is used, the reward is conditional on being the very first individual. If multiple people enter, there is no unique first, so the reward is invalidated.
'Man' is not strong in generality; it defaults to a single individual.
4
Derive the ruling
Based on the analysis, state who gets the reward. For 'kull': each gets reward if multiple, only first if one enters first. For 'man': reward invalid if multiple; only first if single entrant.
The ruling hinges on the word's scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : في كلمة كل يجب لكل منهم النقل
اور لفظ کل کی صورت میں دس میں سے ہر ایک کو مستقل انعام دیا جائے گا اس لئے کہ لفظ کل احاطہ کیلئے علی سبیل الافراد آتا ہے، اور اگر ایک شخص پہلے داخل ہو گیا تو صرف اس کو انعام دیا جائیگا۔ کیونکہ لفظ کل خصوص کا بھی احتمال رکھتا ہے۔
قوله : وفي كلمة من يبطل المنقل
اور تیسری صورت من دخل .... الخ کی صورت میں انعام باطل ہو جائے گا اس لئے کہ اول نام ہے فرد سابق کا جو سب سے پہلے پہلے داخل ہو اور اس سے پہلے کوئی نہ گیا ہو جبکہ یہاں کوئی ایسا فرد نہیں ہے بلکہ کئی داخل ہونے والے ہیں اور کلمہ من عموم میں اتنا محکم نہیں کہ اول کے معنی کو بدل سکے۔
His saying: Regarding the word 'kull', it is obligatory to give a reward to each one of them. And in the case of the word 'kull', from ten, each one is given an independent reward because the word 'kull' comes for encompassment on an individual basis. And if one person enters first, then only he is given a reward because the word 'kull' also carries the possibility of specificity. His saying: And regarding the word 'man', the reward is invalidated. And in the third scenario, 'man enters...' etc., the reward will be invalidated because 'the first' is the name of the previous individual who entered first and no one before him, whereas here there is no such individual; rather there are multiple entrants, and the word 'man' is not so strong in generality that it can alter the meaning of 'first'.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of how the Arabic words 'kull' and 'man' function in conditional rewards. The author explains that when the word 'kull' is used, it gives each individual an independent share, as it indicates encompassment of all individuals separately. However, if only one person fulfills the condition first, only he gets the reward because 'kull' can also imply specificity in some contexts. For the word 'man', if multiple people enter, the reward is invalidated because 'man' seeks a unique first individual, and when multiple enter, no such unique individual exists. 'Man' is not strong enough in generality to override the concept of 'first'.

Key Terms
كل (kull): A word indicating encompassment of all individuals, but can also imply specificity.
من (man): A conditional word indicating the first person to perform an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The ruling on conditional rewards hinges on whether the condition uses 'kull' (individual inclusivity) or 'man' (unique first); multiple entrants invalidate 'man' but not 'kull'.

Indefinite Nouns in Negation and Affirmation: Hanafi Rule and Shafi'i Dispute
Defines the rule and discusses the Shafi'i disagreement with Hanafi response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an indefinite noun in negation is general while in affirmation it is specific but absolute, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the two Hanafi usul rules.

Core Idea
An indefinite noun in negation is general; in affirmation it is specific but absolute — a key Hanafi distinction.

This lesson examines a foundational usul rule that governs how indefinite nouns function in legal texts. The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between negative and affirmative contexts, a position that leads to practical differences in rulings such as the validity of freeing a disbelieving slave in zihar. You will see how this rule is supported by linguistic consensus and Quranic evidence, and how it contrasts with the Shafi'i approach.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Identify the core distinction: an indefinite noun (nakira) in negation becomes general (ta'mm), while in affirmation it remains specific to one individual but absolute in attributes.
The rule is summarized in the lesson title.
2
Examine the first proof
Analyze the consensus of linguists on 'La ilaha illa Allah'. The indefinite 'ilah' in the negative context negates all false deities, proving generality.
This proof is qat'i in thubut and dalala.
3
Examine the second proof
Analyze the Quranic verse (Al-An'am 91) where a particular affirmation is the opposite of universal negation, supporting the rule.
The verb 'anzala' in the negative context is universal.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the indefinite in affirmative is also general, as in 'fatahriru raqaba' (free a slave). He then excludes certain slaves by evidence.
This leads to a practical difference in the validity of freeing a disbelieving slave.
5
Apply the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response relies on two usul rules: (1) the absolute remains on its absoluteness, and (2) when absolute, the perfect individual is intended. Thus, deficient slaves (sick, mudabbar) are not included, but a disbelieving slave is a perfect slave and valid.
The response preserves the generality of the absolute for perfect cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : النكرة في موضع النفي تعم و في الاثبات تخص لكنها مطلقة وعند الشافعي رحمه الله تعم حتى قال بعموم الرقبة المذكورة في الظهار.
قاعده :
نکرہ جب تحت نفی واقع ہو تو عام ہوتا ہے اس لئے کہ نکرہ اصل وضع کے اعتبار سے یا ماہیت ہوتا ہے یا فرد واحد غیر متعین کیلئے ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا جب اس پر نہی داخل ہوگی تو وہ عام ہو جائیگا کیونکہ اگر نفی عام نہ ہو تو پھر ماہیت یا فرد غیر معین کی نفی نہیں ہو سکے گی پھر اگر نکرہ من استغراقیہ کے معنی کو شامل ہو تو وہ عموم میں نص ہوگا اور اگر من استغراقیہ کے معنی کو شامل نہ ہو تو عموم میں ظاہر ہو گا اگر چہ خصوص کا بھی احتمال رکھتا ہے۔

دوسری دلیل اللہ تعالیٰ کا یہ فرمان ہے ۔ اذ قالوا ما انزل الله على بشر من شئى . من انزل الكتاب الذي جاء به موسى الخ، کیونکہ من انزل الكتاب میں ایجاب جزئی ہے اور ایجاب جزئی سلب کلی کی نقیض بنتی ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ ما انزل اللہ میں سلب کلی ہے۔ اور نکرہ موضع اثبات میں خاص ہوتا ہے یعنی فرد واحد غیر معین کیلئے ہوتا ہے لیکن اوصاف کے اعتبار سے مطلق ہوتا ہے جیسے اعتق رقبة میں رقبہ نکرہ موضع اثبات میں ہے لہذا یہ جملہ کسی ایک غلام کے اعتاق پر دلالت کر دیگا مگر اس غلام کے اوصاف متعدد ہو سکتے ہیں ۔ مذهب واضع دیگر امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ اس صورت میں بھی وہ عام ہی ہوتا ہے چنانچہ انہوں نے کہا فتحرير رقبة ، یہ عام ہے ہر قسم کے غلام کو شامل ہے پھر اس میں سے بالا جماع معذور اور مدبر کو خاص کر دیا گیا تو اس میں سے کا فر غلام کو بھی انہوں نے خاص کر لیا ہے۔ شوافع کو جواب ہم جواب دیتے ہیں کہ معذور رقبہ مطلقہ میں سرے سے داخل ہی نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ اس میں جنس منفعت فوت ہوتی ہے اسطرح مدبر بھی شامل نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ وہ من وجہ غلام ہے اور من وجہ غلام نہیں ہے تو یہاں ہم دو قاعدوں پر عمل کرنے ہیں ۔
احناف کے دو قاعدے:
(1) پہلا قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ مطلق اپنے اطلاق پر باقی رہتا ہے المطلق یجری علی اطلاقه لہذا آیت مذکورہ میں لفظ رقبہ رقبہ مؤمن اور کافر دونوں کو شامل ہے۔ (۲) دوسرا قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ جب کسی کو مطلق ذکر کیا جائے تو اس کا ذکر کامل مراد ہوتا ہے المطلق اذا اطلق یراد به الفرد الكامل تو چونکہ مدبر اور معذور کامل غلام نہیں بلکہ ناقص ہیں لہذا اس آیت کے حکم میں سرے سے شامل ہی نہیں ہیں۔
His saying: The indefinite noun in the position of negation is general, and in affirmation it is specific, but it is absolute. And according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, it is general, so much so that he said regarding the generality of the slave mentioned in zihar. Rule: When an indefinite noun occurs under negation, it becomes general, because the indefinite in its original placement is either for essence or for a single unspecified individual. So when negation enters upon it, it becomes general, because if the negation were not general, then the essence or the unspecified individual could not be negated. Then if the indefinite includes the meaning of 'generalizing min', then it is explicit (nass) for generality; if it does not include that meaning, then it is apparent (zahir) for generality, although it also carries the possibility of specificity. The proof for this generality is the consensus of linguists: 'La ilaha illa Allah' is by consensus the word of tawhid. So if this word were not general in negation, i.e., negating all true deities, then how could the oneness of Allah be proven? Second proof: Allah's saying, 'When they said, Allah has not sent down anything to a human being. Say: Who sent down the Book that Musa brought...' because in 'Who sent down the Book' there is a particular affirmation, and a particular affirmation is the opposite of a universal negation, so it becomes clear that in 'Allah has not sent down' there is universal negation. And the indefinite in the position of affirmation is specific, meaning it is for a single unspecified individual, but in terms of attributes it is absolute, like in 'free a slave' (i'tiq raqaba) - 'raqaba' is an indefinite in the position of affirmation, so this sentence indicates the freeing of any one slave, but the attributes of that slave can be multiple. The scholar of the opposite school, Imam al-Shafi'i, says that even in this case it is general, so he said: 'then the freeing of a slave' (fatahriru raqaba) is general, including every type of slave. Then he excluded by consensus the sick and the mudabbar, and he also excluded the disbelieving slave. Our response to the Shafi'is: The sick slave is not included at all in the absolute 'raqaba' because the species of benefit is lost in him; similarly, the mudabbar is not included because he is a slave from one aspect and not a slave from another. So here we act on two rules. The two rules of the Hanafis: (1) The first rule: The absolute remains on its absoluteness; so in the mentioned verse, the word 'raqaba' includes both believing and disbelieving slaves. (2) The second rule: When something is mentioned in absolute terms, the perfect individual is intended. So since the mudabbar and the sick are not perfect slaves but deficient, they are not included in the ruling of this verse at all.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental Hanafi usul rule: an indefinite noun (nakira) when placed in a negative context becomes general (ta'mm), and when in an affirmative context it is specific (takhassus) but remains absolute (mutlaq) regarding attributes. The author provides two proofs: first, the consensus of linguists on 'La ilaha illa Allah' which negates all false deities, proving its generality; second, the Quranic verse (Al-An'am 91) where the negation is universal. The Hanafi position is contrasted with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that the indefinite in affirmative is also general. The Shafi'i argument uses the verse of zihar (Surah Mujadila) where 'freeing a slave' is interpreted generally by them, then they exclude certain categories by consensus or analogy. The Hanafi response defends the absoluteness of 'raqaba' by citing two rules: (1) the absolute remains on its absoluteness, so it covers both believing and disbelieving slaves, and (2) when absolute, the perfect individual is intended, so deficient slaves like the sick or mudabbar are not included. This preserves the generality of the absolute for perfect cases.

Key Terms
النكرة (al-nakira): An indefinite noun; in negation it becomes general, in affirmation it is specific but absolute in attributes.
المطلق (al-mutlaq): An absolute term that remains on its absoluteness without restriction, and when absolute, the perfect individual is intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi usul, what is the ruling on an indefinite noun in an affirmative context?
A) It is general and includes all possibilities.
B) It is specific to one unspecified individual but absolute in attributes.
C) It is completely unspecified and ambiguous.
D) It is the same as in negation.

Key Takeaway
An indefinite noun in negation is general; in affirmation it is specific but absolute — a foundational Hanafi usul rule with practical impact on rulings like expiation.

When an Indefinite is Described with a General Attribute
States the rule and provides three examples from oaths and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an indefinite noun described by a general attribute (sifa 'amma) becomes general in scope, and classify examples accordingly.

Core Idea
An indefinite described by a general attribute encompasses all individuals fitting that attribute.

In Hanafi usul, the scope of indefinite nouns (nakira) can be expanded by a qualifying attribute if that attribute is general. This lesson illustrates the principle with examples from oaths and conditions, showing how a general attribute renders the entire expression general.

Guided Path
1
Identify the indefinite noun
Locate the word in the phrase that is indefinite (nakira), often marked by tanwin or the context. In 'rajulan kufiyyan', 'rajulan' is indefinite.
Indefinite nouns are general in potential but often restricted by context.
2
Identify the describing attribute
Find the adjective or qualifier that describes the indefinite noun. In the example, it is 'Kufiyyan' (a Kufi man).
Ensure it is an attribute (sifa), not a separate clause.
3
Determine if the attribute is general
Check if the attribute applies uniformly to a class of individuals without further restriction. 'Kufi' applies to all men from Kufa, making it general.
Specific attributes (e.g., 'the one wearing a red cloak') do not trigger the rule.
4
Apply the rule: the expression becomes general
The indefinite noun, qualified by a general attribute, now covers all individuals who fit that description. The exception or condition negates all such individuals.
The result is that the indefinite is treated as 'amm (general) in that context.
5
Confirm with the given examples
Work through the three examples in the passage: (1) oath regarding Kufi men, (2) oath regarding a pleasing day, (3) condition regarding slaves hitting.
Each example demonstrates how the rule operates in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وان وصفت بصفة عامة تعم كقوله والله لا اكلم احداً الا رجلاً كوفيا وقوله والله لا اقربكما الا يوماً اقر بكما فيه وكذا اذا قال أى عبيدي ضربك فهو حر فضربوه انهم يعتقون
His saying: And if it is described with a general attribute, it becomes general, like his saying: 'By Allah, I will not speak to anyone except a Kufi man' and his saying: 'By Allah, I will not come near you two except on a day that pleases you both' and likewise if he says: 'Whichever of my slaves hits you, he is free', then if they hit him, they are all freed.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage introduces another rule: when an indefinite noun is qualified by a general attribute (sifa 'amma), the entire expression becomes general. The author provides three examples. First: 'I will not speak to anyone except a Kufi man' – the attribute 'Kufi' is general in the sense that it applies to all men from Kufa, so the exception negates all Kufi men. Second: 'I will not come near you two except on a day that pleases you' – the attribute 'pleases you' is general because it can apply to any day that satisfies both, so it negates all such days. Third: 'Whichever of my slaves hits you, he is free' – if all the slaves hit him, they are all freed because the relative pronoun 'whichever' (ayyu) is general and the attribute 'hits you' is a general description.

Key Terms
صفة عامة (sifa 'amma): A general attribute that describes an indefinite noun, making the indefinite general.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not speak to any man except a Kufi', what does the oath entail?
A) He will not speak to any man at all.
B) He will not speak to any non-Kufi man, but can speak to Kufi men.
C) He can speak to anyone except Kufi men.
D) The oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
An indefinite noun qualified by a general attribute becomes general in scope, covering all instances of that attribute.

The Hanafi Principles of Mutlaq: Raqabah and the Shafi'i Dispute
Second proof from Quran; Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on mutlaq vs 'amm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two Hanafi qawa'id on mutlaq (al-mutlaq yajri 'ala itlaqihi and al-mutlaq yurad bihi al-fard al-kamil) to distinguish mutlaq from 'amm in Quranic texts.

Core Idea
A mutlaq term includes perfect individuals by default; deficient ones are never included.

This passage presents a second Quranic proof and a key dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is over the interpretation of 'raqabah' (slave) in emancipation verses. The Hanafis rely on two foundational qawa'id: a mutlaq term operates in its absolute scope, and when mentioned absolutely, it refers to the perfect individual. Understanding these principles is crucial for distinguishing mutlaq from 'amm in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Logical Argument
The passage uses the Quranic verse 'Who sent down the Book?' as a particular affirmative that contradicts the universal negative 'Allah has not sent down anything.' Understand this logical relationship.
This shows how a single positive statement can negate a universal claim.
2
Classify 'Nakirah fi Mawdi' al-Ithbat'
A nakirah (indefinite noun) in a positive sentence like 'raqabah' indicates a specific individual but is absolute (mutlaq) in attributes. Distinguish this from 'amm (general).
Hanafis: khas for individual, mutlaq for attributes.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i Position
Imam al-Shafi'i considers 'raqabah' as 'amm (general) covering all slaves, then excludes mu'dhar, mudabbir by consensus, and also kafir. Understand their sequence: general then specification.
4
Apply the Two Hanafi Qawa'id
First qa'idah: al-mutlaq yajri 'ala itlaqihi – the absolute includes all individuals (including kafir). Second: al-mutlaq yurad bihi al-fard al-kamil – the absolute intends the perfect individual, so deficient slaves (mu'dhar, mudabbir) are not included from the start.
These two qawa'id together justify the Hanafi ruling.
5
Extract the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Practical consequence: Hanafis allow expiation via a kafir slave; Shafi'is require a believing slave. This difference stems directly from the usul disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دوسری دلیل اللہ تعالیٰ کا یہ فرمان ہے ۔ اذ قالوا ما انزل الله على بشر من شئى . من انزل الكتاب الذي جاء به موسى الخ، کیونکہ من انزل الكتاب میں ایجاب جزئی ہے اور ایجاب جزئی سلب کلی کی نقیض بنتی ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ ما انزل اللہ میں سلب کلی ہے۔ اور نکرہ موضع اثبات میں خاص ہوتا ہے یعنی فرد واحد غیر معین کیلئے ہوتا ہے لیکن اوصاف کے اعتبار سے مطلق ہوتا ہے جیسے اعتق رقبة میں رقبہ نکرہ موضع اثبات میں ہے لہذا یہ جملہ کسی ایک غلام کے اعتماق پر دلالت کر دیگا مگر اس غلام کے اوصاف متعدد ہو سکتے ہیں ۔ مذهب واضع دیگر امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ اس صورت میں بھی وہ عام ہی ہوتا ہے چنانچہ انہوں نے کہا فتحرير رقبة ، یہ عام ہے ہر قسم کے غلام کو شامل ہے پھر اس میں سے بالا جماع معذور اور مدبر کو خاص کر دیا گیا تو اس میں سے کا فر غلام کو بھی انہوں نے خاص کر لیا ہے۔ شوافع کو جواب ہم جواب دیتے ہیں کہ معذور رقبہ مطلقہ میں سرے سے داخل ہی نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ اس میں جنس منفعت فوت ہوتی ہے اسطرح مدبر بھی شامل نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ وہ من وجہ غلام ہے اور من وجہ غلام نہیں ہے تو یہاں ہم دو قاعدوں پر عمل کرنے ہیں ۔ احناف کے دو قاعدے: (1) پہلا قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ مطلق اپنے اطلاق پر باقی رہتا ہے المطلق یجری علی اطلاقه لہذا آیت مذکورہ میں لفظ رقبہ رقبہ مؤمن اور کافر دونوں کو شامل ہے۔ (۲) دوسرا قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ جب کسی کو مطلق ذکر کیا جائے تو اس کا ذکر کامل مراد ہوتا ہے المطلق اذا اطلق یراد به الفرد الكامل تو چونکہ مدبر اور معذور کامل غلام نہیں بلکہ ناقص ہیں لہذا اس آیت کے حکم میں سرے سے شامل ہی نہیں ہیں۔
Second proof: Allah Almighty's saying: 'When they said, “Allah has not sent down anything to any human being.” Who sent down the Book that Moses brought...' Because in 'Who sent down the Book' there is a particular positive (ijab juz'i), and a particular positive is the contradictory of a universal negative (salb kulli). So it becomes known that 'Allah has not sent down' is a universal negative. And a nakirah (indefinite noun) in a positive position is specific (khas), i.e., it is for a single undetermined individual, but with respect to attributes it is absolute (mutlaq), like 'free a slave' (a'tiq raqabah) – raqabah is a nakirah in a positive position. Therefore this sentence indicates the emancipation of one slave, but the attributes of that slave can be multiple. The opposing madhhab, Imam al-Shafi'i, says that in this case it is still general ('amm), so he said: 'So the freeing of a slave (fatahriru raqabah) is general, including all types of slaves, then from it by consensus the mu'dhar (disabled) and mudabbir (slave promised freedom after master's death) are excluded, and they also exclude the kafir slave. We respond to the Shafi'is that the mu'dhar is not included in the absolute raqabah at all because the benefit of the species is lost in it; similarly the mudabbir is not included because he is from one aspect a slave and from another not a slave. So here we apply two principles. Hanafi two principles: (1) The first principle is that the absolute remains upon its absoluteness (al-mutlaq yajri 'ala itlaqihi), therefore in the mentioned verse, the word raqabah includes both believing and disbelieving slaves. (2) The second principle is that when something is mentioned in an absolute sense, its mention intends the perfect individual (al-mutlaq idha utliqa yurad bihi al-fard al-kamil). So since the mudabbir and mu'dhar are not perfect slaves but deficient, they are not included in the ruling of this verse at all.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis and usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. It begins by presenting a second proof (for a prior claim about the meaning of 'aam and khaas) from the Quran: the verse where the disbelievers said 'Allah has not sent down anything to any human being,' and then the context asks 'Who sent down the Book that Moses brought?' The Hanafi reasoning is that the phrase 'Who sent down the Book' is a particular affirmative (ijab juz'i), which contradicts the universal negative (salb kulli) implicit in the first statement. This demonstrates that a negative universal can be contradicted by a specific positive. The discussion then moves to the nature of a nakirah (indefinite noun) in a positive statement like 'free a slave' (a'tiq raqabah). The Hanafis hold that such a nakirah is specific (khas) to a single individual but absolute (mutlaq) in attributes, meaning it covers any slave fulfilling the basic condition of being a slave. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that 'raqabah' here is general ('amm) and includes all slaves, but then excludes the disabled slave (mu'dhar) and the mudabbir by consensus, and also excludes the disbelieving slave. The Hanafi response is that the mu'dhar and mudabbir are not even included in the absolute raqabah because they lack the full benefit of a slave or have mixed status. The Hanafis then state two foundational qawa'id (principles): first, the absolute operates on its absolute scope, so the verse includes both believing and disbelieving slaves; second, when something is mentioned absolutely, it refers to the perfect individual, so deficient slaves (like mu'dhar, mudabbir) are excluded from the outset, not by way of specification.

Key Terms
نکرہ موضع اثبات (nakirah fi mawdi' al-ithbat): An indefinite noun occurring in a positive sentence, which according to Hanafusul indicates a specific individual but is absolute in attributes.
مطلق (mutlaq): An unrestricted term that applies to any individual fulfilling the essential meaning, without restriction to attributes.
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave who has been promised freedom after the master's death (a type of incomplete slave).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why are a mu'dhar (disabled) slave and a mudabbir (promised freedom) excluded from the ruling of emancipation in the verse 'fa tahriru raqabah'?
A) Because they are not considered slaves at all.
B) Because the word 'raqabah' is general and they are specified out by consensus.
C) Because the absolute (mutlaq) intends the perfect individual, and they are deficient.
D) Because they are not believers.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi qawa'id on mutlaq: the absolute includes all perfect individuals by default; deficient ones are excluded from the start, not by specification.

When a Nakirah Becomes General: Rule and Exceptions
Usul rule with examples and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a nakirah becomes general due to a general description and apply the rule to oath and manumission examples, while recognizing its exceptions.

Core Idea
A nakirah described by a general description becomes general, but this rule is predominant, not universal.

In Hanafi usul, the scope of a nakirah (indefinite noun) can be expanded by a general description (sifah 'ammah). However, this rule is not absolute; it is based on customary usage and has exceptions. This lesson examines the rule, its supporting examples, and the nuances that prevent overgeneralization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nakirah and its description
Locate the indefinite noun (nakirah) and the phrase that describes it. The description may be a single word or a clause.
2
Determine if the description is general
Check whether the description applies to multiple individuals or acts (general) or is restricted to a specific instance. A general description makes the nakirah general.
A description like 'Kufan' is general because it refers to all Kufan men.
3
Apply the rule to the example
In the oath 'I will not speak to anyone except a Kufan man,' the description 'Kufan' makes the exception general; thus speaking to any Kufan man does not break the oath.
4
Recognize exceptions to the rule
Sometimes a nakirah without a description is general (e.g., 'a date is better than a locust'), and sometimes a general description does not make the nakirah general (e.g., 'I will not marry a Kufan woman' — only one marriage required).
The rule is akthari (predominant) not kulli (universal).
5
Derive the fiqh ruling
Based on the rule and exceptions, determine the legal consequence. In the manumission example, if all slaves struck, they all become free because 'any slave who struck' is general.

قوله : وان وصفت بصفة عامة تعم كقوله والله لا اكلم احداً الا رجلاً كوفيا وقوله والله لا اقربكما الا يوما اقر بكما فيه وكذا اذا قال أى عبيدي ضربك فهو حر فضربوه انهم يعتقون اگر فکر و صفت نامہ کیسا تھ موصوف ہو تو عام ہو گا یہ عرف اور استعمال کے اعتبار سے ور نہ صفت کا مفہوم تو خصوص اور تنقید ہے پھر یہ قاعدہ کلیہ نہیں بلکہ اکثری ہے کیونکہ بغیر صفت کے بھی نکر و عام ہوتا ہے جیسے حضرت عمر فاروق رضی اللہ عنہ کے فرمان، تمره خير من جرادة اور علمت نفس ما احضرت میں اس طرح علمت نفس ماقد مت میں اور کبھی صفت عامہ کے باوجود بھی نکرہ خاص ہوتا ہے جیسے اگر کوئی شخص کہے والله لا تزوجن امرأة كوفية ، تو اس صورت میں ساری عورتوں سے شادی کرنا ضروری نہیں ہے بلکہ ایک کے ساتھ شادی کرنے سے بھی قائل حانث ہونے سے نکل جائیگا اس کی مثال جیسے والله لا اكلم احدا الا رجلا كو قيا تو اگر كوفيانہ کہتا تو ایک سے زائد آدمی سے بات کرنے سے حانث ہو جاتا لیکن جب کو فیا کہا تو اب ہر شخص سے بات کرنے کی اجازت ہوگی۔ (۲) دوسری مثال یہ ہے کہ اگر کوئی شخص اپنی دو بیویوں کو مخاطب کرتے ہوئے کہے والله لا اقر بكما الا يوما أقر بكما فيه اگر صرف يوماً كتا تو ایک دن قربان کے بعد مولی ہو جاتا لیکن اقربکما کی صفت فیہ لانے سے اب کبھی بھی مولی نہیں ہو گا اس لئے کہ جس دن بھی بیویوں کے قریب ہو گا وہ دن اس صفت عامہ کی وجہ سے یمین سے مستثنیٰ ہوگا اور حانث بھی نہ ہوگا۔ (۳) تیسری مثال کسی شخص کا اپنے غلاموں کے متعلق یہ کہنا ہے ای عبيدي ضربك فهو حد " میرے جس غلام نے بھی تمہیں مارا وہ آزاد ہے تو اگر سب غلاموں نے اس آدمی کی پٹا ئی کر دی تو سب آزاد ہو جائیں گے لیکن اگر اس نے کہا که ای عبيدي ضربته .. الخ تو اگرمی طب نے سب کو مار دیا تو سب آزاد نہیں ہوں گے بلکہ ان میں سے ایک آزاد ہوگا۔ مصنف نے دونوں مثالوں میں فرق بیان کیا ہے کہ متکلم نے پہلی مثال میں ای کو نسار بیت کی صفت کے ساتھ وصوف کیا تے لہذا صفت کے عموم کی وجہ سے یہ عام ہو گا لیکن دوسری مثال میں صفت ضرب کی نسبت فرد واحد مخاطب کی طرف کی ہے جو خاص واقع ہو رہا ہے لہذا
His statement: 'And if it is described with a general description, it becomes general, like his saying: By Allah, I will not speak to anyone except a Kufan man; and his saying: By Allah, I will not approach you two except on a day in which I approach you both; and likewise if he says: Any of my slaves who struck you is free, and they struck him, they all become free.' If a nakirah is described with a general description, it becomes general. This is according to custom and usage, otherwise the meaning of a description is specification and restriction. However, this principle is not universal but predominant, because sometimes a nakirah without a description is general, like the saying of Umar al-Faruq (may Allah be pleased with him): 'A date is better than a locust' and 'Every soul knows what it has sent ahead' – similarly, 'Every soul knows what it has sent forward.' And sometimes despite a general description, a nakirah remains specific, like if a person says: 'By Allah, I will not marry a Kufan woman' – in that case, it is not necessary to marry all women; rather, marrying one woman is sufficient to avoid oath-breaking. Similarly, the example: 'By Allah, I will not speak to anyone except a Kufan man' – if he had said 'Kufan' alone, he would have broken the oath by speaking to more than one, but when he said 'Kufan', he is now allowed to speak to anyone (since the exception makes it general). (2) Second example: If a person says to his two wives: 'By Allah, I will not approach you two except on a day in which I approach you both' – if he had said 'a day' only, he would have broken the oath after one day, but by adding 'in which I approach you both' as a general description, he will never break the oath because any day he approaches them is exempted by the description. (3) Third example: A person's saying about his slaves: 'Any of my slaves who struck you is free' – if all slaves struck that person, they all become free. But if he said: 'Any of my slaves whom I struck'... then if he struck all of them, they do not all become free; rather, only one becomes free. The author explained the difference: in the first example, the speaker qualified 'ayy' with the attribute of striking (ضرب as a verbal attribute?) so due to the generality of the attribute, it became general; but in the second example, the attribute 'striking' is related to a single addressee, which is specific.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage explains a detailed rule in usul al-fiqh: When a nakirah (indefinite noun) is qualified by a general description (sifah 'ammah), it becomes general ('amm). The text provides three examples from oaths and manumission. The first example is an oath: 'I will not speak to anyone except a Kufan man.' The description 'Kufan' is a general description (since it applies to all Kufan men), so the exception includes all such men. The second example involves two wives: 'I will not approach you two except on a day in which I approach you both.' The description 'in which I approach you both' is general, so it excuses any day the husband approaches them, preventing oath-breaking. The third example involves manumission: 'Any of my slaves who struck you is free.' If all slaves struck, they all become free because 'ayy' (any) is qualified by the general possibility of striking. In contrast, if the master said 'Any of my slaves whom I struck is free,' the attribute 'whom I struck' refers to a specific act directed at a single addressee, so only one slave is freed. The commentator notes that this rule is not absolute: sometimes a nakirah without a description is general (like in 'A date is better than a locust' which is general in meaning despite no description), and sometimes despite a general description it remains specific (like 'I will not marry a Kufan woman' – the description 'Kufan' is general, but the oath is only broken by marrying one, not all). This shows the principle is predominant (akthari) not universal.

Key Terms
نکرہ موصوف بصفة عامة (nakirah mawsufah bi-sifah 'ammah): An indefinite noun qualified by a general description, which according to this rule makes the noun general in scope.
صفت عامہ (sifah 'ammah): A description that applies to many individuals, such as 'Kufan' (from Kufa) or 'in which I approach you both'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'والله لا أقر بكما إلا يوما أقر بكما فيه' (By Allah, I will not approach you two except on a day in which I approach you both), why does the husband never break the oath?
A) Because the description 'أقر بكما فيه' is general, so any day he approaches them is excepted.
B) Because the nakirah 'يوما' becomes general due to the description, so all days are excluded.
C) Because the oath is invalid from the start.
D) Both A and B.

Key Takeaway
A nakirah with a general description becomes general, but this is a predominant rule with exceptions; always check context and customary usage.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Effect of a Sifah on the 'Umum of a Nakira
Usul rule: effect of adjective on indefinite generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a sifah generally makes a nakira 'amm, identify exceptions, and analyze the objection regarding grammatical vs conceptual sifah.

Core Idea
A descriptive adjective usually makes an indefinite noun general, but custom and usage can override — it's a dominant pattern, not universal.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar teaches a nuanced usul rule: when an indefinite noun is qualified by an adjective, it generally becomes general in meaning. However, this is a predominant tendency, not an absolute rule. We will examine examples from oaths and property, and then analyze a critical objection about the nature of the adjective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its qualification
Start by locating the core claim: the author states that a nakira with a sifah becomes 'amm according to custom and usage. Note immediately that this is not a universal rule (qawida kulliya) but a predominant one (akthari). This qualification is crucial.
Hanafi usul often uses 'urf as a source; here it limits the applicability.
2
Examine the examples from oaths and property
Work through the three examples: (1) 'imra'atan kufiyya' — avoiding one Kufan woman suffices; (2) 'yawman aqru fihi' — any day you approach is exempted; (3) 'ay 'abidi darabaka' vs 'ay 'abidi darabtuhu' — all vs one slave freed. See how the sifah affects whether 'ay' is general or particular.
The difference hinges on whether the sifah attaches to the subject or object.
3
Trace the objection against the distinction
The objector argues that if by 'sifah' you mean grammatical adjective, neither example has one (since 'ay' is not a described noun). If you mean conceptual attribute, both examples have it. So the ruling should be the same in both examples.
This objection exposes the ambiguity of the term 'sifah'.
4
Follow the Hanafi response
The author responds: 'striking' (ضرب) is based on the striker (ضارب), not the struck (مضروب). The direct object (maf'ul bih) is a surplus of speech, unlike the adverb of time (zarf) which is part of the verb. This explains why the sifah in the first example (darabaka) is attached to the subject (making it general) while in the second (darabtuhu) it is attached to the object (making it particular).
This is a linguistic argument rooted in the structure of Arabic verbal sentences.

Source Text

جیسے حضرت عمر فاروق رضی اللہ عنہ کے فرمان، تمره خير من جرادة اور علمت نفس ما احضرت میں اس طرح علمت نفس ماقد مت میں اور کبھی صفت عامہ کے باوجود بھی نکرہ خاص ہوتا ہے جیسے اگر کوئی شخص کہے والله لا تزوجن کہتا تو ایک سے زائد آدمی سے بات کرنے سے حانث ہو جاتا لیکن جب کو فیا کہا تو اب ہر شخص سے بات کرنے کی اجازت ہوگی۔ (۲) دوسری مثال یہ ہے کہ اگر کوئی شخص اپنی دو بیویوں کو مخاطب کرتے ہوئے کہے والله لا اقر بكما الا يوما أقر بكما فيه اگر صرف يوماً كتا تو ایک دن قربان کے بعد مولی ہو جاتا لیکن اقربکما کی صفت فیہ لانے سے اب کبھی بھی مولی نہیں ہو گا اس لئے کہ جس دن بھی بیویوں کے قریب ہو گا وہ دن اس صفت عامہ کی وجہ سے یمین سے مستثنیٰ ہوگا اور حانث بھی نہ ہوگا۔ (۳) تیسری مثال کسی شخص کا اپنے غلاموں کے متعلق یہ کہنا ہے ای عبیدی ضربك فهو حد " میرے جس غلام نے بھی تمہیں مارا وہ آزاد ہے تو اگر سب غلاموں نے اس آدمی کی پٹا ئی کر دی تو سب آزاد ہو جائیں گے لیکن اگر اس نے کہا که ای عبیدی ضربته .. الخ تو اگرمی طب نے سب کو مار دیا تو سب آزاد نہیں ہوں گے بلکہ ان میں سے ایک آزاد ہوگا۔ مصنف نے دونوں مثالوں میں فرق بیان کیا ہے کہ متکلم نے پہلی مثال میں ای کو نسار بیت کی صفت کے ساتھ وصوف کیا تے لہذا صفت کے عموم کی وجہ سے یہ عام ہو گا لیکن دوسری مثال میں صفت ضرب کی نسبت فرد واحد مخاطب کی طرف کی ہے جو خاص واقع ہو رہا ہے لہذا (ای) عام نہیں ہوگا۔ اعتراض اعتراض یہ ہوتا ہے کہ اگر تم صفت سے صفت نحوی مراد لیتے ہوتو وہ دونوں مثالوں میں نہیں ہے کیونکہ ای موصوفہ نہیں ہے بلکہ موصولہ یا شرطیہ ہے اور اگر تم صفت معنوی مراد لیتے ہو تو وہ دونوں مثالوں میں ہے۔ کیونکہ پہلی مثال میں ان ضاربیت کے ساتھ موصوف ہے اور دوسری مثال میں مضروبینہ کے ساتھ موصوف ہے لہذا دونوں کا حکم یکساں اور مساوی ہونا چاہئے ۔ پھر یہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے کہ پہلی مثال میں الا يو ما اقر بکما پہلی مثال یعنی اقر بکما پر عموم پایا جاتا ہے حالانکہ اس میں یو ما مفعول فیہ ہے فائل نہیں ہے تو مناسب یہ ہے کہ مفعول بہ یعنی اس کا بھی یہی حکم ہو۔ جواب ... اس کا یہ ہے کہ ضرب ضارب کیسا تھ قائم ہے لہذا مضروب کیسا تھ قائم نہ ہوگی اور مفعول یہ فضلہ کلام پر قائم ہوتا ہے، بخلاف مفعول فیہ کے اس لئے کہ وہ فعل کا ایک جز ہوتا ہے بعض لوگوں نے فرق یہ کیا ہے کہ پہلی مثال میں چونکہ حق کو متعلق کیا غلاموں کے مارنے کے ساتھ تو وہ سب مارنے کیلئے جلدی کریں گے تو مولیٰ کسی کو بلا وجہ ترجیح دیگا۔ لیکن دوسری مثال میں عشق کو متعلق کیا ہے مخاطب کیسا تھ لہذا امخاطب کیلئے مناسب نہیں کہ وہ اپنے آقا ء کو مارے۔
If a thought and a descriptive adjective is such that the described is indefinite, then it becomes general. This is according to custom and usage, for the meaning of the adjective is particularity and restriction. Then this is not a universal rule but a predominant one, because sometimes an indefinite without an adjective is also general, like the command of Umar al-Faruq (may Allah be pleased with him): 'A date is better than a locust' and in 'antam nafs ma ahdarat' in that way, and 'antam nafs ma qaddamat'. And sometimes despite a general adjective, the indefinite is particular, like if a person says: 'By Allah, I will not marry a Kufan woman', then in that case it is not necessary to marry all women, rather marrying one will also save him from being an oath-breaker. Its example is like 'By Allah, I will not speak to anyone except a Kufan man', so if he had said 'Kufan', then speaking to more than one person would cause him to break the oath, but when he said 'Kufyan' (Kufan), then now speaking to every person is allowed. (2) The second example: if a person addresses his two wives and says: 'By Allah, I will not approach you except on a day I approach you in it.' If he had only said 'a day', then after approaching one day he would become an oath-breaker, but by bringing the adjective 'I approach in it' now he will never break the oath, because whichever day he approaches the wives, that day by virtue of this general adjective is exempted from the oath and he will not break the oath. (3) The third example is a person saying about his slaves: 'Any slave of mine who strikes you is free.' So if all the slaves beat that man, all become free. But if he said: 'Any slave of mine I strike you' ... etc., then if they all beat him, they do not all become free, rather one of them becomes free. The author explained the difference in both examples: that the speaker in the first example qualified 'ay' with the adjective 'beating you', so due to the generality of the adjective, it becomes general. But in the second example, he attributed the adjective 'striking' to the individual addressee, which is particular, so 'ay' is not general. Objection: This objection is made that if you mean by 'adjective' a grammatical adjective, then it is not present in both examples because 'ay' is not a described noun but a relative or conditional. And if you mean a conceptual attribute, then it is present in both examples because in the first example it is described with being a striker, and in the second with being struck. So both should have the same ruling. Furthermore, it is possible that in the first example, 'except a day I approach you in it' generality is found in 'I approach you' even though 'day' is a zarf (adverb of time), not a subject. So it is appropriate that the direct object also has the same ruling. Response: Its answer is that 'striking' is based on the striker, so 'struck' is not based on it. And the object (maf'ul bih) is based on the surplus of the speech, unlike the adverb of time because it is a part of the verb. Some people differentiated that in the first example, because the truth is related to the slaves beating, they will all rush to beat, so the master will prefer one without reason. But in the second example, love is related to the addressee, so it is not appropriate for the addressee to beat his master.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a usul rule regarding the effect of a descriptive adjective (sifah) on an indefinite noun (nakira). The author states that when a nakira is qualified by a sifah, it generally becomes general (amm) in meaning according to custom and usage, because the sifah itself implies particularization? Actually, the text says the sifah's meaning is particularization (takhsis), but it makes the nakira general? Wait, the Arabic/Urdu: 'ور نہ صفت کا مفہوم تو خصوص اور تنقید ہے' suggests the sifah's own meaning is particularization, yet it causes generality—this is nuanced. The author clarifies this is not a universal rule (qawida kulliya) but a predominant pattern (akthari). He gives examples: the statement of Umar radiAllahu anhu 'tamratun khayrun min jaradatin' uses indefinite words without sifah, yet they are general. Conversely, an adjective can restrict generality, as in the oath 'la atazawwajanna imra'atan kufiyya' only requires avoiding one Kufan woman. The passage then examines three oath examples with 'ay' (any) showing how the sifah affects generality. An objection arises: the sifah in these examples is either grammatical or conceptual, and the response explains the difference based on the relationship of the sifah to the verb. The author follows the Hanafi method of linguistic and rational analysis, using 'urf (custom) and language usage as proofs.

Key Terms
نکرہ (nakira): An indefinite noun; in this passage, its ruling on generality when qualified by an adjective.
صفت (sifah): A descriptive adjective or attribute that qualifies a noun; can be grammatical or conceptual.
عموم ('umum): Generality; the passage discusses how a sifah can cause a nakira to become general.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling when a person swears 'By Allah, I will not marry a Kufan woman'?
A) He must marry all Kufan women to avoid breaking the oath.
B) He must marry one Kufan woman to avoid breaking the oath.
C) He cannot marry any Kufan woman.
D) The oath is invalid because 'imra'atan kufiyya' is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
A descriptive adjective generally makes an indefinite noun general, but this is a predominant pattern (akthari) subject to exceptions based on linguistic structure and context.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Lam al-Ta'rif and Generality: Covenant vs. Umum
States the rule for lam al-ta'rif shifting from covenant to generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that lam al-ta'rif necessitates 'umum when 'ahd is impossible, and identify which cases fall under this rule.

Core Idea
When lam al-ta'rif cannot refer to a known particular, it automatically indicates generality.

The definite article in Arabic (al-) can serve two functions: indicating a previously known specific referent (covenant, 'ahd) or indicating generality (umum). In Hanafi usul, the default is 'ahd, but when the word cannot accept definition by 'ahd, the article shifts to 'umum. This rule has direct implications for legal language, such as oaths and contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word with lam al-ta'rif
Locate the noun phrase that begins with the definite article (al-). This is the subject of the analysis.
Example: 'al-nisa' (the women) in the oath 'La atazawwaju al-nisa'.
2
Determine if it can accept definition by 'ahd
Ask whether the word can refer to a specific, previously mentioned or contextually known referent. If a prior covenant exists, the lam is for 'ahd; if not, the word may still be capable of 'ahd if it is a singular that could be known.
Plurals like 'al-nisa' often cannot be for 'ahd unless there is a specific group previously mentioned, e.g., 'the women of the tribe'.
3
Apply the rule: if 'ahd is impossible, lam indicates 'umum
When the word cannot be defined by 'ahd (i.e., there is no prior or known particular referent), the lam shifts to indicate generality. This generality either covers the entire genus or all individuals.
The passage states: 'awjabat al-'umum' (it obligates generality).
4
Test the rule with the oath example
Consider the oath 'La atazawwaju al-nisa' (I will not marry the women). Since no specific group of women is indicated, 'ahd is impossible. Therefore, al-nisa means all women. Marrying one woman fulfills the violation condition.
The Hanafi ruling: he breaks the oath by marrying any single woman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وكذ اذا دخلت لام التعريف فيما لا يحتمل التعر يف بمعنى العهد أوجبت العموم حتى يسقط اعتبار الجمعية اذ ا دخلت على الجمع عملاً بالدليلين فيحنث بتزوج امرأة واحدة اذا حلف لا يتزوج النساء . تشریح عبارت جب لام تعریف ایسے لفظ پر داخل ہوتا ہے جو تعریف عبدی کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا تو وہ عموم کیلئے ہو گا خواہ عموم جنس کی وجہ سے ہو یا استغراق کی وجہ سے اور اس میں اس بات پر تنبیہ ہے کہ لام تعریف میں اصل یہ ہے کہ وہ عہد کے لئے ہو اور جب تک عہد والا معنی صحیح ہو سکتا ہے دوسرا معنی
His statement: And likewise, when the definite article enters upon something that cannot accept definition in the sense of covenant, it obligates generality, such that the consideration of plurality falls when it enters upon a plural, acting on both proofs, so he breaks the oath by marrying one woman if he swore not to marry women. Explanation of the phrase: When the definite article enters upon a word that does not possess the possibility of definition by covenant, then it will be for generality, whether the generality is due to genus or due to inclusive coverage. And in this there is a warning that the original in the definite article is that it is for covenant, and as long as the meaning of covenant can be correct, the other meaning...
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents a usul rule regarding the definite article (lam al-ta'rif). The matn states that when lam al-ta'rif enters a word that is not capable of having definition by 'ahd (a previously mentioned or known particular), then it necessitates generality (umum). This applies even when the word is plural, so the consideration of plurality (that one instance suffices) is overridden. The example given: if a person swears 'la atazawwaju al-nisa' (I will not marry women), and then marries one woman, he breaks the oath because the lam in 'al-nisa' indicates all women generally, not just a specific group. The commentator explains that the lam could be for 'umum al-jins (generality of genus) or 'umum al-istighraq (inclusive generality). The key point is that the default (asl) for lam al-ta'rif is for 'ahd (covenant/present knowledge), but when that is impossible, it shifts to 'umum. This is a standard Hanafi position distinguishing between different types of definition.

Key Terms
لام تعریف (lam al-ta'rif): The definite article in Arabic; its default use is for 'ahd, but if that is impossible, it indicates 'umum.
عہد ('ahd): Prior covenant or known reference; the definite article indicating a previously mentioned or mutually known particular.
عموم ('umum): Generality; here it is the secondary meaning of lam al-ta'rif when 'ahd is not possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears 'La atazawwaju al-nisa' (I will not marry the women), what is the ruling according to the passage if he marries one woman?
A) He does not break the oath because 'al-nisa' refers to a specific group of women.
B) He breaks the oath because 'al-nisa' with the definite article indicates all women generally when no 'ahd is present.
C) The oath is invalid because 'al-nisa' is ambiguous.
D) He only breaks the oath if he marries all women.

Key Takeaway
When lam al-ta'rif cannot be for 'ahd, it necessitates 'umum, but the default is 'ahd.

The Dispute on 'Amm in 'Ayyu' and Sifah
Objection and answer on 'ayyu' not being 'amm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between grammatical adjective and descriptive attribute in determining whether 'ayyu' is 'amm, and evaluate the Hanafi response to the objection.

Core Idea
Whether 'ayyu' is 'amm depends on its grammatical function; the Hanafi answer hinges on the object type.

This passage presents a classic usul dispute: does the word 'ayyu' (which) indicate generality? The objector argues that if we take sifah as grammatical adjective, it fails; if as descriptive attribute, both examples are equal. The Hanafi response distinguishes between the grammatical roles of the object, showing that not all objects generate generality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim and objection
The passage opens with the claim that 'ayyu' will not be 'amm. Then an objection is raised: if sifah is interpreted grammatically, it is absent in both examples; if descriptively, it is present, so rulings should be equal. Additionally, the first example has generality despite different grammatical roles.
Understand the two-way objection: (a) inconsistency in sifah interpretation, (b) generality in first example with maf'ul fihi.
2
Parse the two types of sifah
The objector distinguishes between sifah nahwiyyah (grammatical adjective) and sifah ma'nawiyyah (descriptive attribute). In the first example, 'ayyu' is a relative/conditional particle (not a grammatical adjective), so if sifah is grammatical, it is absent. If descriptive, both examples have a descriptive attribute (being a beater vs being beaten).
This distinction is crucial: the same word can function differently in different contexts.
3
Follow the objector's reasoning
The objector argues that if sifah is descriptive, both examples are equal, so 'ayyu' should be 'amm in both. Moreover, the first example has 'ayyu' with 'ma' as maf'ul fihi yet shows generality, so the same should apply to maf'ul bihi.
The objector uses consistency as a critique.
4
Analyze the Hanafi answer
The response clarifies that ضرب (beating) inherently implies a beater (ضارب) but not necessarily a beaten (مضروب). The direct object (maf'ul bihi) stands on the surplus part of the sentence, whereas the adverb of time/place (maf'ul fihi) is integral to the verb. Thus, they are not analogous. A practical difference is also given: in the first example, the master's right is connected to beating all slaves, necessitating equal treatment; in the second, love is connected to the addressee, so it would be inappropriate for him to beat his masters.
The answer uses both grammatical and practical (ma'nawi) reasons.
5
Determine the practical legal implication
The Hanafi position implies that 'ayyu' is not always 'amm; its generality depends on the nature of the object and the context. This affects how commands with 'ayyu' are interpreted in fiqh.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the ruling differs based on the grammatical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ای) عام نہیں ہوگا۔
اعتراض اعتراض یہ ہوتا ہے کہ اگر تم صفت سے صفت نحوی مراد لیتے ہوتو وہ دونوں مثالوں میں نہیں ہے کیونکہ ای موصوفہ نہیں ہے بلکہ موصولہ یا شرطیہ ہے اور اگر تم صفت معنوی مراد لیتے ہو تو وہ دونوں مثالوں میں ہے۔ کیونکہ پہلی مثال میں ان ضاربیت کے ساتھ موصوف ہے اور دوسری مثال میں مضروبینہ کے ساتھ موصوف ہے لہذا دونوں کا حکم یکساں اور مساوی ہونا چاہئے ۔ پھر یہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے کہ پہلی مثال میں الا يو ما اقر بکما پہلی مثال یعنی اقر بکما پر عموم پایا جاتا ہے حالانکہ اس میں یو ما مفعول فیہ ہے فائل نہیں ہے تو مناسب یہ ہے کہ مفعول بہ یعنی اس کا بھی یہی حکم ہو۔
جواب ... اس کا یہ ہے کہ ضرب ضارب کیسا تھ قائم ہے لہذا مضروب کیسا تھ قائم نہ ہوگی اور مفعول یہ فضلہ کلام پر قائم ہوتا ہے، بخلاف مفعول فیہ کے اس لئے کہ وہ فعل کا ایک جز ہوتا ہے بعض لوگوں نے فرق یہ کیا ہے کہ پہلی مثال میں چونکہ حق کو متعلق کیا غلاموں کے مارنے کے ساتھ تو وہ سب مارنے کیلئے جلدی کریں گے تو مولیٰ کسی کو بلا وجہ ترجیح دیگا۔ لیکن دوسری مثال میں عشق کو متعلق کیا ہے مخاطب کیسا تھ لہذا امخاطب کیلئے مناسب نہیں کہ وہ اپنے آقا ء کو مارے۔
(Ayyu) will not be 'amm. Objection: It is objected that if you mean by 'sifah' grammatical adjective, then it is not in both examples because 'ayyu' is not mawsufah but mawsulah or shartiyyah. And if you mean descriptive adjective, then it is in both examples, because in the first example it is described by ضاربیت and in the second by مضروبینہ, so the ruling of both should be equal. Moreover, it is possible that in the first example, 'ayyu ma aqribakuma' has generality, even though 'ma' is maf'ul fihi, not fa'il, so it is appropriate that the same ruling apply to maf'ul bihi. Answer: The answer is that ضرب is established with ضارب, so مضروب will not be established, and maf'ul stands on the surplus part of the utterance, unlike maf'ul fihi because it is part of the verb. Some people made a distinction: in the first example, since the right is connected to beating slaves, they will all rush to beat, so the master will not favor anyone unjustly. But in the second example, love is connected to the addressee, so it is not appropriate for the addressee to beat his masters.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding whether the word 'ayyu' ('which') can be considered 'amm (general). The objector argues that if by 'sifah' we mean grammatical adjective, then 'ayyu' is not an adjective but a relative or conditional particle, so it cannot be 'amm. If we mean descriptive attribute, then both examples have it, so they should be treated equally. The answer distinguishes: ضرب (beating) implies a beater (ضارب) but not necessarily a beaten (مضروب), and the direct object stands on the surplus part of the sentence, whereas the adverb of time/place is part of the verb. Additionally, a practical distinction is given: in the first example, the master's right is connected to beating all slaves, so he must treat them equally; in the second, love is connected to the addressee, so it would be inappropriate for him to beat his own masters.

Key Terms
عام (ʿāmm): A word that covers all members of its category without limitation.
صفت (ṣifah): Here a distinction is made between grammatical adjective (sifah nahwiyyah) and descriptive attribute (sifah ma'nawiyyah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, why does 'ayyu' in the second example not produce generality like the first?
A) Because 'ayyu' is a conditional particle
B) Because the direct object is a surplus part, unlike the adverb of time/place
C) Because the second example involves love, not beating
D) Because 'ayyu' is definite

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not treat 'ayyu' as always 'amm; the grammatical role of the object (adverb vs direct object) determines whether generality is indicated.

Laam Ta'rif: From 'Ahd to Generality When 'Ahd Is Impossible
Rule on laam ta'rif causing generality when 'ahd impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that laam ta'rif indicates generality when 'ahd is impossible, and derive its consequences for plural forms and Quranic interpretation.

Core Idea
When laam ta'rif cannot refer to a specific known referent, it triggers generality over plural forms.

This lesson explores a foundational rule in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the default function of the definite article 'al' is to indicate 'ahd (previous mention). However, when 'ahd is impossible, the laam shifts to indicate generality (umum). This rule has significant practical consequences in interpreting Quranic verses and legal oaths.

Guided Path
1
Understand the default: laam ta'rif for 'ahd
The original use of laam ta'rif is to refer to a previously mentioned or contextually known entity. Recognize that this is the default.
If 'ahd is possible, the laam does NOT indicate generality.
2
Identify when 'ahd is impossible
When the noun has no prior mention or known referent (e.g., generic nouns, first mention), 'ahd cannot be intended. In such cases, the laam must indicate another meaning.
Examples: 'al-nisa' in an oath without prior context, 'al-fuqara' in the zakat verse.
3
Apply the shift: laam indicates generality
When 'ahd is impossible, laam ta'rif gives benefit of generality (umum) either by genus or istighraq. For plurals, it overrides plurality, so the term applies even to one individual.
The rule: 'تعم لدفع الاشتراك' – generality to resolve ambiguity.
4
Test with oath example
If someone swears 'لا أتزوج النساء' and marries one woman, he breaks the oath because 'al-nisa' indicates any woman, even one.
This shows the practical fiqh consequence.
5
Analyze Quranic examples and dispute
In 'لَا يَحِلُّ لَكَ النِّسَاءُ' and 'إِنَّمَا الصَّدَقَاتُ لِلْفُقَرَاءِ', the laam indicates generality. The Shafi'i school differs on the zakat verse, requiring multiple recipients.
Hanafi: one poor person suffices. Shafi'i: at least three from each category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وكذ اذا دخلت لام التعريف فيما لا يحتمل التعر يف بمعنى العهد أوجبت العموم حتى يسقط اعتبار الجمعية اذ ا دخلت على الجمع عملاً بالدليلين فيحنث بتزوج امرأة واحدة اذا حلف لا يتزوج النساء .
تشریح عبارت
جب لام تعریف ایسے لفظ پر داخل ہوتا ہے جو تعریف عبدی کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا تو وہ عموم کیلئے ہو گا خواہ عموم جنس کی وجہ سے ہو یا استغراق کی وجہ سے اور اس میں اس بات پر تنبیہ ہے کہ لام تعریف میں اصل یہ ہے کہ وہ عہد کے لئے ہو اور جب تک عہد والا معنی صحیح ہو سکتا ہے دوسرا معنی مراد نہیں لیا جائیگا لیکن جب عبدو الا معنی صحیح نہ ہو تو استغراق کیوجہ سے عموم کا فائدہ دے گا۔ اگر یہ جمع کے صیغہ پر داخل ہو تو جمعیت کا اعتبار ساقط ہو جائے گا اس لئے کہ اگر جمعیت کو باقی رکھیں تو لام تعریف کا کوئی فائدہ ہی نہ ہوگا۔ لہذا اس کو جنس پر عمل کیا جائیگا تو جنس ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کا اطلاق ما دون الثلاث پر ہوگا اور جمع ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کا اطلاق مافوق الثلاث پر ہوگا لہذا اگر کسی نے قسم اٹھائی کہ لا اتزوج النساء تو وہ صرف ایک عورت سے شادی کرنے سے حانث ہو جائیگا ۔
ای طرح قرآن پاک میں حضور اکرم صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کو خطاب ہے ۔ لا يحل لك النساء من بعد یعنی ان ازواج مطہرات رضی اللہ عنہن کے بعد آپ کیلئے کوئی عورت حلال نہیں ) تو یہاں نساء سے ایک عورت بھی مراد ہے اسی طرح انما الصدقات للفقرا ءالخ میں کسی ایک فقیر یا مسکین کو صدقہ دینا کافی ہے اس میں امام شافعی کا اختلاف ہے۔ ان کے نزدیک مصارف زکوۃ کے احناف سبعہ میں سے ہر صنف کے تین افراد کوز کو ۃ دینا ضروری ہے۔
His statement: And likewise, when laam ta'rif enters upon a word that does not admit definition in the sense of 'ahd (previous mention), it necessitates generality, to the extent that consideration of plurality falls when it enters upon a plural, acting upon both proofs. So if someone swears لا أتزوج النساء (I will not marry women), he becomes a sinner by marrying one woman. Explanation: When laam ta'rif enters upon a word that cannot accept definition by 'ahd, it will be for generality, whether due to genus or istighraq. And this alerts that the original use of laam ta'rif is for 'ahd, and as long as the meaning of 'ahd is valid, another meaning is not intended. But when the meaning of 'ahd is not valid, it gives the benefit of generality due to istighraq. If it enters upon a plural form, consideration of plurality is dropped, because if plurality is retained, laam ta'rif would have no benefit. So it is applied to genus, so its application covers ما دون الثلاث (less than three) due to genus, and ما فوق الثلاث (more than three) due to plurality. So if someone swears لا أتزوج النساء, he becomes a sinner by marrying one woman. Similarly in the Quran, addressing the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم: لَا يَحِلُّ لَكَ النِّسَاءُ مِن بَعْدُ (Women are not lawful for you after this), meaning after the pure wives, here نساء means one woman. Similarly in إِنَّمَا الصَّدَقَاتُ لِلْفُقَرَاءِ (Alms are only for the poor), giving to one poor or needy person is sufficient. In this, Imam Shafi'i has a difference: according to him, it is necessary to give zakat to three individuals from each of the seven categories of zakat recipients.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage explains a key usul rule: when laam ta'rif (the definite article) enters a word that cannot be made definite by 'ahd (previous contextual reference), it indicates generality (umum) either by genus or istighraq (complete inclusion). The default use of laam is for 'ahd, but if 'ahd is impossible, it switches to generality. When it enters a plural, the consideration of number is dropped because otherwise the laam would have no function. Thus, 'al-nisa' in an oath means any woman, even one. Examples from Quran: لَا يَحِلُّ لَكَ النِّسَاءُ means one woman, and إِنَّمَا الصَّدَقَاتُ لِلْفُقَرَاءِ means giving to one poor person suffices. The text notes that Imam Shafi'i disagreed on the zakat distribution, requiring three recipients from each category.

Key Terms
لام تعریف (lām al-taʿrīf): The definite article 'al' which normally indicates a previously known referent (عهد) but if 'ahd is impossible, it indicates generality.
عهد (ʿahd): Previous mention or known reference that makes the noun definite.
استغراق (istighrāq): Complete inclusion of all individuals of a genus, a type of generality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rule explained in this passage, if someone swears لا أتزوج النساء and marries one woman, does he become a sinner (hanith)?
A) Yes, because النساء with laam ta'rif indicates one woman
B) No, because النساء requires at least two women
C) Yes, because the laam indicates generality including one
D) No, because oaths are interpreted strictly

Key Takeaway
Laam ta'rif defaults to 'ahd; only when 'ahd is impossible does it indicate generality, overriding the numerical implication of plurals.

Repetition of Nakira and Ma'rifa in Usul al-Fiqh
Rules for repeated indefinite and definite nouns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of nakira/ma'rifa repetition to Quranic verses and derive whether the second instance refers to the same or a different entity.

Core Idea
When an indefinite noun is repeated as definite, it is the same; when repeated as indefinite, it is different.

This passage establishes a core usul principle: the definiteness of a repeated noun determines whether the second occurrence refers to the same entity or a different one. Understanding this rule is critical for interpreting Quranic promises and legal texts correctly, as a single repetition can carry distinct legal implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the noun and its repetition pattern
Locate the noun in its first occurrence and note whether it is nakira (indefinite) or ma'rifa (definite). Then examine the second occurrence: is it also nakira or ma'rifa?
The pattern determines the identity relationship.
2
Apply the rule for nakira repeated as ma'rifa
If the first is nakira and the second is ma'rifa, the second refers to the same entity as the first. Confirm with the Quranic example of 'رسولا' (indefinite) followed by 'الرسول' (definite).
The definitive article 'al' signals identity.
3
Apply the rule for nakira repeated as nakira
If both are nakira, each refers to a different entity. Use the example of 'يسر' (indefinite ease) appearing twice in Surah Ash-Sharh to confirm distinct eases.
Indefiniteness indicates non-identity when repeated.
4
Analyze the Quranic example from the source
In the verse 'فإن مع العسر يسرا إن مع العسر يسرا', identify 'العسر' as ma'rifa both times (same hardship) and 'يسر' as nakira both times (different eases). This yields a profound ruling: one hardship is followed by multiple eases.
The istinbat chain shows how the rule yields legal meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والنكرة اذا اعيدت معرفة كانت الثانية عين الا ولى
واذا أعيدت نكرة كانت الثانية غير الا ولى والمعرفة اذا اعيدت معرفة كانت الثانية عين الا ولى واذا اعيدت نكرة كانت الثانية غير الاولى
اگر نگرہ کو دوبارہ معرفہ لایا جائے تو ثانی عین اول ہو گا جیسے کما قالا اللہ تعالیٰ ارسلنا الى فرعون رسولا فعصى فرعون الرسول ، اور اگر نکرہ کو دو بار مکر رلایا جا ئے تو ثانی غیر اول ہوگا اور اگر معرفہ کو معرفہ لایا جائے تو ثانی مین اول ہوگا۔
ان دنوں قاعدوں کی مثال اللہ تعالی کا فرمان ہے ۔ فان مع العسر يسرا ان مع العسر يسراً المر کو چونکہ دوبارہ معرفہ لایا گیا ہے لہذا یہ مین اول ہے اور یسر کو چونکہ دوبارہ نکرہ ای یہ
His statement: And when a nakira is repeated as a ma'rifa, the second is the same as the first. And when it is repeated as a nakira, the second is other than the first. And when a ma'rifa is repeated as a ma'rifa, the second is the same as the first, and when it is repeated as a nakira, the second is other than the first. Explanation: If a nakira is brought again as a ma'rifa, then the second is the same as the first, as Allah said: أرسلنا إلى فرعون رسولا فعصى فرعون الرسول (We sent to Pharaoh a messenger, but Pharaoh disobeyed the messenger). And if a nakira is repeated as a nakira, then the second is other than the first. And if a ma'rifa is repeated as a ma'rifa, then the second is the same as the first. An example of these rules is Allah's saying: فإن مع العسر يسرا إن مع العسر يسرا (For indeed, with hardship, ease. Indeed, with hardship, ease). Since العسر is brought twice as a ma'rifa, it is the same, and يسر is brought twice as a nakira, so it is other.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents fundamental usul rules about the repetition of indefinite (nakira) and definite (ma'rifa) nouns. In Arabic, when an indefinite noun is repeated as a definite noun, it refers to the same individual; when repeated as indefinite, it refers to a different one. Similarly, a definite noun repeated as definite refers to the same, but if repeated as indefinite, it refers to another. The Quranic example: 'رسولا' (indefinite) then 'الرسول' (definite) shows it is the same messenger. The example of Surah Ash-Sharh: العسر is definite both times, so it is the same hardship; يسر is indefinite both times, so each is a different ease. This rule is crucial for understanding Quranic interpretation and legal deduction.

Key Terms
نکرہ (nakira): An indefinite noun that does not refer to a specific entity
معرفہ (ma'rifa): A definite noun referring to a specific or previously known entity
ثانی عین اول (thānī ʿayn al-ūlā): The second instance is identical to the first

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the verse 'إن مع العسر يسرا', why is يسر understood to be a different ease each time?
A) Because it is indefinite and repeated as indefinite
B) Because it is definite and repeated as indefinite
C) Because it is a different word
D) Because the context requires two different eases

Key Takeaway
The definiteness pattern of a repeated noun determines whether it refers to the same or a different entity, a foundational principle in Quranic interpretation.

Two Types of What Specification Ends At
Defines the two categories of the extreme limit of specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of the extreme limit of specification (ma yantahi ilayhi al-khusus) in the Hanafi usul and apply them to distinguish between singular and plural general words.

Core Idea
A general word's specification ends at one for singulars, but at three for plurals by consensus.

This lesson introduces a key concept from Hanafi principles of jurisprudence: the two types of 'what specification ends at'. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting general commands (awamir) in the Quran and Sunnah, as it determines the minimum number of individuals required to fulfill a general directive.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the classification
The passage divides words into two categories: singular in form (or annexed) and plural in form or meaning.
2
Examine the first type
For singular words (e.g., 'man', 'ma') or definite singulars like 'al-mar'ah', the extreme limit is one.
If the word lacks even one individual, it becomes empty of meaning.
3
Examine the second type
For plural words (e.g., 'rijal', 'qawm'), the extreme limit is three.
This applies whether plural in form and meaning or meaning only.
4
Consider the basis
The minimum of plural is three by consensus of linguists (ijma ahl al-lugha).
5
Apply the rule
In interpreting commands, if a general word is singular, it can be fulfilled by one; if plural, at least three are required.
For example, 'akrim al-ulama' (honor the scholars) implies at least three scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وما يتنهى اليه الخصوص نوعان : النوع الاول الواحد
هو فرد بصيغته و ملحق به كالمرأة والنساء والنوع الثاني فيما كان
جمعاً صيغة ومعنى لأن أدنى الجمع الثلاثة بإجماع اهل اللغة
مایعی الیہ المخصوص کی تقسیم
(1)
پہلی قسم یہ ہے کہ اگر صیغہ مفرد کا ہو جیسے من اور مایا ملحق بالمفرد ہو جیسے جمع معرف بلام الجنس تو اسکی انتہا ایک تک ہوگی کیونکہ اگر لفظ اس ایک سے بھی خالی ہو جائے تو لفظ اپنے مدلول سے بھی خالی ہو جائیگا جیسے المرأة والنساء
(۲) دوسری قسم یہ ہے کہ لفظ صیفہ اور معنی کے اعتبار سے جمع ہو جیسے رجال اور نساء یا صرف معنی کے اعتبار سے جمع ہو جیسے قوم اور رحط تو اس کی انتہا تین تک ہوگی کیونکہ اقل جمع تین ہے تو اگر اس کے تحت تین بھی نہر ہیں تو لفظ اپنے مقصود سے خالی ہو جائیگا۔
His statement: 'And what the specification ends at is of two types: the first type is the singular (al-wahid), it is an individual by its form or annexed to it, like al-mar'ah and al-nisa. The second type is what is plural in form and meaning, because the minimum of plural is three by consensus of the linguists.' Division of what the specification ends at: (1) The first type: if the form is singular, such as 'man' and 'ma', or annexed to the singular, such as a definite plural with 'al' for the genus, then its extreme is one, because if the word becomes devoid of this one, the word would be empty of its meaning, like 'al-mar'ah' and 'al-nisa'. (2) The second type: if the word is plural in form and meaning, like 'rijal' and 'nisa', or plural in meaning only, like 'qawm' and 'raht', then its extreme is three, because the minimum of plural is three; if it does not contain three, the word would be empty of its intended meaning.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of 'what specification ends at' (ma yantahi ilayhi al-khusus). The author, citing the musannif, explains that the first type is when the word is in singular form or attached to a singular, like 'man' or 'ma', or a definite noun with 'al' for the genus, e.g., 'al-mar'ah' and 'al-nisa'. In this case, the extreme of specification is one, because if the word is left with no individuals, it would be empty of meaning. The second type is when the word is plural in form and meaning, like 'rijal' and 'nisa', or plural in meaning only, like 'qawm' and 'raht'. For these, the extreme is three, because the minimum plural is three by consensus of linguists. If the word does not include at least three individuals, it fails to convey its intended meaning.

Key Terms
ما يتنهى إليه الخصوص (ma yantahi ilayhi al-khusus): The extreme limit of specification, i.e., the minimum number of individuals required to fulfill a general word's meaning.
اقل الجمع (adna al-jam‘): The minimum number of individuals required for a word to be considered plural.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the extreme limit of specification for singular words is one, and for plural words is three, based on linguistic consensus.

Defining Mushtarak: A Word of Multiple Meanings
Defining mushtarak and its constraints

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of mushtarak, explain the function of each constraint, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i view on alternative vs. collective use.

Core Idea
Mushtarak applies to individuals of different definitions alternatively — one meaning at a time.

The term 'mushtarak' (common/ambiguous) is a cornerstone of Hanafi usul al-fiqh, referring to a word that has multiple distinct lexical meanings. In this passage, the author presents the definition with three critical constraints, each excluding a different category of words. The discussion also touches on a key intra-madhhab dispute: whether mushtarak can be used for all its meanings simultaneously. Let's parse the definition carefully.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition and its components
Find the phrase 'ما يتناول أفراداً مختلفة الحدود على سبيل البدل' and identify the three main parts: 'أفراد', 'مختلفة الحدود', and 'على سبيل البدل'. Understand that each serves as a constraint (qayd).
The definition is quoted from the matn.
2
Analyze each constraint
Consider how each constraint excludes a specific type of word: 'أفراد' excludes khass (since it has no individuals); 'مختلفة الحدود' excludes amm (whose individuals share one definition); 'على سبيل البدل' excludes any word that could be used collectively.
The author explains these exclusions in the commentary.
3
Understand the alternative (badal) vs. collective (shumul) dispute
The constraint 'على سبيل البدل' is presented as precautionary (ihtirazi) to reject the Shafi'i view that mushtarak can be used for all meanings at once. Recognize that the Hanafi position requires picking one meaning at a time.
The text mentions Imam al-Shafi'i explicitly.
4
Apply the definition to an example: 'qur''
The word 'qur'' is given as an example, meaning either menstruation or purity. Verify that it satisfies the definition: it has individuals (instances), they have different definitions, and it must be used alternatively.
In legal texts, context specifies which meaning is intended.

Discussion of Mushtarak. His statement: As for al-mushtarak, it is what encompasses individuals of different definitions alternatively, like al-qur' for menstruation and purity. Definition of mushtarak: It encompasses individuals of different definitions alternatively. Translation: Mushtarak is a word that includes, on an alternative basis, words whose definitions differ from each other. Benefits observed in the definition and its constraints: (1) Individuals: by this, al-khass (specific) is excluded because it does not have individuals. (2) Different definitions: by this constraint, al-amm (general) is excluded because its individuals all are agreed in terms of definitions. (3) On an alternative basis: from this constraint several benefits are obtained which are listed below: (a) If this constraint is not precautionary, it is only for describing the reality. (b) If it is considered precautionary, then it is to avoid the statement of Imam Muhammad bin Idris al-Shafi'i that it is on a collective basis, because according to the Shafi'is, mushtarak can have multiple meanings simultaneously. Or it is to avoid the view of some scholars of usul that 'thing' in terms of existing meaning is mushtarak ma'nawi, and in terms of individuals having different realities it is superior to mushtarak lafzi, but its relation to its individuals is on a collective basis, so the constraint 'alternatively' avoids that. 12. (2) Example of mushtarak: The example of mushtarak is the word qur' which is between menstruation and purity.

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'mushtarak' (common/ambiguous word) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by quoting the matn (the original text) stating that mushtarak is a word that applies to individuals of different definitions on an alternative basis (badal). The Arabic definition is given, followed by an Urdu translation and explanation. The definition contains three key constraints: 'individuals' excludes specific terms (khass) which have no individuals; 'different definitions' excludes general terms (amm) whose individuals share the same definition; and 'on an alternative basis' excludes the possibility of multiple simultaneous meanings. The author then discusses the purpose of the 'alternative' constraint, noting that it serves to differentiate from the Shafi'i position that mushtarak can be used in multiple meanings at once (shumul). It also distinguishes from mushtarak ma'nawi (a single meaning with different realizations) which relates to its individuals collectively, not alternatively.

Key Terms
مشترک (mushtarak): A word that applies to individuals of different definitions on an alternative basis (علی سبیل البدل).
علی سبیل البدل (ala sabi al-badal): On an alternative basis; one meaning at a time, not simultaneously.
علی سبیل الشمول (ala sabi al-shumul): On a collective basis; multiple meanings intended at once.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of mushtarak requires that multiple meanings be taken one at a time (badal), not all together (shumul), ensuring clarity in legal interpretation.
