Definition of Mushtarak and Its Conditions
Defines the term mushtarak and gives an example with explanation of the definition's conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of mushtarak, identify its three conditions, and explain why the condition 'ala sabil al-badal' excludes the Shafi'i view of simultaneous meanings.

Core Idea
Mushtarak: a word with multiple distinct meanings, but only one at a time per usage.

In Hanafi usul, mushtarak is a key term that governs how we interpret words with multiple distinct meanings. This lesson dissects its definition, focusing on the three conditions that distinguish it from other categories like 'amm (general) and 'khas (specific). Understanding these conditions is crucial for correctly applying mushtarak in istinbat, especially when encountering such words in the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components
Break down the definition into its constituent parts: 'ma yatanawalu afradan' (encompasses individuals), 'mukhtalifata al-hudud' (different definitions), and 'ala sabil al-badal' (on an alternative basis).
2
Exclude the 'khas'
The condition 'individuals' (afrad) excludes 'khas' (specific), which has no individuals because it refers to a particular meaning directly.
3
Exclude the 'amm'
The condition 'different definitions' (mukhtalifata al-hudud) excludes 'amm' (general), whose individuals all share the same definition.
4
Understand 'ala sabil al-badal'
This condition means that when used, only one meaning is intended, not multiple simultaneously. It distinguishes mushtarak from the Shafi'i view allowing simultaneous meanings (umum al-mushtarak) and from conceptual mushtarak which relates to its individuals comprehensively.
5
Apply the example
The word 'quru' is a classic mushtarak meaning both menstruation (hayd) and purity (tahr). Without a qarina (contextual evidence), one cannot assume both meanings at once; only one is intended.

مشترک ہے۔
His statement: 'As for al-mushtarak, it is that which encompasses individuals with different definitions, alternativel; for example, 'quru' for menstruation and purity.' Definition of mushtarak: 'What encompasses individuals with different definitions on an alternative basis.' Translation: Mushtarak is a word which includes, on an alternative basis, words whose definitions differ and are distinct from each other. Benefits and conditions noted in the definition: Individuals: This excludes the specific because it doesn't have individuals. Different definitions: This excludes the general because its individuals all agree in terms of definitions. On an alternative basis: Several benefits are derived from this condition, as listed: a) If this condition is not precautionary, it is merely for stating reality. b) If it is considered precautionary, then it is to avoid Imam Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i's statement of 'on a comprehensive basis', that according to the Shafi'is, multiple meanings of a mushtarak can be intended simultaneously; or it is to avoid what some usul scholars hold regarding 'entity' that it is a conceptual mushtarak in terms of its meanings existing in the present, and in terms of individuals having different realities it is superior to verbal mushtarak, but its relation to its individuals is on a comprehensive basis, so the condition of 'on an alternative basis' avoids this. Example of mushtarak: The example of mushtarak is the word 'quru', as it is between menstruation and purity, being mushtarak.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the usul term 'mushtarak' (homonym). The author defines it as a word that encompasses individuals (i.e., specific instances or meanings) that have different definitions (i.e., distinct meanings), and these meanings are taken one at a time (on an alternative basis), not simultaneously. The example given is the word 'quru', which means both menstruation (hayd) and purity (tahr) in Arabic. The definition is accompanied by a detailed explanation of its conditions: the word must have individuals (thus excluding 'khas' which has no individuals), the individuals must have different definitions (thus excluding 'amm' whose individuals share a common definition), and the relation to those meanings must be alternative (thus excluding the view of Imam Shafi'i who allows multiple meanings to be intended at once, and excluding conceptual mushtarak which relates to its individuals comprehensively). This is a standard Hanafi usul definition emphasizing that a mushtarak word can only indicate one of its meanings at a time, requiring qarina (contextual evidence) to determine which is intended.

Key Terms
مشترک (mushtarak): A word that encompasses several individuals (meanings) with different definitions, used on an alternative basis (i.e., only one meaning at a time).
علی سبیل البدل (ala sabil al-badal): On an alternative basis, meaning that among the multiple meanings, only one is intended when the word is used, not all at once.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Mushtarak is a word with multiple distinct meanings used alternatively; it requires qarina to resolve which meaning is intended.

Dispute over 'Umum Mushtarak' (Generalization of a Homonym)
Presents Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on whether a homonym can carry multiple intended meanings simultaneously.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on umum mushtarak, identify the proof each school uses, and apply the Hanafi response to resolve apparent evidence from Quran 33:56.

Core Idea
A homonym cannot simultaneously intend all its meanings — that would require both intending and not intending each meaning.

When a word has multiple established meanings, can a single utterance intend all of them at once? This is the question of 'umum mushtarak'. The Hanafi school says no, while the Shafi'i school says yes. This lesson unpacks both positions, their proofs, and the Hanafi refutation — a foundational dispute that affects how ambiguous legal texts are interpreted.

Guided Path
1
State the issue of umum mushtarak
Clearly define what is being debated: whether a mushtarak (homonym) can simultaneously indicate all its possible meanings in one utterance.
The debate is about simultaneous intention, not about the existence of multiple dictionary meanings.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and its rational proof
State the Hanafi ruling: umum mushtarak is invalid. Explain the rational argument: each word is placed for a specific meaning; intending multiple leads to contradiction or combining haqiqa and majaz.
The Hanafi proof is purely rational (aqli), not based on a revealed text.
3
Identify the Shafi'i position and its Quranic proof
State the Shafi'i ruling: umum mushtarak is permissible. Present their proof from Quran 33:56, where 'salat' takes different meanings based on the subject (Allah: mercy, angels: istighfar, believers: supplication).
The Shafi'i dalil is naqli (Quran).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof
Show how Hanafis reinterpret the verse: the common meaning is 'ihtimam bi shanihi' (care for his dignity), which manifests differently per subject, avoiding the need for multiple intended meanings.
This response preserves the singularity of intended meaning while acknowledging contextual variation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Explain the real impact: when a legal text contains a mushtarak, Hanafis restrict it to one meaning per context, while Shafi'is may derive multiple rulings simultaneously.
This affects the scope of obligations and permissions derived from ambiguous texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وحكمه التوقف فيه بشرط التأمل ليترجح بعض وجوده للعمل به
مشترک کا حکم مشترک کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اس کے متعدد معانی کوملحوظ رکھ کر مقام کی نسبت سے سیاق وسباق پر غور و فکر کے ذریعے سے اس کے کسی ایک معنی کو ترجیح دی جائیگی تا کہ اس پر عمل کیا جا سکے۔
مثال کے ذریعے وضاحت : قردہ ایک لفظ مشترک ہے ہم نے اس لفظ میں کئی طریقوں سے غور و فکر کیا مثلاً
(1) اس لفظ کی صفت لفظ ثلاثہ ہے اور خاص ہے۔
(۲) دوسرا یہ ہے کہ اقل جمع تین ہے۔
(۳) تیسرا یہ ہے کہ جمع اور انتقال کے معنی میں ہے۔
اب اگر قروہ سے مرد دوم ( خون حیض) ہو تو ظاہر ہے کہ دم میں جمع اورمنتقل ہونے والی صفت پائی جاتی ہے۔
پس اگر اس سے مراد ایام دم ہوں تو ایام حیض اجتماع اور انتقال کا محل ہیں۔ بخلاف طہر کے کہ نہ وہ جمع ہونے والا ہے اور نہ منتقل ہونے والا اور نہ وہ جمع ہونے اور انتقال کا محل ہے ۔
قوله : ولا عموم له
عموم مشترک جائز ہے یا نہیں؟
ہمارے نزدیک مشترک میں عموم نہیں ہوتا لہذا ایک لفظ سے ایک ہی وقت میں اسکے دو یا دو سے زائد معانی مراد لینا جائز نہیں ہے۔
امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ کا مذاب امام شافعی کے نزدیک عموم مشترک جائز ہے یعنی ایک ہی وقت میں مشترک کے متعدد معانی مراد لئے جاسکتے ہیں ۔
امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ کی دلیل وہ قرآن مجید کی اس آیت سے استدلال کرتے ہیں ان الله وملا ئكته يصلون على النبي يا أيها الذين آمنو اصلوا عليه وسلموا تسليماً طرز استدلال اس آیت میں ایک لفظ مشترک ( صلوۃ ) استعمال ہوا ہے جس کی نسبت اللہ تعالیٰ ، فرشتوں اور بندگان خدا کی طرف کی گئی ہے اور ظاہر ہے کہ جب (صلوۃ) کی نسبت اللہ تعالیٰ کی طرف کی جائے تو مراد رحمت کاملہ ہوتی ہے اسی لفظ کی نسبت جب فرشتوں کی طرف کی جاتی ہے تو دعا و استغفار مراد ہوتی ہے اور جب بندوں کی طرف کی جائے تو فقط دعاء مراد ہوتی ہے لہذا معلوم ہوا کہ ایک ہی لفظ مشترک ( صلوۃ) سے اس کے تین معانی ایک ہی آیت میں مراد لئے گئے ہیں اس کا نام عموم مشترک ہے۔
شوافع کی دلیل کا جواب اس آیت کی غرض یہ ہے کہ مومنوں پر اللہ تعالیٰ کی اور فرشتوں کی اقتداء واجب ہے اور یہ غرض اسی وقت حاصل ہو سکتی ہے جب کہ ہم ایک عام معنی مراد لیں جو کہ سب کو شامل ہو جائے اور وہ معنی ہے اعتناء بشانہ۔ اب مطلب یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالٰی اور فرشتے حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کی شان کا اہتمام کرتے ہیں لہذا ایمان والوں تم بھی حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کی شان کا اہتمام کرو ۔ اور ظاہر ہے کہ یہ اہتمام باری تعالٰی عزاسمہ کی طرف سے رحمت، فر شتوں کی طرف سے استغفار اور مومنوں کی طرف سے دعا ہے
احناف کی دلیل ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ واضع نے ہر لفظ کو ایک معنی خاص کے لئے وضع کیا ہے اور دوسرے واضع نے اس لفظ کو دوسرے معنی کے لئے وضع کیا ہے۔ اس طرح کہ اس سے دوسرا معنی مراد لینا جائز نہیں ہے۔ تو اگر عموم مشترک جائز ہو یا زیادہ معانی مراد لئے جائیں گے تو لازم آئیگا کہ ہر معنی مراد بھی ہو اور غیر مراد بھی ہو تو یہ متضاد ین کا اجتماع ہے جو جائز نہیں۔ اور اگر آپ یہ کہتے ہیں کہ معنی متعدد میں سے ایک کو مراد لیتے ہیں موضوع ہونے کی وجہ سے اور دوسرے کو مناسبت کی وجہ سے تو حقیقت اور مجاز کو جمع کرنا لازم آئیگا اور یہ احناف کے ہاں تو مطلقا باطل
His statement: And its ruling is suspension (tawaqquf) on condition of reflection (ta'ammul) to preponderate some of its aspects for acting upon it. The ruling of a homonym (mushtarak) is that its multiple meanings are kept in view, then through consideration of context and situation, one of its meanings is given preponderance so that it can be acted upon. Clarification by example: 'Qur'ah' is a homonym; we reflect on it in several ways: (1) the word's characteristic is that it is a triliteral and specific, (2) the minimum plural is three, (3) it carries the meaning of gathering and transference. So if the intended meaning of 'qur'ah' is menses blood, then blood possesses the quality of gathering and transference. Thus if the intended meaning is the days of blood, then the days of menses are the locus of gathering and transference, unlike the days of purity which are not. His statement: 'And there is no generality ('umum) for it.' Is the generality of a homonym permissible? According to us (Hanafis), there is no generality in a homonym, so it is not permissible to intend two or more meanings from one word at the same time. Imam al-Shafi'i's school: According to him, the generality of a homonym is permissible, i.e., multiple meanings of a homonym can be intended at the same time. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof: He argues from the Qur'anic verse: 'Indeed, Allah and His angels send blessings upon the Prophet. O you who have believed, send blessings upon him and greet him with peace.' The style of reasoning: In this verse, a homonymic word 'salat' is used, attributed to Allah, the angels, and the servants of Allah. When attributed to Allah, it means complete mercy; when to angels, it means supplication and seeking forgiveness; when to servants, it means mere supplication. Thus it is known that from one homonymic word 'salat', three meanings are intended in one verse; this is called 'umum mushtarak'. Response to the Shafi'i proof: The purpose of this verse is that it is obligatory for the believers to follow Allah and the angels, and this purpose can only be achieved when we take a common meaning that encompasses all, which is 'ihtimam bi-shanihi' (care for his dignity). The meaning is that Allah and the angels care for the dignity of the Prophet, so O believers, you too should care for the Prophet's dignity. This care from Allah is mercy, from angels is seeking forgiveness, and from believers is supplication. The Hanafi proof: Our proof is that each word was placed (wad') for a specific meaning by the first coiners, and another coiner placed it for another meaning, such that intending another meaning from it is not permissible. So if 'umum mushtarak' were permissible, or more meanings intended, then it would necessitate that each meaning is both intended and not intended, which is a combination of contradictories, which is not permissible. And if you claim that one meaning is intended as the primary (haqiqa) and the other by suitability (munasaba), then it would necessitate combining haqiqa and majaz, which is absolutely invalid according to the Hanafis.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a classic usul al-fiqh dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the issue of 'umum mushtarak' — the permissibility of using a homonym (mushtarak) to simultaneously indicate multiple distinct meanings. The text first establishes the ruling for a mushtarak: when a word has multiple possible meanings, one must reflect on the context to determine which single meaning is intended for action. This is the Hanafi default position: only one meaning is operative at a time. The author then quotes the matn's statement 'Wa la umum lahu' (And there is no generality for it), confirming that a homonym does not have the capacity for simultaneous multiple meanings. The Shafi'i position is presented next: Imam al-Shafi'i considers 'umum mushtarak' permissible, citing the verse on salat (Quran 33:56) where the word 'salat' attributed to Allah means mercy, to angels means istighfar, and to believers means supplication. Thus a single word yields three distinct meanings in one context. The Hanafi response reinterprets the verse: the common meaning is 'ihtimam bi shanihi' (care for his dignity) — a single overarching meaning that manifests differently depending on the subject. This avoids the need for multiple simultaneous intended meanings. Finally, the Hanafi proof is given: if 'umum mushtarak' were allowed, a single utterance would entail both intending and not intending each meaning simultaneously — a contradiction. Alternatively, if one meaning is taken as haqiqa and another as majaz, this combines literal and figurative usage which Hanafis deem invalid. This dispute is foundational because it affects how legal rulings are derived from ambiguous (mutashabih) texts. The Hanafi insistence on singularity of intended meaning protects against unwarranted extensions of a text's applicability.

Key Terms
مشترک (mushtarak): A word (lafz) that has been placed (wad') for multiple distinct meanings equally, such that no single meaning is immediately predominant without context.
عموم مشترک (umum mushtarak): The doctrine that a homonym (mushtarak) can simultaneously indicate all its possible meanings in a single utterance, such that each meaning is intended.
حقیقت (haqiqa): The literal, original meaning for which the word was coined (wad').
مجاز (majaz): A metaphorical or secondary meaning that is not the original coinage but is used due to a relation (munasaba).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on 'umum mushtarak'?
A) Permissible, as in the word 'salat' in the Quran.
B) Not permissible, because it leads to contradiction or combining haqiqa and majaz.
C) Permissible only if the meanings are all metaphorical.
D) Not permissible if the word is from the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects umum mushtarak because a single utterance cannot coherently intend multiple distinct meanings without contradiction.

The Dispute over Umum Mushtarak: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Presenting Shafi'i and Hanafi arguments on umum mushtarak

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument against umum mushtarak and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the Hanafi principle that combining haqiqah and majaz is invalid.

Core Idea
A homonym cannot convey multiple meanings at once — that would combine literal and metaphorical usage, which Hanafis reject absolutely.

This lesson takes you into a foundational usul dispute: Can a word with multiple meanings (mushtarak) be used for all its meanings in a single context? The Shafi'is argue yes, citing a key Quranic verse. The Hanafis respond with a logical and linguistic argument that has shaped how we interpret ambiguous texts. By the end, you'll see why Hanafis insist on a single meaning per context.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Identify how Imam al-Shafi'i uses the Quranic verse on salah to argue that a homonym can carry multiple meanings at once. Note the three different meanings attributed to Allah, angels, and believers.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof
Understand the Hanafi reinterpretation: the verse intends a general meaning (i'tina' bi shanih) that manifests differently for each subject. This avoids the need for multiple distinct meanings from one word.
3
Examine the Hanafi counter-proof
Follow the Hanafi logical argument: if a word is used for multiple meanings simultaneously, it either creates a contradiction (each meaning both intended and not intended) or combines literal and metaphorical usage (haqiqah and majaz), which Hanafis hold as absolutely invalid.
4
Consider the Shafi'i exception
Note that even Shafi'is only allow combining literal and metaphorical when there is a clear connection (al-i'tiq) and the meanings are not contradictory. This narrows the practical disagreement.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in interpreting ambiguous texts? Hanafis seek a single meaning via context; Shafi'is may allow multiple valid interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
میں مشترک کے متعدد معانی مراد لئے جاسکتے ہیں ۔
امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ کی دلیل وہ قرآن مجید کی اس آیت سے استدلال کرتے ہیں ان الله وملا ئكته يصلون على النبي يا أيها الذين آمنو اصلوا عليه وسلموا تسليماً طرز استدلال اس آیت میں ایک لفظ مشترک ( صلوۃ ) استعمال ہوا ہے جس کی نسبت اللہ تعالیٰ ، فرشتوں اور بندگان خدا کی طرف کی گئی ہے اور ظاہر ہے کہ جب (صلوۃ) کی نسبت اللہ تعالیٰ کی طرف کی جائے تو مراد رحمت کاملہ ہوتی ہے اسی لفظ کی نسبت جب فرشتوں کی طرف کی جاتی ہے تو دعا و استغفار مراد ہوتی ہے اور جب بندوں کی طرف کی جائے تو فقط دعاء مراد ہوتی ہے لہذا معلوم ہوا کہ ایک ہی لفظ مشترک ( صلوۃ) سے اس کے تین معانی ایک ہی آیت میں مراد لئے گئے ہیں اس کا نام عموم مشترک ہے۔
شوافع کی دلیل کا جواب اس آیت کی غرض یہ ہے کہ مومنوں پر اللہ تعالیٰ کی اور فرشتوں کی اقتداء واجب ہے اور یہ غرض اسی وقت حاصل ہو سکتی ہے جب کہ ہم ایک عام معنی مراد لیں جو کہ سب کو شامل ہو جائے اور وہ معنی ہے اعتناء بشانہ۔ اب مطلب یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالٰی اور فرشتے حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کی شان کا اہتمام کرتے ہیں لہذا ایمان والوں تم بھی حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کی شان کا اہتمام کرو ۔ اور ظاہر ہے کہ یہ اہتمام باری تعالٰی عزاسمہ کی طرف سے رحمت، فر شتوں کی طرف سے استغفار اور مومنوں کی طرف سے دعا ہے
احناف کی دلیل ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ واضع نے ہر لفظ کو ایک معنی خاص کے لئے وضع کیا ہے اور دوسرے واضع نے اس لفظ کو دوسرے معنی کے لئے وضع کیا ہے۔ اس طرح کہ اس سے دوسرا معنی مراد لینا جائز نہیں ہے۔ تو اگر عموم مشترک جائز ہو یا زیادہ معانی مراد لئے جائیں گے تو لازم آئیگا کہ ہر معنی مراد بھی ہو اور غیر مراد بھی ہو تو یہ متضاد ین کا اجتماع ہے جو جائز نہیں۔ اور اگر آپ یہ کہتے ہیں کہ معنی متعدد میں سے ایک کو مراد لیتے ہیں موضوع ہونے کی وجہ سے اور دوسرے کو مناسبت کی وجہ سے تو حقیقت اور مجاز کو جمع کرنا لازم آئیگا اور یہ احناف کے ہاں تو مطلقا باطل ہے۔ جبکہ شوافع کے ہاں اس صورت میں جائز ہے کہ معنی مجازی اور معنی موضوع لہ کے درمیان مجاز کے چومیں علائق میں سے کوئی علاقہ مان لیا جائے اور یہ معنی متعددہ باہم متضاد نہ ہوں ۔ اگر باہم متضاد ہوں تو ان کے ہاں بھی جمع بین الحقيقة والجاز باطل ہے۔
اسی طرح من حیث المجموع مراد لینا بھی بالاتفاق جائز نہیں ہے پس عموم مشترک کا
بطلان ثابت ہو گیا لہذا ایک وقت میں لفظ مشترک سے ایک ہی معنی مراد لیا جائے گا۔
That it is possible to take multiple meanings from a homonym. The evidence of Imam Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) is that he deduces from this verse of the Holy Quran: 'Indeed Allah and His angels send blessings (yusallun) upon the Prophet. O you who have believed, send blessings upon him and greet him with peace.' The method of deduction: In this verse, one homonym (salah) is used, and it is attributed to Allah Almighty, the angels, and the servants of God. It is obvious that when salah is attributed to Allah Almighty, it means complete mercy. When this word is attributed to the angels, it means supplication and seeking forgiveness. When it is attributed to the servants, it means only supplication. Therefore, it is known that from the same homonym (salah) its three meanings are intended in one verse. This is called umum mushtarak. 
Response to the Shafi'i evidence: The purpose of this verse is that it is obligatory for the believers to follow Allah Almighty and the angels. This purpose can only be achieved if we take a general meaning that includes all, and that meaning is 'attention to his status' (i'tina' bi shanihi). Now the meaning is that Allah Almighty and the angels pay attention to the status of the Prophet (peace be upon him), so O believers, you too pay attention to the status of the Prophet (peace be upon him). And it is obvious that this attention from Allah Almighty is mercy, from the angels is seeking forgiveness, and from the believers is supplication.
Our evidence (the Hanafis) is that the language-poser placed each word for a specific meaning, and another language-poser placed this word for another meaning, such that taking another meaning from it is not permissible. So if umum mushtarak were permissible, or multiple meanings were intended, it would necessitate that each meaning is both intended and not intended — this is the conjunction of opposites, which is not permissible. And if you say that one of the multiple meanings is intended due to being the original meaning, and another due to suitability, then it would necessitate combining the literal and metaphorical, which is absolutely invalid according to the Hanafis. While according to the Shafi'is, it is permissible in this case if there is some connection from the types of metaphorical connections between the metaphorical meaning and the original meaning, and these multiple meanings are not mutually contradictory. If they are contradictory, then combining literal and metaphorical is also invalid according to them. 
Similarly, taking the meaning as a whole (min hayth al-majmu') is also unanimously impermissible. Thus, the invalidity of umum mushtarak is established. Therefore, at one time only one meaning can be intended from a homonym.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents a usul al-fiqh dispute between the Shafi'is and Hanafis regarding the concept of 'umum mushtarak' — using a homonym to convey multiple meanings simultaneously in one context. The Shafi'is argue that this is possible, citing the Quranic verse about salah (blessings) where the same word refers to different meanings when attributed to Allah, angels, and believers. The Hanafis respond by reinterpreting the verse: they argue that the intended meaning is a general sense of 'attention to status' (i'tina' bi shanih), which then manifests differently for each subject. The Hanafis then present their own proof: each word is originally placed for one specific meaning; using it for multiple meanings would entail contradiction (both intended and not intended) or combining literal and metaphorical usage (haqiqah and majaz), which they reject absolutely. They note that even the Shafi'is only allow combining literal and metaphorical when there is a clear connection and the meanings are not contradictory. The passage concludes that umum mushtarak is invalid, and a homonym can only convey one meaning at a time.

Key Terms
مشترک (mushtarak): A word that has been placed for multiple distinct meanings (e.g., 'salat' meaning mercy, supplication, or prayer).
عموم مشترک (umum mushtarak): The view that a homonym can be used to convey all its meanings simultaneously in one instance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument, why is combining literal and metaphorical usage (haqiqah and majaz) for a homonym invalid?
A) It is impossible for a word to have both a literal and a metaphorical meaning.
B) It leads to a logical contradiction: the same word would be both intended and not intended for each meaning.
C) The Shafi'is do not allow it either.
D) The Quran proves it is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A homonym can only convey one meaning per context; umum mushtarak is invalid because it would require combining literal and metaphorical usage, which Hanafis reject absolutely.

Mu'awwal: The Interpreted Meaning and Its Place in Nass
Defining mu'awwal and defending its classification within nass.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mu'awwal and explain why the Hanafis count it as a category of nass despite its reliance on ta'wil.

Core Idea
Mu'awwal is a text meaning reached by preponderant ijtihad—yet the ruling is attributed to the text, not the interpreter.

In this lesson, we dissect the concept of mu'awwal (interpreted meaning) as defined by the Hanafi school of usul al-fiqh. We will see how a homonym (mushtarak) with multiple possible meanings becomes assigned a preponderant meaning through the mujtahid's dominant opinion (ghalabat al-zann). We will then confront a key objection: if mu'awwal is the product of ijtihad, why is it considered part of the text (nass)? The answer reveals the Hanafi stance on the authority of interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its core definition
The passage defines mu'awwal: it is the meaning that becomes preponderant from a homonym by dominant opinion. Note that before preponderance, the word was mushtarak (ambiguous).
2
Understand the mechanism of ta'wil
What brings about mu'awwal? The act of ta'wil, which uses various tools (khabar wahid, qiyas, deliberation, context) to give preponderance to one meaning. Recognize that ta'wil is a human (mujtahid) act.
3
Identify the objection
The objection: if mu'awwal comes from ta'wil (human reasoning), how can it be counted among the categories of nass (divine text)? This is a methodological challenge.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response: after ta'wil, the ruling is attributed to the nass itself, not to the mujtahid's reasoning. The interpretation reveals what the text actually intends, so mu'awwal is considered part of the textual categories.
The Hanafi principle: even interpreted meanings are authoritative as text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وأما المؤول فما ترجح من المشترك بعض وجوهه
بغالب الرأى .
(۱) موول کی تعریف
مؤول وہ ایک معنی ہے جو اس وقت حاصل ہوتا ہے جب مشترک کے بعض وجوہ یعنی کسی ایک معنی کو غالب رائے کے ساتھ ترجیح دے دی جائے گویا کہ ترجیح سے قبل وہ مشترک تھا اور کسی ایک معنی کو تر نیج دینے کے بعد وہ موال بن گیا۔
لکھتے ظن غالب میں عموم ہے جس کی کئی صورتیں ہیں کبھی تو ظن غالب خبر واحد کے ذریعہ حاصل ہوگا کبھی قیاس کے ذریعہ اور کبھی لفظ مشترک میں شدید غور وفکر اور تامل کے ذریعہ اور کبھی جس کلام میں لفظ مشترک وارد ہوا ہے اس کے سیاق و سباق میں تامل کے ذریعہ بہر حال کسی بھی ذریعہ سے مشترک کے وجوہ متعددہ میں سے کسی ایک کو ترجیح دینا تاویل ہے اور وہ معنی جس کو ترجیح
دی گئی موول ہے اعتراض اعتراض یہ ہوتا ہے کہ موکول تو تاویل کے ذریعے سے حاصل ہوتا ہے تو آپ نے اس کو نظم کے اقسام میں کیوں شمار کیا۔
جواب: ہمیں اس سے انکار نہیں کہ موول تاویل یعنی فعل مجتہد سے حاصل ہوتا ہے مگر چونکہ تاویل کے بعد حکم کی نسبت نظم ہی کی طرف ہوتی ہے۔ اس لئے ہم نے اس کو نظم کے اقسام میں شمار
کیا۔
His statement: 'As for mu'awwal, it is that which is preponderant from some aspects of the homonym by dominant opinion.' 
(1) Definition of mu'awwal: Mu'awwal is that meaning which is obtained when some aspects of the homonym (i.e., one of the meanings) is given preponderance by dominant opinion. As if before the preponderance it was mushtarak, and after giving preponderance to one meaning it becomes mu'awwal. 
They write: 'Dominant opinion (ghalabat al-zann) is general and has several forms. Sometimes dominant opinion is obtained through khabar wahid, sometimes through qiyas, sometimes through intense deliberation and contemplation on the homonym, and sometimes through contemplation of the context (siyaq wa sabaq) of the speech in which the homonym occurs. In any case, giving preponderance to one of the multiple aspects of the homonym through any means is ta'wil, and that meaning which is given preponderance is mu'awwal.' 
Objection: The objection is that mu'awwal is obtained through ta'wil, so why have you counted it among the categories of nass (text)? 
Answer: We do not deny that mu'awwal is obtained through ta'wil, i.e., the act of the mujtahid. However, since after ta'wil, the ruling is attributed to the nass (text) itself, we have counted it among the categories of nass.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'mu'awwal' — the interpreted meaning. It explains that mu'awwal is not the literal, immediate meaning of a homonym, but rather the meaning that becomes preponderant after the mujtahid applies his dominant opinion (ghalabat al-zann) through various tools like khabar wahid, qiyas, or contextual analysis. The passage then addresses an important objection: if mu'awwal is the result of ijtihad (ta'wil), why is it considered a category of nass (the text) rather than a product of human reasoning? The answer is that although the process of preferring one meaning is the mujtahid's act, the resulting ruling is attributed to the text itself because the interpretation reveals what the text actually intends. Thus, mu'awwal holds a position within the hierarchy of textual clarity, being weaker than the literal (zahir) but still considered part of the nass.

Key Terms
موول (mu'awwal): A meaning of a homonym that is given preponderance over other possible meanings through dominant opinion (ghalabat al-zann).
تأویل (ta'wil): The act of a mujtahid giving preponderance to one possible meaning of a homonym over others, using tools like khabar wahid, qiyas, or context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is mu'awwal defined according to this passage?
A) It is the literal meaning of a word without any interpretation.
B) It is a meaning of a homonym that is given preponderance through dominant opinion.
C) It is a metaphorical meaning that cannot be traced back to the text.
D) It is a unanimous meaning agreed upon by all scholars.

Key Takeaway
Mu'awwal is a meaning of a homonym made preponderant by ijtihad, yet the Hanafi school attributes the resulting ruling to the text, not to the interpreter.

Definition and Ruling of Zahir
Defines zahir and states its ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define zahir according to the Hanafi school and identify its ruling and the absence of a condition of speaker's intent.

Core Idea
Zahir is a text whose meaning is clear from its wording; acting on it is obligatory and definitive, but it is open to specification.

The Hanafi classification of texts by clarity begins with zahir, the lowest level of apparent meaning. This lesson unpacks its definition, ruling, and key features as presented in the source. Understanding zahir is foundational for distinguishing higher levels of textual clarity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of zahir
Locate the statement: 'Zahir is a name for speech whose intended meaning is apparent to the listener from its wording.' Note that the clarity comes solely from the wording, not from external context.
The Arabic phrase 'li-sami'in bi-sighathihi' emphasizes the self-sufficiency of the wording.
2
Understand the ruling of zahir
The text states: 'Its ruling is the obligation to act upon what has become apparent from it with certainty and yaqeen.' This means the ruling derived from a zahir text is binding definitively (qat'i).
This does not mean the meaning itself is beyond ta'wil; it means the obligation to act is certain until a valid specification appears.
3
Recognize the condition (or lack thereof) of speaker's intent
The author clarifies that for zahir, neither the presence nor absence of speaker's intent (sawq al-kalam) is a condition. This distinguishes it from nass, which requires sawq.
Some scholars mistakenly thought zahir requires absence of sawq; the correct view is that it is neutral.
4
Apply to the example
The verse 'Allah has permitted trade and forbidden usury' is given as an example. Analyze why it is zahir: the wording directly indicates permissibility of trade and prohibition of riba without needing additional intent.
This verse is zahir in its rulings, but it is subject to possible specification (e.g., what constitutes trade).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وأما الظاهر فاسم لكلام ظهر المراد به للسامع بصيغته وحكمه وجوب العمل بالذي ظهر منه على سبيل القطع واليقين . یہاں سے مصنف تقسیم ثانی کو شروع فرماتے ہیں اس تقسیم کے کلی چار افراد باعتبار ظہور معنی کے اور چار افراد بات بار شفائے معنی کے ہیں جو مندرجہ ذیل ہیں :
نص کی ظہور معنی کے اعتبار سے تقسیم (۱) ظاہر (۲) نص (۳) مفر (۴) محکم
قسم اول ظاہر کی تعریف
ظاہر اس کلام کا نام ہے جس کی مراد صرف اس کے صیغے سے سامع کے سامنے ظاہر ہو۔
ظاہر کی مثال: احل الله البيع وحرم الربوا۔ یہ آیت بیع کی حلت اور ربوا کی حرمت میں ظاہر ہے۔
ظاہر کا حکم: اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اس کی مراد پر عمل کرنا واجب ہے یقینی طور پر۔
قوله : وأما النص فما ازداد وضوحاً على الظاهر لمعنى من المتكلم لا فى نفس الصيغة وحكمه وجوب العمل بما وضح على احتمال التاويل وهو فى حيز المجاز.
قسم ثانی نص کی تعریف:
نص وہ کلام ہے جس کو اسی مقصد کے لئے متکلم لیکر آیا ہو۔ ماتن رحمہ اللہ نے اس کی تعریف "ما سيق الكلام لاجلہ" نہیں فرمائی جس طرح صاحب اصول الشاشی اور دیگر علمائے اصول نے کی ہے بلکہ وہ یہ تعریف کرتے ہیں:
نص وہ ہے جس میں ظاہر سے زیادہ ظاہریت پائی جاتی ہو متکلم کی طرف سے کسی معنی کی وجہ سے اس طرح کہ متکلم نے اس نظم کو اس معنی کے لئے لایا ہو گا۔
مشہور یہ ہے کہ نص میں سوق کلام شرط ہے اور ظاہر میں عدم سوق شرط ہے لیکن صحیح یہ ہے کہ نص میں تو سوق شرط ہے لیکن ظاہر میں سوق یا عدم سوق میں سے کچھ بھی شرط نہیں ہے۔
نص کی مثال: اللہ تعالی نے قرآن مجید میں تعدد ازواج سے متعلق ارشاد فرمایا: فانكحوا ما
His saying: 'As for zahir, it is a name for speech whose intended meaning is apparent to the listener from its wording, and its ruling is the obligation to act upon what has become apparent from it with certainty and yaqeen.' From here, the author begins the second division. This division has four types in terms of clarity of meaning and four types in terms of ambiguity of meaning, which are as follows:
Division of nass according to clarity of meaning: (1) Zahir (2) Nass (3) Mufassar? (4) Muhkam.
First type: Definition of Zahir: Zahir is the name of that speech whose intended meaning is apparent to the listener solely from its wording.
Example of Zahir: 'Allah has permitted trade and forbidden usury.' This verse is zahir in the permissibility of trade and prohibition of usury.
Ruling of Zahir: Its ruling is that acting upon its meaning is obligatory, definitively.
His saying: 'As for nass, it is that which has increased in clarity over zahir due to a meaning from the speaker, not in the wording itself, and its ruling is the obligation to act upon what is clear, with the possibility of ta'wil, and it is in the realm of majaz.'
Second type: Definition of Nass: Nass is that speech which the speaker has brought for that very purpose. The author (matan) did not define it as 'that for which the speech was brought' as did the author of Usul al-Shashi and other usul scholars, but rather he defines it thus: Nass is that in which there is greater apparentness than zahir, due to a meaning from the speaker, such that the speaker brought this expression for that meaning. The well-known view is that in nass, the speaker's intent (sawq al-kalam) is a condition, and in zahir, the absence of such intent is a condition, but the correct view is that in nass, intent is a condition, but in zahir, neither intent nor its absence is a condition.
Example of Nass: Allah Ta'ala said in the Quran regarding polygyny: 'Then marry what...'
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage begins the second major division of texts according to clarity of meaning (zuhur al-ma'na). The author lists four types: Zahir, Nass, Mufassar (likely 'Mufassar' is intended, though written as 'Mufr'), and Muhkam. The focus here is on the first two. Zahir is a text whose meaning is clear from its wording, but it may still be open to ta'wil or specification. Its ruling is that acting upon it is obligatory and definitive (qat'i) in terms of obligation, but the meaning itself is not beyond possibility of interpretation. The example given is the verse about trade and usury: the apparent meaning is that trade is halal and usury is haram. Nass, on the other hand, is clearer than zahir because the speaker's intended meaning is explicitly the one for which the text was brought. It is still subject to ta'wil (interpretation) and is in the realm of majaz (figurative speech). The author notes a difference among scholars regarding the condition of 'sawq al-kalam' (speaker's intent): some require it for nass but not for zahir, but the correct view is that only nass requires it. This is a typical Hanafi textual hierarchy: nass is stronger than zahir. The example of nass is cut off, but it likely refers to a verse where the intended meaning is clear from context, such as 'fankihu ma taba lakum' (marry what is good for you).

Key Terms
ظاہر (zahir): A text whose meaning is apparent from its wording to the listener, without needing external intent; it is acted upon definitively but open to ta'wil.
صیغہ (sighah): The specific wording or expression of the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about zahir?
A) The speaker's intent (sawq) is a required condition.
B) Its ruling is that acting upon it is recommended, not obligatory.
C) It is a text whose meaning is clear only from the wording.
D) It cannot be subject to ta'wil.

Key Takeaway
Zahir is the first level of clarity: its meaning is apparent from wording, and acting on it is obligatory with certainty, but it can still be specified.

Definition of Nass and the Condition of Sawq al-Kalam
Defines nass and discusses the condition of sawq al-kalam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define nass according to the Hanafi school, explain why it is clearer than zahir, and evaluate the dispute over the condition of sawq al-kalam.

Core Idea
Nass is clearer than zahir because the speaker intended that meaning; it requires sawq al-kalam, unlike zahir.

Moving up the clarity hierarchy, nass is a text whose intended meaning is explicitly what the speaker brought the speech for. This lesson distinguishes it from zahir and explores the intra-madhhab dispute about the condition of speaker's intent. Mastering this distinction is key to understanding how Hanafi jurists prioritize texts in istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of nass
The author defines nass as 'that which has increased clarity over zahir due to a meaning from the speaker, not in the wording itself.' Note that the increase comes from the speaker's purpose, not the wording.
Contrast with the definition from Usul al-Shashi: 'ma siqa al-kalam li ajlihi' (that for which the speech was brought). The author's definition is more precise.
2
Understand the condition of sawq al-kalam
The passage states: 'The well-known view is that in nass, the speaker's intent (sawq al-kalam) is a condition, and in zahir, the absence of such intent is a condition, but the correct view is that in nass, intent is a condition, but in zahir, neither intent nor its absence is a condition.'
This means for nass, you must know that the speaker intended that specific meaning; for zahir, it doesn't matter.
3
Analyze the dispute
Some scholars (like author of Usul al-Shashi) define nass as 'that for which the speech was brought,' which implicitly includes intent. The author's definition explicitly adds 'increase in clarity over zahir' and considers sawq a condition. Both agree on the need for intent, but the author emphasizes the comparative clarity.
The dispute is subtle: the core concept is the same, but the wording affects how one identifies a nass text.
4
Apply the concept of ta'wil
The passage notes that nass is still in the realm of majaz and open to ta'wil, but its clarity makes ta'wil more difficult than for zahir. This affects the strength of the ruling derived.
Ta'wil here means interpretation that departs from the apparent meaning; it is possible but heavily restricted.
5
Consider the example (cut off)
The text begins an example with 'fankihu ma taba lakum min al-nisa'' (marry what is good for you). This verse is nass because it is specifically about marriage options; the intended meaning is clear from the context of polygyny.
The full example would show how this text is clearer than a general verse.

This passage begins the second major division of texts according to clarity of meaning (zuhur al-ma'na). Key Terms
نص (nass): A text clearer than zahir due to the speaker's intended meaning; it is still in the realm of majaz and open to ta'wil, but stronger than zahir.
سوق کلام (sawq al-kalam): The speaker's intent or purpose in uttering the speech; a condition for nass.
تأویل (ta'wil): Interpretation that departs from the apparent meaning; possible for both zahir and nass but more restricted for nass.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative speech; nass is said to be in the realm of majaz because it can be interpreted non-literally, though that is unlikely.

Al-Mushkil: Definition and Resolution through Contextual Analysis
Defining the second ambiguous type in usul: al-mushkil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-mushkil, distinguish it from other ambiguous types, and apply the Hanafi method of resolving textual ambiguity using contextual clues.

Core Idea
Al-mushkil is text whose ambiguity arises from resemblance to other texts, requiring reflection after seeking to determine the intended meaning.

In Hanafi usul, texts are classified by their clarity. Al-mushkil is the second of the ambiguous categories, sitting between khafi (obscure) and mutashabih (unclear). Unlike khafi, where ambiguity stems from an external factor, mushkil's ambiguity is intrinsic due to resemblance to other texts. This lesson unpacks its definition, ruling, and the method of resolution using the Quranic verse 'fa'atu harthakum anna shi'tum' (2:223) as a primary example.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the Definition of Mushkil
Understand that mushkil is a text that becomes ambiguous because it resembles other texts or contexts, requiring reflection (ta'ammul) after seeking (talab) to arrive at the intended meaning. The author compares it to a person who changes his appearance – mere searching is not enough; careful reflection is needed.
The term 'ishkāl' refers to the ambiguity itself.
2
Identify the Ruling of Mushkil
The ruling is that one must first believe that the intended meaning of the text is true (since it is from Allah or the Prophet), then engage in ijtihad through reflection to clarify it. This distinguishes mushkil from texts that are clear (zahir/nass) where no reflection is needed.
The belief in truth is a prerequisite – we do not question the correctness of the revelation.
3
Analyze the 'Anna' Example: Two Possible Meanings
In Quran 2:223, the word 'anna' can mean either 'where' (place) or 'how' (manner). The ambiguity arises because both meanings are lexically valid and occur elsewhere in the Quran. This creates mushkil because the word resembles both contexts.
The example shows that mushkil often involves words with homonymous or polysemous meanings.
4
Apply Contextual Analysis to Resolve the Ambiguity
The context of the word 'harth' (tillage) is key. The anus is not a place of tillage; therefore, interpreting 'anna' as 'where' would permit sodomy, which is contrary to the metaphor. Thus, 'anna' must mean 'how', referring to manner of intercourse, not location.
This step uses qarinah (contextual indicator) to restrict the meaning – a standard Hanafi tool for resolving mushkil.
5
Note the Qiyas and Additional Example
The prohibition of sodomy is analogized to prohibition during menstruation due to shared impurity. The passage also introduces another mushkil example from 'qawarir min fidda' (vessels of silver) where a metaphor causes ambiguity, though the text is incomplete.
The qiyas strengthens the ruling by connecting two cases with a common illah (cause).

He said: As for al-mushkil, it is that which enters into its resemblance, and its ruling is to believe that its intended meaning is true, then to engage in seeking and reflecting upon it until the intended meaning becomes clear. Section Two: Definition of Mushkil: Mushkil is that which becomes ambiguous because of its resemblance to its likes, and its intended meaning cannot be obtained without reflection after seeking. Like a person who changes his appearance and mixes with people; searching is not enough, but due to the change of features, reflection is also needed to recognize him. The ruling of Mushkil: Its ruling is that one must believe that its intended meaning is true, then reflect upon it until its intended meaning is clear. As in the Quran: 'So come to your tillage however you wish' (2:223). The word 'anna' is mushkil because sometimes it comes in the meaning of 'ayn' (where) indicating generality of place, as in the Quran when Zakariya asked Maryam: 'Anna laki hadha' (O Mary, from where did this come to you?). And sometimes it comes in the meaning of kayf (how), as in the Quran when Maryam said: 'Anna yakunu li ghulam' (How can I have a son...). But when we reflected on the word 'harth' (tillage), we knew that here 'anna' is in the meaning of kayf because the anus is not the place of tillage but the place of feces. Therefore, in the mentioned verse 'fa'atu harthakum anna shi'tum', is 'anna' for generality of place or generality of states? First we sought the meaning. Now if 'anna' is for generality of place (meaning 'ayn'), then the meaning would be that sodomy with women is also permissible, but the context of the verse 'fa'atu harthakum' prevents that, because the woman is designated as 'harth' (tillage), and it is obvious that the anus is not the place of tillage but the place of feces. So it becomes determined that since 'anna' is not for generality of place, it is necessarily for generality of states, and the intended meaning of the verse is: have intercourse with your wives in whatever state you wish, standing, sitting, or lying down. However, the correct way is what the Prophet taught; any other way is against the Sunnah, and human reason also cannot be satisfied with it despite permissibility. In short, in the mentioned verse 'anna' is in the meaning of kayf. Several things: The prohibition of sodomy is also analogized to the prohibition of intercourse during menstruation in that the cause of prohibition in menstruation is impurity, which exists even more fully in sodomy. And sometimes ambiguity occurs due to a hidden kind of metaphor, as in the Quran: 'Qawarir min fidda' (vessels of silver). The problem is that a qarura (vessel) is not made of silver but of glass. But when we reflected, we concluded that Paradise...
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage defines the second category of ambiguous text in usul al-fiqh: al-mushkil. The author first quotes the matn defining it as text that becomes problematic due to its resemblance to other texts, requiring reflection after seeking to understand its intended meaning. He uses an analogy of a person who changes his appearance, needing more than a search to be identified. The ruling is that one must believe the intended meaning is correct (i.e., it is from Allah) and then engage in ijtihad to resolve the ambiguity. The primary example is the Quranic verse 'fa'atu harthakum anna shi'tum' (2:223). The word 'anna' is ambiguous because it can mean 'where' (place) or 'how' (manner). By analyzing the context, especially the word 'harth' (tillage) which applies to the vagina, not the anus, the author concludes that 'anna' here means 'how' (manner), refuting the possibility of sodomy. The passage also analogizes the prohibition of sodomy to prohibition during menstruation due to impurity. Finally, it mentions another example of mushkil from 'qawarir min fidda' where the metaphor is subtle, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
مشکل (mushkil): Text that is ambiguous due to resemblance to other texts, requiring reflection to resolve.
اشکال (ishkāl): Ambiguity or problematic nature of a text.
حرث (harth): Tillage; used in the verse metaphorically for the vagina.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling regarding the word 'anna' in the verse 'fa'atu harthakum anna shi'tum' after reflection?
A) It means 'where', permitting intercourse in any place.
B) It means 'how', permitting various positions but not sodomy.
C) It is ambiguous and must be left unresolved.
D) It means 'where', and sodomy is allowed.

Key Takeaway
Al-mushkil is resolved through contextual analysis; the word 'anna' in Quran 2:223 means 'how', not 'where', thus permitting various positions but not sodomy.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Haqiqah and Majaz: Definitions and Rulings
Defining Haqiqah and Majaz with rulings and the haythiyyah refinement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definitions of Haqiqah and Majaz, identify the four types of wad', and explain why the haythiyyah restriction is necessary in their definitions.

Core Idea
A word can be Haqiqah in one aspect and Majaz in another—haythiyyah preserves definitional precision.

This passage introduces the fundamental classification of words into literal (Haqiqah) and figurative (Majaz). The author defines each, outlines their rulings, and underscores a critical refinement: to avoid contradictions, definitions must include the aspect (haythiyyah) from which the word is considered. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping how Hanafi usul handles textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Define Haqiqah and its wad' types
Identify the definition of Haqiqah as a word used for its coined meaning. Then dissect the four types of wad' (lughawi, shar'i, 'urfi khas, 'urfi 'amm) as listed.
Each type specifies who did the coining: lexicographer, Lawgiver, specific group, or common people.
2
Define Majaz and its condition
State the definition of Majaz as a word used for other than its coined meaning due to a relationship (munasabah). Note that without this relationship, the usage is invalid.
The condition excludes arbitrary or jocular uses.
3
State the ruling of each
For Haqiqah: the coined meaning is present. For Majaz: the borrowed meaning is present. Both apply whether the meaning is specific or general.
The ruling affirms that the intended meaning is established.
4
Understand the haythiyyah refinement
The author notes that without adding 'aspect' (haythiyyah), the definitions would fail because a word like 'salat' is Haqiqah in Shari'ah and Majaz in language. Adding haythiyyah makes the definitions comprehensive and exclusive.
Haythiyyah means considering the context or discipline from which the word is viewed.
5
Apply the definitions to a sample word
Take the word 'salat' and classify it as Haqiqah shar'iyyah when referring to ritual prayer and as Majaz lughawi when used for supplication, due to lack of relationship in the latter context? Actually it has relationship. But the point is that the same word can be both depending on aspect.
The exercise reinforces the necessity of haythiyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسیم ثالث
حقیقت کی تعریف :
حقیقت اس لفظ کا نام ہے جس سے وہ معنی مراد ہو جس کیلئے اس کو وضع کیا گیا ہو۔ وضع کی تعریف اب وضع کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اس لفظ کو کسی معنی کیلئے متعین کرنا پھر معین کرنا اگر واضع لغت کی طرف سے ہو تو وہ ضع لغوی ہوگی جیسے انسان کی وضع حیوان ناطق کیلئے۔ اور اگر شارع کی طرف سے ہو تو وضع شرعی ہوگی جیسے صلوٰۃ کی وضع نماز کیلئے ۔ اور اگر یہ تعین کسی قوم مخصوص کی طرف سے ہو تو وضع عرفی خاص ہوگی جیسے فعل کی وضع کلمہ مخصوص کیلئے ۔ اور اگر تعیین عوام " عرف عام کی طرف سے ہو تو وضع عرفی عام ہوگی اس کی مثال دابتہ ہے جس کو چوپائے کیلئے وضع کیا گیا ہے۔
حقیقت کا حکم :
حقیقت کا حکم یہ ہیکہ جس معنی کیلئے اس کو وضع کیا گیا ہے وہ پایا جائے خواہ وہ معنی خاص ہو یا عام۔
قوله : وأما المجاز فاسم لما اريد به غير ما وضع له لمناسبة بينها وحكمه وجود ما استعير له خاصاً كان أو عام.
مجاز کی تعریف
مجاز ہر اس لفظ کا نام ہے جس سے مراد معنی غیر موضوع الہ ہو دونوں معنی حقیقی اور معنی مجازی کے درمیان کسی مناسبت کی وجہ ہے۔
مناسبت کی قید کا فائدہ مناسبت کی قید لگا کر احتراز کیا اس سے کہ ارض کو سماء کے لئے استعمال کیا جائے کیونکہ زمین اور اسمان میں کوئی مناسبت نہیں ہے اسی طرح ہزل یعنی مذاق سے احتراز کیا ہے۔
نکته... پھر صاحب کتاب کہتے ہیں کہ حقیقت اور مجاز دونوں کی تعریف میں حیثیت کی قید لگا نا ضروری ہے تا کہ دونوں تعریفیں جامع مانع ہونے کے اعتبار سے ٹوٹ نہ جائیں کیونکہ صلوٰۃ کا لفظ لغت میں دعا کے لئے ہے اور شریعت میں ارکان معلومہ مخصوصہ کے لئے تو لفظ صلوۃ لغت کے اعتبار سے دعاء میں حقیقت ہے اور ارکان معلومہ مخصوصہ میں مجاز ہے اور شریعت کے اعتبار سے ارکا ن معلومہ مخصوصہ میں حقیقت ہے اور دعاء میں مجاز ہے ۔
مجاز کا حکم :
مجاز کا حکم یہ ہے جس معنی کے لئے اس کو مستعار لیا گیا ہو وہ معنی پایا جائیگا خواہ وہ معنی خاص ہو یا عام ۔
قوله : وقال الشافعى رحمه الله لا عموم للمجاز لأنه ضرورى وانا نقول ان عموم الحقيقة لم يكن لكونها حقيقة بل لدلا لة زائدة على تلك الحقيقة وكيف يقال انه ضروري وقد كثر ذلك في كتاب الله تعالى.
Third Division
Definition of Haqiqah: Haqiqah is the name of a word from which the meaning for which it was coined is intended. Now the meaning of wad' is to assign a word for a meaning. Then if the assignment is from the side of the lexicographer, it is wad' lughawi, like the assignment of 'insan' for 'hayawan natiq'. And if from the side of the Lawgiver (Shari'), it is wad' shar'i, like the assignment of 'salat' for prayer. And if this assignment is from a specific group, it is wad' 'urfi khas, like the assignment of 'fi'l' for a specific kalimah. And if the assignment is from the common people ('urf 'amm), it is wad' 'urfi 'amm, its example is 'dabbah' which was assigned for quadruped.
Ruling of Haqiqah: The ruling of Haqiqah is that the meaning for which it was coined is found, whether that meaning is specific or general.
His saying: As for Majaz, it is a name for every word intended by it other than what it was coined for, due to a relationship between them, and its ruling is that the meaning for which it is borrowed exists, whether it is specific or general.
Definition of Majaz: Majaz is the name of every word from which the meaning other than the coined meaning is intended, due to some relationship between the literal and figurative meanings.
Benefit of the restriction of relationship: By restricting with relationship, avoidance is made from using 'earth' for 'sky', because there is no relationship between earth and sky, similarly jest (mazaq) is avoided.
Note: Then the author says that adding the restriction of aspect (haythiyyah) in the definitions of both Haqiqah and Majaz is necessary, so that both definitions are not broken in terms of being comprehensive and exclusive. Because the word 'salat' in language is for supplication, and in Shari'ah for specific known actions. So the word 'salat' with respect to language is Haqiqah in supplication and Majaz in the specific known actions, and with respect to Shari'ah it is Haqiqah in the specific known actions and Majaz in supplication.
Ruling of Majaz: The ruling of Majaz is that the meaning for which it is borrowed will be found, whether that meaning is specific or general.
His saying: And al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: There is no generality in Majaz because it is necessary. And we say: Verily the generality of Haqiqah is not because it is Haqiqah, but due to an additional indication over and above that Haqiqah. And how can it be said that it is necessary, when it is abundant in the Book of Allah?
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage introduces the third classification of words: Haqiqah (literal) and Majaz (figurative). It defines Haqiqah as a word used for its original coinage, which can be lexical, shar'i, 'urfi khas, or 'urfi 'amm. Its ruling is that the coined meaning is present. Majaz is defined as a word used for other than its original meaning due to a relationship; its ruling is that the borrowed meaning is present. A critical note explains that definitions should include the aspect (haythiyyah) to avoid contradictions when a word is Haqiqah in one context and Majaz in another, as with 'salat'. Finally, the dispute over 'Umum al-Majaz (generality of figurative speech) is presented: Imam al-Shafi'i denies generality in Majaz, while the Hanafis argue that generality is not intrinsic to Haqiqah either but comes from an external indicator (dalalah za'idah), and the frequent occurrence of general Majaz in the Quran supports their view.

Key Terms
وضع (wad'): Assignment of a word for a meaning by a lexicographer, Lawgiver, or custom
حیثیت (haythiyyah): Aspect or perspective; adding this to definitions ensures a word can be Haqiqah in one context and Majaz in another

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the restriction of 'aspect' (haythiyyah) necessary in defining Haqiqah and Majaz?
A) To ensure the definitions are short and concise
B) To avoid contradiction when the same word is Haqiqah in one context and Majaz in another
C) To exclude figurative speech from the Quran
D) To limit definitions to lexical coinage only

Key Takeaway
A word's classification as Haqiqah or Majaz depends on the aspect (haythiyyah) from which it is viewed, making both definitions context-dependent.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Dispute over 'Umum al-Majaz: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Dispute over 'Umum al-Majaz with proofs and responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on 'umum al-majaz, state the Shafi'i objection, and articulate the Hanafi response using the concept of dalalah za'idah.

Core Idea
Generality in Majaz is not intrinsic but comes from an external indicator — so it is possible and occurs in the Quran.

This passage presents a key disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: can figurative speech (Majaz) be general ('amm)? Imam al-Shafi'i denies it, claiming it is necessary that Majaz is not general. The Hanafis respond by distinguishing between the source of generality and the nature of the word. Understanding this dispute clarifies how each school approaches general commands in the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i is quoted as saying: 'There is no generality in Majaz because it is necessary (daruri).' Understand that he considers it a logical necessity that figurative expressions cannot be general.
The term 'daruri' implies self-evident or necessary knowledge.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafis affirm that Majaz can indeed be general. They reject the notion that generality is exclusive to Haqiqah.
The Hanafi position relies on textual evidence and a refined understanding of generality.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The Hanafi proof presented is: 'Generality is abundant in the Book of Allah (the Quran) even in figurative expressions.' Therefore, the existence of general Majaz in the Quran demonstrates its possibility.
This is a naqli (textual) proof.
4
Understand the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i necessity claim
The Hanafis argue that generality in Haqiqah itself is not due to being literal, but due to an additional indication (dalalah za'idah) over and above the literal meaning. Therefore, there is no barrier for such an additional indication to also occur in Majaz.
This response refutes the necessity argument by separating the source of generality.
5
Apply the concepts: identify a Quranic verse with general Majaz
Find an example like 'وَجَاءَ رَبُّكَ' (wa jaa rabbuka) where 'jā'a' (came) is Majaz and is a general verb encompassing any coming. This illustrates the point.
Practice recognizing the interplay of 'umum and Majaz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسیم ثالث
حقیقت کی تعریف :
حقیقت اس لفظ کا نام ہے جس سے وہ معنی مراد ہو جس کیلئے اس کو وضع کیا گیا ہو۔ وضع کی تعریف اب وضع کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اس لفظ کو کسی معنی کیلئے متعین کرنا پھر معین کرنا اگر واضع لغت کی طرف سے ہو تو وہ ضع لغوی ہوگی جیسے انسان کی وضع حیوان ناطق کیلئے۔ اور اگر شارع کی طرف سے ہو تو وضع شرعی ہوگی جیسے صلوٰۃ کی وضع نماز کیلئے ۔ اور اگر یہ تعین کسی قوم مخصوص کی طرف سے ہو تو وضع عرفی خاص ہوگی جیسے فعل کی وضع کلمہ مخصوص کیلئے ۔ اور اگر تعیین عوام " عرف عام کی طرف سے ہو تو وضع عرفی عام ہوگی اس کی مثال دابتہ ہے جس کو چوپائے کیلئے وضع کیا گیا ہے۔
حقیقت کا حکم :
حقیقت کا حکم یہ ہیکہ جس معنی کیلئے اس کو وضع کیا گیا ہے وہ پایا جائے خواہ وہ معنی خاص ہو یا عام۔
قوله : وأما المجاز فاسم لما اريد به غير ما وضع له لمناسبة بينها وحكمه وجود ما استعير له خاصاً كان أو عام.
مجاز کی تعریف
مجاز ہر اس لفظ کا نام ہے جس سے مراد معنی غیر موضوع الہ ہو دونوں معنی حقیقی اور معنی مجازی کے درمیان کسی مناسبت کی وجہ ہے۔
مناسبت کی قید کا فائدہ مناسبت کی قید لگا کر احتراز کیا اس سے کہ ارض کو سماء کے لئے استعمال کیا جائے کیونکہ زمین اور اسمان میں کوئی مناسبت نہیں ہے اسی طرح ہزل یعنی مذاق سے احتراز کیا ہے۔
نکته... پھر صاحب کتاب کہتے ہیں کہ حقیقت اور مجاز دونوں کی تعریف میں حیثیت کی قید لگا نا ضروری ہے تا کہ دونوں تعریفیں جامع مانع ہونے کے اعتبار سے ٹوٹ نہ جائیں کیونکہ صلوٰۃ کا لفظ لغت میں دعا کے لئے ہے اور شریعت میں ارکان معلومہ مخصوصہ کے لئے تو لفظ صلوۃ لغت کے اعتبار سے دعاء میں حقیقت ہے اور ارکان معلومہ مخصوصہ میں مجاز ہے اور شریعت کے اعتبار سے ارکا ن معلومہ مخصوصہ میں حقیقت ہے اور دعاء میں مجاز ہے ۔
مجاز کا حکم :
مجاز کا حکم یہ ہے جس معنی کے لئے اس کو مستعار لیا گیا ہو وہ معنی پایا جائیگا خواہ وہ معنی خاص ہو یا عام ۔
قوله : وقال الشافعى رحمه الله لا عموم للمجاز لأنه ضرورى وانا نقول ان عموم الحقيقة لم يكن لكونها حقيقة بل لدلا لة زائدة على تلك الحقيقة وكيف يقال انه ضروري وقد كثر ذلك في كتاب الله تعالى.
Third Division
Definition of Haqiqah: Haqiqah is the name of a word from which the meaning for which it was coined is intended. Now the meaning of wad' is to assign a word for a meaning. Then if the assignment is from the side of the lexicographer, it is wad' lughawi, like the assignment of 'insan' for 'hayawan natiq'. And if from the side of the Lawgiver (Shari'), it is wad' shar'i, like the assignment of 'salat' for prayer. And if this assignment is from a specific group, it is wad' 'urfi khas, like the assignment of 'fi'l' for a specific kalimah. And if the assignment is from the common people ('urf 'amm), it is wad' 'urfi 'amm, its example is 'dabbah' which was assigned for quadruped.
Ruling of Haqiqah: The ruling of Haqiqah is that the meaning for which it was coined is found, whether that meaning is specific or general.
His saying: As for Majaz, it is a name for every word intended by it other than what it was coined for, due to a relationship between them, and its ruling is that the meaning for which it is borrowed exists, whether it is specific or general.
Definition of Majaz: Majaz is the name of every word from which the meaning other than the coined meaning is intended, due to some relationship between the literal and figurative meanings.
Benefit of the restriction of relationship: By restricting with relationship, avoidance is made from using 'earth' for 'sky', because there is no relationship between earth and sky, similarly jest (mazaq) is avoided.
Note: Then the author says that adding the restriction of aspect (haythiyyah) in the definitions of both Haqiqah and Majaz is necessary, so that both definitions are not broken in terms of being comprehensive and exclusive. Because the word 'salat' in language is for supplication, and in Shari'ah for specific known actions. So the word 'salat' with respect to language is Haqiqah in supplication and Majaz in the specific known actions, and with respect to Shari'ah it is Haqiqah in the specific known actions and Majaz in supplication.
Ruling of Majaz: The ruling of Majaz is that the meaning for which it is borrowed will be found, whether that meaning is specific or general.
His saying: And al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: There is no generality in Majaz because it is necessary. And we say: Verily the generality of Haqiqah is not because it is Haqiqah, but due to an additional indication over and above that Haqiqah. And how can it be said that it is necessary, when it is abundant in the Book of Allah?
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage introduces the third classification of words: Haqiqah (literal) and Majaz (figurative). It defines Haqiqah as a word used for its original coinage, which can be lexical, shar'i, 'urfi khas, or 'urfi 'amm. Its ruling is that the coined meaning is present. Majaz is defined as a word used for other than its original meaning due to a relationship; its ruling is that the borrowed meaning is present. A critical note explains that definitions should include the aspect (haythiyyah) to avoid contradictions when a word is Haqiqah in one context and Majaz in another, as with 'salat'. Finally, the dispute over 'Umum al-Majaz (generality of figurative speech) is presented: Imam al-Shafi'i denies generality in Majaz, while the Hanafis argue that generality is not intrinsic to Haqiqah either but comes from an external indicator (dalalah za'idah), and the frequent occurrence of general Majaz in the Quran supports their view.

Key Terms
عموم مجاز (umum al-majaz): Generality found in figurative expressions
دلالة زائدة (dalalah za'idah): Additional indication that confers generality (e.g., 'kull', 'jami')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the core Hanafi response to Imam al-Shafi'i's claim that 'there is no generality in Majaz because it is necessary'?
A) They agree and treat Majaz as always specific.
B) They argue that generality in Haqiqah also comes from an additional indication, so it can exist in Majaz.
C) They deny the existence of Majaz altogether.
D) They rely on consensus that Majaz cannot be general.

Key Takeaway
Generality in words depends on an external indicator (dalalah za'idah), not on whether the word is literal or figurative, so Majaz can indeed be general.

Definition of Majaz and Its Ruling
Defines majaz with its condition of munasaba and explains its ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of majaz, identify the essential condition of munasaba, and apply the ruling that the intended meaning is established.

Core Idea
Majaz transfers meaning, but only when a valid connection (munasaba) exists between literal and metaphorical senses.

Words in legal texts may be used in their literal sense (haqiqa) or in a transferred sense (majaz). Knowing how to identify majaz is crucial for correct interpretation. This lesson defines majaz, explains the condition of munasaba (connection), and clarifies its ruling—that the borrowed meaning is fully effective in conveying the intended legal rule.

Guided Path
1
Isolate the definition of majaz
Read the first sentence: Majaz is a word used for a meaning other than its original (subject) meaning, but with a connection between the two.
The term 'original meaning' refers to the meaning for which the word was coined in the language (wad').
2
Analyze the condition of munasaba
Note the phrase 'munasaba' (connection). This condition excludes arbitrary or nonsensical usage, e.g., using 'earth' for 'sky' without any relation.
Munasaba is the basis for valid metaphorical extension.
3
Understand the role of perspective (haysiyya)
The author stresses that definitions of haqiqa and majaz must be qualified by perspective. For example, 'salat' is haqiqa in language (supplication) and majaz in Shariah (prayer), and vice versa from the Shariah perspective.
This ensures the definitions remain mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive.
4
Apply the ruling of majaz
The ruling states that the borrowed meaning is present and effective, whether specific or general. This prepares for later discussions on whether majaz can convey general meanings.
The ruling establishes that majaz is not weaker than haqiqa in conveying legal intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجاز کی تعریف
مجاز ہر اس لفظ کا نام ہے جس سے مراد معنی غیر موضوع الہ ہو دونوں معنی حقیقی اور معنی
مجازی" کے درمیان کسی مناسبت کی وجہ ہے۔
مناسبت کی قید کا فائدہ مناسبت کی قید لگا کر احتراز کیا اس سے کہ ارض کو سماء کے لئے استعمال کیا جائے کیونکہ زمین اور اسمان میں کوئی مناسبت نہیں ہے اسی طرح ہرل یعنی مذاق سے
احتراز کیا ہے۔
نکته... پھر صاحب کتاب کہتے ہیں کہ حقیقت اور مجاز دونوں کی تعریف میں حیثیت کی قید لگا نا ضروری ہے تا کہ دونوں تعریفیں جامع مانع ہونے کے اعتبار سے ٹوٹ نہ جائیں کیونکہ صلوٰۃ کا لفظ لغت میں دعا کے لئے ہے اور شریعت میں ارکان معلومہ مخصوصہ کے لئے تو افظ صلوۃ لغت کے اعتبار سے دعاء میں حقیقت ہے اور ارکان معلومہ مخصوصہ میں مجاز ہے اور شریعت کے اعتبار سے ارکا ن معلومہ مخصوصہ میں حقیقت ہے اور دعاء میں مجاز ہے ۔
مجاز کا حکم :
مجاز کا حکم یہ ہے جس معنی کے لئے اس کو مستعار لیا گیا ہو وہ معنی پایا جائیگا خواہ وہ معنی خاص ہو یا عام ہو ۔
Definition of Majaz: Majaz is the name for every word by which a meaning other than its original intended meaning is intended, and between the literal meaning and the metaphorical meaning there is some connection (munasaba). The benefit of the condition of connection: By imposing the condition of connection, avoidance is made from using 'earth' for 'sky' because there is no connection between earth and sky; similarly, avoidance is made from foolish talk. Note: Then the author says that it is necessary to impose the condition of perspective (haysiyya) in the definition of both haqiqa and majaz so that both definitions are not invalidated in terms of being comprehensive and exclusive. For the word 'salat' in language is for supplication, and in Shariah it is for specific known actions. So the word 'salat', from the linguistic perspective, is haqiqa in supplication and majaz in specific known actions; and from the Shariah perspective, it is haqiqa in specific known actions and majaz in supplication. Ruling of Majaz: The ruling of majaz is that the meaning for which it is borrowed will be established, whether that meaning is specific or general.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi usul definition of majaz (metaphorical usage). The author begins by defining majaz as any word used for a meaning other than its original (subject) meaning, provided there is a connection (munasaba) between the literal and metaphorical senses. The connection is essential to distinguish valid majaz from arbitrary or nonsensical usage. The note explains that both haqiqa and majaz must be qualified by perspective (haysiyya) to avoid contradictions: a word may be haqiqa in one context (e.g., salat in language for supplication) and majaz in another (salat in Shariah for prayer). This ensures the definitions are comprehensive and exclusive. Finally, the ruling states that when majaz is used, the intended meaning (the borrowed meaning) is considered present and effective, whether that meaning is specific or general. This sets the stage for the dispute about generality in majaz.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): A word used for a meaning other than its original meaning due to a connection between the two.
مناسبت (munasaba): A connection or relationship between the literal and metaphorical meanings that justifies the metaphorical usage.
حیثیت (haysiyya): Perspective or aspect; the condition that defines whether a word is haqiqa or majaz relative to a particular discipline (language, Shariah, etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author require the condition of perspective (haysiyya) in the definitions of haqiqa and majaz?
A) To make the definitions longer and more precise.
B) To prevent contradictions when a word is haqiqa in one discipline and majaz in another.
C) To ensure that only Shariah meanings are considered haqiqa.
D) To limit majaz to specific meanings only.

Key Takeaway
Majaz is a valid linguistic tool in Hanafi usul, requiring a connection (munasaba) between literal and metaphorical meanings, and its borrowed meaning is fully effective regardless of specificity.
