Generality in Majaz: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Dispute
Usul al-Fiqh dispute on generality in majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits generality in majaz and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the principle of extra indications.

Core Idea
Generality in haqiqa comes from extra indicators, not from literalness — so majaz with those indicators is also general.

This passage presents a foundational usul dispute: can a metaphorical expression (majaz) be general (amm), covering all instances of its metaphorical meaning? The Shafi'i school argues that majaz is used only out of necessity and thus limited, while the Hanafi school defends generality based on the existence of extra linguistic indicators that create breadth. Understanding this dispute clarifies how different schools read Quranic and prophetic language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
First, clearly state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on generality in majaz as presented in the text. Note the core reasoning of each.
Hanafis: permissible with extra indicators. Shafi'is: not permissible because majaz is by necessity.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
Analyze the Hanafi argument: generality in haqiqa is not due to haqiqa itself but due to extra indications (zawa'id al-dalala) like plural forms or indefinite in negation. These same indications, when present in majaz, produce generality.
Focus on the concept of 'extra indication' — the key to the Hanafi position.
3
Handle the Shafi'i objection and response
The Shafi'is argue that majaz is dharoori (necessary) and necessity is limited. The Hanafis respond: majaz is abundant in the Quran, so attributing necessity to Allah is impossible; necessity, if any, relates to the listener/mujtahid. Then consider the Shafi'i counter-objection about muqtada (implied meaning) and the Hanafi reply.
The distinction between speaker and listener is crucial.
4
Apply to an example
The text gives the hadith of Ibn Umar using the word 'sa'' (a measure) metaphorically. Explain how this example illustrates the Hanafi position: the word is general for what it makes permissible, showing majaz can be general.
This concrete case demonstrates the practical outcome of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وقال الشافعى رحمه الله لا عموم للمجاز لأنه ضرورى وانا نقول ان عموم الحقيقة لم يكن لكونها حقيقة بل لد لا لة زائدة على تلك الحقيقة وكيف يقال انه ضروري وقد
كثر ذلك في كتاب الله تعالى.
عموم مجاز جائز ہے یا نہیں؟
شوافع کا مذہب امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ مجاز میں عموم نہیں ہوتا یعنی عموم مجاز جائز نہیں ہے
امام شافعی کی دلیل کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ مجاز ضروری ہے یعنی ضرورت کی بناء پر اس کی طرف رجوع کیا جاتا ہے جب معنی حقیقی مراد لینا محمد رہو اور ضرورت بقدر ضرورت مقدر مانی جاتی ہے۔
الضرورة تنتقد ربقد رها .
احناف کا مذہب احناف کے نزدیک عموم مجاز جائز ہے یعنی معنی کو اس قدر عام کر دیا جائے کہ اس میں حقیقی اور مجازی دونوں معنی آجائیں۔
احناف کی دلیل ہی کہتے ہیں کہ حقیقت میں جو عموم ہوتا ہے وہ حقیقت ہونے کی وجہ سے نہیں ہوتا بلکہ کسی زائد دلالت کی وجہ سے ہوتا ہے مثلاً جمع کا صیغہ ہونا، نکرہ کا سیاق
نفی میں واقع ہونا وغیرہ وغیرہ۔
شوافع کو جواب... الہذا جب یہ دلالتیں مجاز میں پائی جائینگی تو وہ بھی عام ہو گا باقی آپ کا یہ کہنا کہ مجاز ضروری ہوتا ہے یہ صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ کتاب اللہ میں مجاز کثرت کے ساتھ آیا ہے ، حالانکہ اللہ تعالیٰ ضرورت سے پاک ہیں۔ اگر ہم مجاز کو ضرورت کی حد تک قرار دے دیں تو اللہ کے لئے
ضرورت ثابت کرنالازم آئے گا جو بجز کی علامت ہے اور اللہ تعالیٰ بجز سے پاک ہے۔ اعتراض... شوافع احناف پر اعتراض کرتے ہیں کہ یہی بات تو مقتضیٰ کی صورت میں بھی لازم
آتی ہے مقتضی قرآن مجید میں بکثرت واقع ہے حالانکہ بھی ضروری ہے۔
جواب ... مقتضاء استدال کی اقسام میں سے ہے لہذا وہاں ضرورت کا تعلق مجتہد کے ساتھ ہے تکلم کیسا تھ نہیں جو کہ اللہ تعالی ہے لہذا اللہ تعالیٰ کیلئے عجز و ضرورت ثابت نہیں ہوتا شارح فرماتے
ہیں کہ انصاف کی بات یہ ہے کہ مجاز کا ضرورت ہونا بھی سامع کے اعتبار سے ہے۔
قوله : ولهذا جعلنا لفظ الصاع في حديث ابن عمر رضى
الله عنه عا ما فيما يحله
عموم مجاز کی ایک مثال :
اسی وجہ سے ہم کہتے ہیں کہ چونکہ مجاز میں عموم ہوتا ہے لہذا حضرت عبداللہ ابن عمر رضی اللہ
His statement: And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: There is no generality in majaz because it is by necessity, and we say that the generality of haqiqa is not because it is reality but due to an extra indication beyond that reality. And how can it be said that it is necessary when it occurs frequently in the Book of Allah? Is generality in majaz permissible or not? The Shafi'i school: Imam Shafi'i says that majaz does not have generality, i.e., generality in majaz is not permissible. The conclusion of Imam Shafi'i's proof is that majaz is necessary, meaning it is resorted to out of necessity when the literal meaning cannot be intended, and necessity is measured according to its extent: 'Necessity is measured by its measure.' The Hanafi school: According to the Hanafis, generality in majaz is permissible, meaning the meaning is made general to such an extent that both literal and metaphorical meanings are included. The Hanafi proof: They say that the generality that occurs in haqiqa is not due to it being haqiqa but due to some extra indication, e.g., the form of a plural, a indefinite noun in a negative context, etc. Response to the Shafi'is: Thus when these indications are found in majaz, it too will become general. As for your statement that majaz is necessary, that is not correct because majaz occurs frequently in the Book of Allah, while Allah is free from necessity. If we restrict majaz to the extent of necessity, then it would necessitate proving necessity for Allah, which is a sign of incapability, and Allah is free from incapability. Objection: The Shafi'is object to the Hanafis that the same thing applies to muqtada (implied meaning), which also occurs frequently in the Quran despite being necessary. Response: Muqtada is a category of inference (istidlal), so necessity relates to the mujtahid, not to the speaker, who is Allah. Therefore, necessity and incapability are not proven for Allah. The commentator says that the truth is that the necessity of majaz is from the perspective of the listener. His statement: And for this reason we made the word 'sa'' in the hadith of Ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, general in what it makes permissible. An example of generality in majaz: For this reason we say that since majaz has generality, therefore Abdullah ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with him...
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents a classic usul dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is on whether generality (umoom) can occur in majaz (metaphorical usage). The Shafi'i position, attributed to Imam al-Shafi'i, holds that majaz does not admit generality because it is used only out of necessity (dharoora). Necessity is limited to the extent needed, so a metaphorical expression cannot be general. The Hanafi position, supported by the author, argues that generality in haqiqa does not stem from its being literal but from additional indicators (zawa'id al-dalala) such as plural forms or indefinite nouns in negative contexts. When these indicators exist in majaz, it too becomes general. The Hanafi response refutes the necessity argument by noting that majaz appears frequently in the Quran, and Allah is free from necessity. To attribute necessity to Allah would imply incapability. The Shafi'i counter-objection about muqtada (implied meanings) is answered by distinguishing: muqtada relates to the mujtahid's inference, not Allah's speech. The commentator clarifies that the 'necessity' of majaz is from the listener's perspective, not the speaker's. The example of the word 'sa'' (a measure) in the hadith of Ibn Umar is given to illustrate that a metaphorical word can be general in its application.

Key Terms
عموم مجاز (umoom al-majaz): The permissibility of a metaphorical expression to encompass all individuals or cases to which the metaphorical meaning applies.
ضروری (dharoori): Necessary; something resorted to due to necessity when the literal meaning cannot be intended.
زائد دلالت (za'id dalala): An extra indication or indicator that brings about generality, such as a plural form or an indefinite noun in a negative context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

The Rule of Haqiqah over Majaz and Its Two Quranic Applications
Usul rule with two applied furu'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that haqiqah takes precedence over majaz to derive rulings from ambiguous Quranic texts, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-arguments on oath expiation and in-law prohibition.

Core Idea
When haqiqah is possible, majaz is dropped — majaz cannot compete with the original.

This lesson unpacks a foundational usul rule: the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a word always takes priority over its figurative meaning (majaz) as long as the literal meaning is applicable. We will learn how to distinguish haqiqah from majaz, and then see two practical applications where this rule settles major fiqh disputes: whether oaths of deception require expiation, and whether in-law relationships are established through invalid unions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rule and the distinction between haqiqah and majaz
First, grasp that haqiqah is the original literal meaning that cannot be negated without contradiction, while majaz can be negated. The rule: as long as haqiqah is possible, majaz is dropped because it is subordinate.
The sign: if you can correctly negate the term (e.g., calling a grandfather 'father' can be negated), it is majaz.
2
Apply the rule to the word 'aqd in the oath verses
Examine Surah al-Ma'idah 5:89: 'but He will take you to account for what you have contracted of oaths.' The word 'aqd is haqiqah for a concluded (mun'aqidah) oath, not for resolve (cazm) or a false oath. Since haqiqah is possible, majaz (resolve) is dropped.
Thus expiation is only for mun'aqidah oaths, not ghamoos (false oaths) or laghw.
3
Compare with the opposing school's interpretation
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that 'aqd in the verse is general and includes both mun'aqidah and ghamoos, so expiation applies to both. The Hanafi response: when haqiqah is possible, majaz is not taken, and the verse of Surah al-Baqarah (accountability for what hearts earn) already covers sin for both, so expiation is additional only for mun'aqidah.
This is a thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in expiation rulings.
4
Apply the rule to the word 'nikah' in the marriage verse
Surah al-Nisa' 4:22: 'Do not marry those whom your fathers married.' The word nikah has haqiqah meaning 'joining' (al-damm), which is most directly achieved by the guardian (wali) who joins the couple. Majaz meaning 'contract' is dropped since haqiqah is possible.
Thus the prohibition covers any act of joining by the guardian, including fornication with guardian's involvement, establishing in-law prohibition in both valid and invalid unions.
5
Analyse the dispute and the thamarah
Imam al-Shafi'i restricts 'nikah' to the marriage contract, so in-law prohibition only from valid contract. Hanafis extend to any union through the guardian. The thamarah: for Hanafis, if a man commits fornication with a woman under her guardian's arrangement, his father cannot marry that woman; for Shafi'is, he can.
This shows how a usul rule directly impacts fiqh rulings.

From them in this hadith: 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams nor a sa' for two sa's.' Sa' is the name of everything that fits into a sa'. Selling a container (sa') for two containers (sa') is permissible. So Imam Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) here extracts the word food, as if the original phrase is: 'Do not sell food present in a sa' for food present in two sa's.' And we extract the interpretation as: 'Do not sell everything that fits into a sa' for two sa's.' His saying: 'And haqiqah does not drop from the named thing, unlike majaz, and whenever it is possible to act upon it, majaz is dropped. So the contract is for what is concluded, not for the resolve, and marriage is for intercourse, not for the contract.' The sign of distinguishing haqiqah and majaz. The author is answering this question: how will haqiqah and majaz be recognized—i.e., how will it be known whether a word is applied to its literal meaning or to its figurative meaning? So he said that in the case of haqiqah, the literal meaning does not drop from its named object. For example, a father is called 'ab' and it cannot be said 'he is not a father', but in majaz, it drops from its named object. For example, a grandfather is also called 'ab', but it is correct to say of the grandfather 'he is not a father'. Next, the author states a principle and rule: as long as acting upon haqiqah is possible, then majaz will be dropped, because majaz is the deputy, successor, and borrowed of haqiqah, and when the original exists, the deputy, successor, and borrowed thing are not resorted to (the borrowed does not compete with the original). On this principle, the author raises two applications. (1) The first application... In the Qur'an: 'But He will take you to account for what you have contracted of oaths.' The word 'aqd is haqiqah for the concluded oath and majaz for resolve. So since here it is taken as haqiqah, majaz is dropped. The detail of this summary is that oaths are of three types. (1) Laghw oath: swearing on something believing it true, or that which occurs as an excuse. Its ruling: there is no sin nor expiation. (2) Ghamoos oath: intentionally swearing a false oath about a past matter to deceive someone. Its ruling: there is sin but no expiation. (3) Mun'aqidah oath: swearing with intent to do or not do something, then doing the opposite. Its ruling: there is both sin and expiation. The original matter is that Allah mentioned the issue of oaths in two places in the Holy Qur'an: first in Surah al-Baqarah He said 'But He will take you to account for what your hearts have earned', then in Surah al-Ma'idah He said 'But He will take you to account for what you have contracted of oaths.' Imam Shafi'i says both places have the same meaning, so expiation is required in both ghamoos and mun'aqidah. We say that the word 'aqd is haqiqah for mun'aqidah oath, so the verse of Surah al-Ma'idah indicates that expiation is only in mun'aqidah, contrary to the verse of Surah al-Baqarah which indicates non-accountability? It includes both ghamoos and mun'aqidah, and the accountability there is absolute, so its perfect individual is intended, which is the Hereafter accountability, i.e., sin. The second application: the author raises a second application that in the Qur'an: 'And do not marry those whom your fathers married.' We say that marriage here means the wife? No, it means the husband? Actually, the verse: 'Do not marry what your fathers married' — we say that by 'nikah' here is meant the act of marriage contract? The text: 'We say that by nikah here is meant the guardian, not the marriage contract. So this verse includes the guardian whether permissible or prohibited, and in both cases the prohibition of in-law relationship is established because the meaning of nikah in language is 'joining', and joining occurs through the guardian, and the contract is called nikah because it is a cause of joining the spouses. But Imam Shafi'i says that here only the marriage contract is meant, so the guardian is not meant; the prohibition of in-law relationship is established only from the marriage contract, not from fornication.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a principle from Noor al-Anwar regarding the precedence of haqiqah (literal meaning) over majaz (figurative meaning). The commentator first gives an example of a hadith on riba to show how Hanafis and Shafi'is differ in interpreting 'food' — Hanafis take a broader literal meaning (everything that fits in a sa') while Shafi'is narrow it. Then the rule is stated: haqiqah does not drop from its named thing, but majaz does. To distinguish them, if a word can be correctly negated (e.g., calling a grandfather 'father' can be negated by saying 'he is not a father'), it is majaz; if not, it is haqiqah. The key legal maxim: when haqiqah is possible, majaz is dropped because majaz is subordinate to haqiqah. Two applications follow. First: the Quranic verse on oaths (Surah al-Ma'idah: 'but He will take you to account for what you have contracted of oaths'). The Hanafi position: 'aqd (contract) is haqiqah for the mun'aqidah oath (intentional), not for resolve or ghamoos. So the verse only imposes expiation on mun'aqidah, while Surah al-Baqarah ('for what your hearts have earned') covers sin for both ghamoos and mun'aqidah. Shafi'i holds both verses mean the same and expiation is for both. Second: the verse 'Do not marry those whom your fathers married' (Surah al-Nisa'). Hanafis interpret 'nikah' as the guardian's act (joining), so the prohibition applies to the guardian's act whether valid or invalid, thus in-law relationship is established from both valid marriage and fornication (zina) via the guardian's involvement. Shafi'is interpret 'nikah' as the marriage contract only, so in-law relationship only comes from valid contract.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): A word used in its original, literal meaning; it does not drop from its named object.
مجاز (majaz): A word used in a figurative meaning different from its original; it can be negated without contradiction.
عقد ('aqd): Literal meaning: to tie or contract; in oaths, it refers to the deliberate oath (mun'aqidah), not resolve.
نكاح (nikah): Literal meaning: joining; here it is interpreted as the guardian's act of joining, not the marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Both meanings are equally valid.
B) The figurative meaning is dropped.
C) The figurative meaning takes precedence.
D) The literal meaning is dropped.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul rule that haqiqah takes precedence over majaz when possible resolves major fiqh disputes on oaths and in-law prohibitions.

The Principle of Not Combining Haqiqah and Majaz: Bequest to Mawali
Applying the principle of not combining haqiqah and majaz to a bequest case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined to resolve ambiguities in textual directives, using the case of a bequest to 'mawali'.

Core Idea
A word cannot simultaneously intend its literal and figurative meanings in a single bequest.

In Hanafi usul, a word may have both a literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) sense, but these cannot be combined in a single utterance. This principle has practical fiqh implications, as illustrated by a bequest (wasiyyah) using the word 'mawla'. Understanding this rule helps in interpreting ambiguous testaments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the multiple meanings of 'mawla'
List all possible referents of the word 'mawla' as found in the passage: mu'tiq (emancipator), mu'taq (freed slave), mu'tiq al-mu'tiq (emancipator of the emancipator), mu'taq al-mu'taq (freed slave of the freed slave).
Mu'tiq and mu'taq are literal (haqiqah); the others are metaphorical (majaz).
2
Classify each meaning as haqiqah or majaz
Using the Hanafi classification: mu'tiq and mu'taq are haqiqah because they are the primary usage; mu'tiq al-mu'tiq and mu'taq al-mu'taq are majaz due to contextual extension.
This classification is based on the principle that the true meaning is the first established.
3
Apply the rule that haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined
If the testator has both a mu'tiq and a mu'taq, then the word 'mawla' would need to be used in two different haqiqah meanings simultaneously, which is impossible according to Hanafi usul. Hence the bequest is void.
If there is only one type of haqiqah (e.g., only mu'taq) and also majaz referents, only the haqiqah meaning is intended, so the bequest goes to the mu'taq.
4
Derive the specific rulings for each scenario
From the rule, derive rulings: (a) If only mu'taq exists, he gets half. (b) If both mu'tiq and mu'taq exist, bequest void. (c) If only mu'taq and mu'taq al-mu'taq exist, only mu'taq gets. (d) If only mu'tiq al-mu'taq exists, he gets.
These outcomes reflect that the word cannot combine haqiqah and majaz, nor two haqiqahs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) پہلی تفریح اگر کسی شخص نے اپنے ثلث مال کی وصیت کی اپنے موالی کیلئے اور حالت یہ ہے کہ اس کا ایک ہی معتق بالفتح یعنی آزاد کردہ غلام ہے تو وہ پورے نصف کا مستحق ہوگا، لیکن اگر اس کے آزاد کنندہ اور آزاد کردہ ہر قسم کے موالی ہیں تو وصیت باطل ہو جائے گی۔ اس لئے کہ مولی کا اطلاق هیچه معتق اور معشق دونوں پر برابر ہوتا ہے اور مجاز اس کا اطلاق معیق المُعتق اور مُعتق المُعتق پر ہوتا ہے تو اگر اس کے مُعتق اور معتق دونوں ہیں تو اشتراک معنوی کی بناء پر وصیت باطل ہو جائے گی اور اگر اس کے معتق نہ ہوں بلکہ صرف معتق اور معتق المعتق ہوں تو صرف معتق مستحق ہوگا ۔ ہاں اگر اس کا کائی معلق نہ ہو بلکہ صرف تعيق المعتق ہو تو وہ وصیت کا مستحق ہوگا۔
(1) First branch: If a person bequeaths one-third of his wealth to his mawali, and he has only one mu'taq (freed slave), then he deserves half; but if he has both mu'tiq (emancipator) and mu'taq (freed slave) types of mawāli, then the bequest becomes void. This is because the word 'mawla' applies equally to both mu'tiq and mu'taq, and metaphorically it applies to mu'tiq al-mu'tiq and mu'taq al-mu'taq. So if he has both mu'tiq and mu'taq, then due to sharing of meaning, the bequest becomes void. And if he has no mu'tiq but only mu'taq and mu'taq al-mu'taq, then only the mu'taq is entitled. Yes, if he has no mu'taq but only mu'tiq al-mu'taq, then he is entitled to the bequest.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is the first branch (furu') derived from the principle that haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined. The issue is a bequest (wasiyyah) of one-third of one's wealth to one's 'mawali' (plural of mawla). The word 'mawla' has multiple meanings: in its literal sense (haqiqah), it applies to both the mu'tiq (the master who frees a slave) and the mu'taq (the freed slave). In a metaphorical sense (majaz), it extends to mu'tiq al-mu'tiq (the master of the master) and mu'taq al-mu'taq (the freed slave of the freed slave). According to the Hanafi rule, a single word cannot intend both haqiqah and majaz simultaneously. Therefore, if the testator has a mu'tiq (someone who freed him) and a mu'taq (someone he freed) — i.e., both literal meanings exist — the word 'mawla' would attempt to combine both haqiqah meanings, which itself is impossible as per the principle (since haqiqah cannot combine with another haqiqah? Actually the text says that because the word applies to both mu'tiq and mu'taq as haqiqah, and also metaphorically to further extensions, but the issue here is combining haqiqah and majaz? The text says: 'because the word mawla applies equally to mu'tiq and mu'taq (haqiqah) and metaphorically to mu'tiq al-mu'tiq etc.' So if both mu'tiq and mu'taq exist, there is already a combination of two haqiqah meanings? But the principle is about haqiqah and majaz. The commentary explains that the word mawla is used in haqiqah for mu'tiq and mu'taq, and in majaz for others. Thus, if the testator has both mu'tiq and mu'taq, the word would need to be used for both haqiqah meanings, which is already impossible? Actually the Hanafi position is that a word cannot be used in two different haqiqah meanings either? The text seems to say that the bequest is void because of 'ishtirak ma'nawi' (shared meaning) which leads to ambiguity. Alternatively, it may be that the word mawla is haqiqah for mu'tiq and mu'taq, and majaz for others, so if both mu'tiq and mu'taq are present, the intended meaning would combine two haqiqahs, which is impossible. The result: bequest void. If only mu'taq and mu'taq al-mu'taq (haqiqah+majaz) are present, then only the mu'taq (haqiqah) is intended, so the bequest goes to him. If only mu'tiq al-mu'taq (majaz) is present, he gets it. This illustrates how the principle prevents combining haqiqah and majaz.

Key Terms
مولی (mawla): A word with literal meanings (mu'tiq and mu'taq) and metaphorical extensions (mu'tiq al-mu'tiq, mu'taq al-mu'taq).
معتق (mu'tiq): The person who frees a slave; one of the literal meanings of mawla.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a testator has both a mu'tiq (emancipator) and a mu'taq (freed slave), what happens to the bequest to 'mawali'?
A) The bequest is valid and divided equally.
B) The bequest is void because the word mawla cannot combine both literal meanings.
C) Only the mu'tiq inherits.
D) Only the mu'taq inherits.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule that haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined ensures that a single ambiguous word in a bequest yields a clear outcome based on the principle of non-combination.

The Impossibility of Combining Haqiqah and Majaz: Principle and Four Branches
Explaining the usul rule and four fiqh branches applying it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi usul principle that haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined in a single usage, and apply it to four fiqh scenarios involving wills, intoxicants, and Quranic interpretation.

Core Idea
A single word can only carry one intended meaning; literal and figurative cannot coexist in one usage.

This lesson presents a foundational Hanafi usul principle: a word cannot simultaneously intend its literal (haqiqah) and figurative (majaz) meanings. The author demonstrates this through four practical branches (tafri'at), each illustrating how the principle resolves apparent conflicts. You will see how the Hanafi school applies this rule to wills, hadd punishments, and Quranic exegesis, and how it differs from the Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
State the rule: haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined in a single usage. Understand that a word has one intended meaning per context.
This is a logical principle, not derived from a specific text.
2
Apply to first branch: will for mawali
When a testator bequeaths to 'mawali', if he has both a mu'tiq (manumitter) and a mu'taq (freed slave), the term covers both categories literally. Since both cannot be intended, the will is void. If only one category exists, the literal meaning applies.
Mawla is a mushtarak (homonym) for mu'tiq and mu'taq.
3
Apply to second branch: khamr vs other intoxicants
Khamr literally means grape wine. Its ruling (hadd for a sip) cannot be extended to other intoxicants via majaz because the literal meaning is specific. Shafi'is consider all intoxicants khamr by figurative extension.
This illustrates the difference in tafsir of nusus.
4
Apply to third branch: sons vs grandsons in a will
In a will for 'sons', only literal sons are included; grandsons are excluded because they are a figurative extension. The literal meaning takes precedence.
Contrast with the exception for aman where caution allows inclusion.
5
Apply to fourth branch: lams in the Quran
The Quranic 'aw lamastum al-nisa' is interpreted figuratively as intercourse, not literally as touch. Combining both meanings is impossible. The Shafi'i view that both are intended is rejected.
This is the key Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute.
6
Address the objection: caution in hudud
When a harbi seeks aman for his 'sons', grandsons are included because of caution (shubha) in sparing blood. This is a practical exception, not a violation of the principle.
The principle remains; the exception is due to the gravity of the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۲) دوسری تفریح خمر کے ساتھ غیر خمر نشہ آور اشیاء مثلا طلاء ، تقيع التمر والزبيب کو لاحق نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ خمر میں تو ایک قطرے کے پینے سے بھی حد واجب ہو جاتی ہے لیکن غیر خمر میں جب تک نشہ نہ ہو تو اس وقت تک حد واجب نہیں ہوگی اور خمر انگوروں کا کچاپا فی ہوتا ہے جب وہ ابلنے لگے اور گاڑھا ہو جائے اور جھاگ پھینکنے لگے ۔

امام شافعی کا مذہب امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ جو چیز بھی نشہ آور ہو وہ خمر ہے۔

قوله : فلايراد بنو بنيه في الوصية لأبنائه

(۳) تیسری تفریح اگر کسی شخص نے زید کے ابن کیلئے وصیت کی اور حالت یہ ہیکہ اس کے ابن بھی ہے اور ابن الا بن بھی ہے تو وصیت میں صرف ابن داخل ہونگے نہ کہ ابن الابن

قوله : ولا يرادا للمس باليد في قوله تعالى أولا مستم النساء لان الحقيقة فيما سوى الاخير والمجاز فيه مراد فلم يبق الاخر مراداً .

(۴) چوتھی تفریح قرآن کریم میں ہے ( أولمستم النساء ) لمس کا حقیقی معنی لمس بالید ہاتھ سے چھوٹا ہے اور مجازی معنی جماع ہے تو امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہاں دونوں معنی مراد ہیں اس لئے کہ اگر لمس بالید ہو تو حدیث کی وجہ سے تیمم کی اجازت ہوگی اور اگر لمس بالجما ع ہو تو جنابت کی وجہ سے تیمم کی اجازت ہوگی۔

احناف کا مذہب ..... ہم کہتے ہیں کہ یہاں بالجماع مجازی معنی مراد ہے لہذا حقیقی معنی مراد نہیں لیا جائے گا کیونکہ دونوں کا جمع ہونا محال ہے۔

خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ پہلی مثالوں میں حقیقی معنی مراد لیا گیا ہے اور چوتھی مثال میں مجازی معنی مراد ہے۔

قوله : وفى الاستيمان على الابناء والموالي تدخل الفروع لأ ن ظاهر الاسم صار شبهة في حقن الدم

پہلا اعتراض اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ جب حربی اپنے ابن اور موالی کیلئے امان مانگے تو اس میں ابن الابن اور موالی الموالی کیوں داخل ہوتے ہیں؟ حالانکہ جب ابن حقیقی مراد لیا گیا تو مجازی معنی ابن الا بن مراد نہیں ہونا چاہئے اسی طرح موالی میں بھی ۔

جواب ...... جواب یہ ہے کہ ظاہری اسم کے اعتبار سے حفاظت دم میں شبہ آگیا کیونکہ قتل انسان اور ایقاع حدود میں بہت زیادہ احتیاط کی ضرورت ہوتی ہے اسی وجہ ت حدیث سے معلوم ہوتا ہے
It is true that this cloth is both owned and borrowed. Answer: The cloth is not with the pledgee as a loan but as ownership, and wearing it was prohibited due to the right of the pledgor. When that right was removed, it returned to its original state. Branches on the impossibility of combining haqiqah and majaz.

The author has presented several branches on this principle.

(1) First branch: If a person bequeaths one-third of his wealth to his mawali, and he has only one mu'taq (freed slave), then he deserves the whole half. But if he has both a manumitter and a freedman as mawali, the will becomes void. Because the term mawla applies equally to both mu'tiq and mu'taq literally, and figuratively it applies to mu'tiq al-mu'taq and mu'taq al-mu'taq. So if he has both mu'tiq and mu'taq, due to shared meaning the will is void. If he has no mu'tiq but only mu'taq and mu'taq al-mu'taq, only the mu'taq receives. Yes, if he has no mu'taq at all but only mu'taq al-mu'taq, then that one receives the will.

(2) Second branch: Non-khamr intoxicating substances like tilaa', naqi' of dates and raisins are not joined to khamr. Because with khamr, even one sip makes hadd obligatory, but with other intoxicants, hadd is not obligatory until intoxication occurs. Khamr is specifically from grapes when it boils, thickens, and foams.

Imam Shafi'i's school: Imam Shafi'i says that whatever is intoxicating is khamr.

(3) Third branch: In a will for one's sons, grandsons are not included.

(4) Fourth branch: In the Qur'anic verse "aw lamastum al-nisa", the literal meaning of lams is touching with the hand, and the figurative meaning is intercourse. Imam Shafi'i says that both meanings are intended: if touching with the hand, it permits tayammum due to hadith; if touching through intercourse, it permits tayammum due to janabah.

Hanafi school: We say that here the figurative meaning of intercourse is intended; the literal meaning is not intended because combining both is impossible.

Summary: In the first examples, the literal meaning was intended; in the fourth example, the figurative meaning is intended.

Objection: When a harbi seeks aman for his sons and mawali, why are grandsons and mawali al-mawali included? When 'son' is taken literally, the figurative meaning of grandson should not be intended; similarly for mawali.

Answer: Because in terms of the apparent name, a doubt arises for sparing blood, since in killing people and executing hudud, great caution is required. Hence it is known from hadith.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi usul principle that it is impossible to intend both the literal (haqiqah) and figurative (majaz) meanings of a word in a single usage. The author demonstrates this principle through four fiqh applications (tafri'at) and then defends it against an objection.

First application: In a will for mawali (clients/freed slaves), the term 'mawla' literally applies to both the manumitter and the freed slave. If the testator has both types, the will is void because both meanings cannot be intended simultaneously. If only one type exists, the will is valid but limited to that meaning.

Second application: Khamr (grape wine) is a specific term; its ruling (hadd for drinking any amount) cannot be extended to other intoxicants because the literal meaning is restricted to grape wine. This contrasts with Imam Shafi'i who considers all intoxicants as khamr by figurative extension.

Third application: In a will for 'sons', the literal meaning excludes grandsons; the figurative meaning is not included.

Fourth application: The Qur'anic phrase 'aw lamastum al-nisa' (or you have touched women) is interpreted figuratively as intercourse by the Hanafis, not literally as touch. Combining both meanings would violate the principle.

Finally, an objection is raised: In seeking aman for 'sons', why are grandsons included? The answer is that in matters of life and death, caution (shubha) is applied, so the literal name creates doubt, allowing inclusion. This is a practical concession, not a violation of the usul principle.

Key Terms
حقیقت (haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as used in language
مجاز (majaz): The figurative, non-literal meaning of a word used with a contextual indicator
مولی (mawla): A term for a client, including both manumitter (mu'tiq) and freed slave (mu'taq) and their respective clients
خمر (khamr): Specifically wine made from grapes, distinguished from other intoxicants
لمس (lams): Touching: literal (with hand) vs figurative (intercourse)

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The mu'tiq receives the entire bequest.
B) The mu'taq receives the entire bequest.
C) The bequest is void because both meanings cannot be intended.
D) The bequest is split equally between them.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle that haqiqah and majaz cannot be combined forces a choice between literal and figurative meanings, shaping rulings on wills, intoxicants, and Quranic interpretation.

Siyaq al-Kalam as Qarinah for Majaz
Explaining how siyaq al-kalam serves as qarinah for majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when context (siyaq) indicates figurative meaning and apply the Hanafi principle to determine legislative intent.

Core Idea
Context overrides literal meaning in Islamic legal interpretation.

In the previous lessons, we discussed the concept of qarinah (indicators) that signal a shift from literal (haqiqah) to figurative (majaz) meaning. The third and final type is siyaq al-kalam — the linguistic context of speech. This passage teaches us how context can completely reinterpret an apparently clear statement. We will analyze two examples: a hypothetical divorce statement and a Quranic verse, both of which illustrate how context transforms literal meaning into a different legal intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qarinah
Look for a phrase or element in the speech that suggests the literal meaning is not intended. This is the qarinah.
In the first example, the qarinah is 'in kuntu rajula' (if you are a man).
2
Determine the literal meaning
State what the speech would mean if taken at face value, without considering context.
The literal meaning of 'talliq imra'ataka' is a command to divorce, which constitutes a delegation of divorce (tawkil).
3
Analyze the context (siyaq)
Explain how the qarinah reveals that the literal meaning is not the intended one. Identify the actual communicative purpose (e.g., threat, challenge, incapacitation).
The phrase 'in kuntu rajula' implies that only a 'real man' would divorce, which is often used as a challenge or threat, not a genuine delegation.
4
Derive the ruling (istinbat)
Conclude what the speech actually means in terms of Islamic law based on the context. State the resulting legal interpretation.
The divorce statement is not a valid delegation; it is a threat. Similarly, the Quranic verse is a threat, not permission for disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قرینہ ثالثہ تیرہ قرینہ سیاق ہے یعنی سیاق الکلام اس بات پر دلالت کرتا ہوگا کہ یہاں حقیقت مراد نہیں بلکہ مجاز مراد ہے جیسا کہ کوئی شخص کسی کو کہے ( طلق امر أتى ان كنت رجلا ) اس کلام کی حقیقت تو توکیل بالطلاق ہے لیکن (ان كنت رجلا ) اس بات پر قرینہ ہے کہ یہاں حقیقت مراد نہیں ہے بلکہ مخاطب کو صرف عاجز کرنا اور ڈرانا مراد ہے۔ اس طرح قرآن حکیم میں ہے فمن شاء فليؤمن ومن شاء فليكفر ( یہاں اللہ تعالیٰ کی طرف سے کفر کی اجازت نہیں دی گئی ہے بلکہ یہ تحدید اور تخییر کے لئے ہے کیونکہ آگے قرینہ موجود ہے اور وہ ہے نار جہنم کی وعید ۔
The thirteenth qarinah is siyaq (context), i.e., the context of speech indicates that here the literal meaning is not intended but the figurative meaning is intended. For example, if someone says to another: 'Divorce your wife if you are a man.' The literal meaning of this speech is delegation of divorce, but 'if you are a man' is an indicator that here the literal meaning is not intended; rather, only incapacitating and frightening the addressee is intended. Similarly, in the Noble Quran: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve.' Here permission for disbelief is not given by Allah, but it is for threat and choice because a qarinah is present ahead, and that is the threat of the fire of Hell.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage discusses the third type of qarinah (contextual indicator) that signals a shift from literal to figurative meaning in Islamic legal discourse. The author states that siyaq al-kalam (the context of the speech) can override the apparent literal meaning when it indicates a different intent. Two examples are provided: first, a hypothetical statement 'Divorce your wife if you are a man' – literally this is a delegation of divorce (tawkil bil-talaq), but the conditional phrase 'if you are a man' shows the speaker intends to challenge or frighten, not actually delegate divorce. Second, the Quranic verse 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve' (Surah Al-Kahf, 18:29) – taken literally, it would grant permission for disbelief, which is impossible. The surrounding threat of Hellfire serves as a qarinah that the verse is a threat and challenging choice, not permission. This analysis follows the Hanafi usul principle that context (siyaq) is a legitimate tool for determining legislative intent, and it is applied before resorting to other interpretive methods.

Key Terms
قرینہ (qarinah): An indicator that accompanies speech and shows that figurative meaning is intended.
سیاق الکلام (siyaq al-kalam): The linguistic context of speech that guides interpretation.

Key Takeaway
Siyaq al-kalam is a powerful qarinah that can transform literal meaning into figurative intent, as shown in both the divorce example and the Quranic verse.

The Fourth Qarinah: Speaker's State as Indicator of Figurative Meaning
Explaining how the speaker's state signals figurative meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of hal al-mutakallim as a qarinah to determine whether a statement is literal or figurative.

Core Idea
The speaker's emotional state can override the literal meaning of a statement in Hanafi usul.

In Hanafi usul, not everything a speaker says is taken at face value. One key indicator (qarinah) that the literal meaning is not intended is the speaker's own state (hal al-mutakallim), especially extreme emotions like anger. This lesson unpacks the fourth qarinah through a classic example: a husband's angry conditional divorce pronouncement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qarinah and its basis
The fourth qarinah is the speaker's state (hal al-mutakallim). It is based on external circumstances surrounding the utterance, not on the text itself.
This qarinah is considered by the Hanafi school to override the literal meaning when the context strongly suggests figurative intent.
2
Analyze the literal meaning (haqiqah) of the example
The example is a conditional divorce statement: 'If you go out, you are divorced.' Literally, this means divorce occurs whenever the wife goes out, without any time restriction.
In Hanafi usul, such a literal reading would classify the statement as general ('amm) in its condition.
3
Apply the qarinah: speaker's state as indicator of majaz
The husband's extreme anger (yamin fawri) suggests the statement was not a binding legal pronouncement but an emotional outburst. The qarinah indicates that the literal meaning is not intended.
The Hanafi principle: a qarinah can restrict the general meaning (takhsis al-amm) even without a separate textual indicator.
4
Derive the ruling
Since the literal meaning is rejected due to the qarinah, the divorce only takes effect (if at all) during the state of anger. After anger subsides, the condition is not triggered.
The usul chain: 'amm → qarinah → majaz → restricted application.
5
Contrast with other madhahib
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may not recognize this qarinah and would enforce the literal meaning regardless of the speaker's state.
This highlights a unique Hanafi attention to context and intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قرینه رابعه ... چو تھا قرینہ کوئی ایسا معنی ہے جس کا تعلق متکلم کیساتھ ہو کہ وہ دلالت کرتا ہو کہ یہاں حقیقت مراد نہیں ہے جیسے کہ یمین فوریعنی وہ قسم جو انتہائی غصے کی حالت میں اٹھائی جائے تو اگر شوہر نے غصے کی حالت میں بیوی کو کہد یا ان خرجت فانت طائق ) تو اس کلام کی حقیقت تو یھی کہ جب بھی عورت نکلے تو اس کو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی لیکن بیتکلم کی حالت اس بات پر دلا لت کرتی ہے کہ یہاں حقیقت مراد نہیں ہے لہذا اگر عورت غصہ ٹھنڈا ہونے کے بعد سے گھر نکل گئی تو طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔
The fourth qarinah... A qarinah that has a connection with the speaker, which indicates that here the literal meaning is not intended. For example, a 'yamin fawri' – that is, an oath taken in a state of extreme anger. So if a husband says to his wife in anger, 'If you go out, you are divorced,' the literal meaning of this speech is that whenever the woman goes out, divorce will occur. But the state of the speaker indicates that here the literal meaning is not intended. Therefore, if the woman goes out after the anger has cooled, divorce will not occur.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth type of qarinah: the state or condition of the speaker (hal al-mutakallim). The author explains that when the speaker is in a state of extreme anger (yamin fawri), his utterances may carry figurative meaning rather than literal intent. The example given is a husband who, in a fit of rage, tells his wife, 'If you go out, you are divorced.' The literal application would mean that divorce takes effect whenever the wife leaves the house, even after the anger subsides. However, because the speaker's state (anger) serves as a qarinah, the statement is interpreted as a threat or emotional outburst, not a binding legal pronouncement. Therefore, if the wife leaves after the husband calms down, no divorce occurs. This is a nuanced Hanafi position that considers the psychological context of the speaker when determining legal consequences, reflecting the school's attention to intent (niyyah) and circumstances (ahwal).

Key Terms
یمین فوری (yamin fawri): An oath taken immediately in a state of intense anger.
حالت متکلم (hal al-mutakallim): The emotional or circumstantial state of the speaker.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of a husband saying 'in kharajti fa anti taliq' in anger, what is the consequence if the wife leaves after the husband calms down?
A) Divorce occurs because the statement was conditional.
B) Divorce does not occur because the speaker's anger indicates the literal meaning is not intended.
C) Divorce occurs only if the wife leaves during the anger.
D) Divorce occurs but can be revoked.

Key Takeaway
The fourth qarinah (hal al-mutakallim) allows the Hanafi school to consider the speaker's emotional state to determine that literal meaning is not intended, preventing unfair outcomes.

The Fifth Qarinah: How Context Signals Figurative Intent in the Hanafi Method
Interpreting two key hadiths through the fifth qarinah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the fifth qarinah (indication of complete speech) to identify when a text requires ta'wil, and explain the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the hadith 'Actions are by intentions'.

Core Idea
Context overrides literal meaning when literal interpretation leads to impossibility or contradiction.

In this lesson, we examine the fifth qarinah — the indication of the complete speech. This is a crucial tool for determining when a text must be interpreted metaphorically. The author analyzes two key hadiths to show how context signals that the literal meaning is not intended. Understanding this principle helps us correctly derive rulings from primary texts, balancing textual commitment with practical reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Fifth Qarinah
Understand that the fifth qarinah refers to the overall context of a complete statement indicating a figurative meaning. Recognize its role in Hanafi usul for ta'wil.
The Arabic term is 'dalalat al-kalam al-kamil'.
2
Analyze the First Hadith on Intentions
Take the hadith 'innama al-a'malu binniyat'. State its literal meaning: 'no act is valid without intention'. Note that many acts (e.g., eating) occur without intention, making literal interpretation impossible.
This impossibility triggers the need for ta'wil according to Hanafis.
3
Trace the Hanafi Argument for Ta'wil
Hanafis argue that taking the literal meaning leads to 'umum mushtarak' (general commonality) — a problematic universal application. The context (reward in the hereafter) indicates a figurative reading focusing on otherworldly judgment.
The hadith is interpreted to mean 'reward is by intentions', not 'validity is by intentions'.
4
Contrast the Shafi'i Objection
Imam al-Shafi'i objects that reading the hadith as metaphor would create 'umum majaz' (general metaphorical interpretation), which he deems impermissible as it undermines textual clarity. Thus he insists on the literal reading, making intention fard in all acts including wudu.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is the ruling on intention in wudu.
5
Apply to the Second Hadith on Forgetfulness
Apply the same qarinah to the hadith 'My nation is pardoned for mistakes and forgetfulness'. Its literal meaning (no mistakes occur) is false; context indicates pardon from sin in the Hereafter, while worldly rulings (e.g., invalidating fast/prayer) still apply.
This reinforces the pattern: context shifts meaning from worldly to otherworldly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قرینہ خامسہ پانچواں قریب مکمل کلام کی دلالت ہے یعنی جس محل میں کلام واقع ہے وہ اس بات پر دلالت کرتا ہو کہ یہاں حقیقت نہیں بلکہ مجاز مراد ہے جیسے حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کا فرمان ( انما الاعمال بالنيتا ( اس کا حقیقی معنی تو یہ ہے کہ کوئی عمل بھی بغیر نیت کے صحیح نہ ہو ۔ حالانکہ بہت ت اعمال بغیر نیت کے واقعہ ہوتے ہیں لہذا یہاں تبازی معنی مراد لیا جائے گا یعنی ( ثواب الا عمال ( حكم الاعمال بالنیات) یعنی اعمال کا آخرت میں تو اب نیت سے ملے گا۔ اور حکم دو قسم کا ہو سکتا ہے: (1) حکم دنیوی (۲) حکم اخروی اب یہاں حکم اخروی یعنی ثواب تو بالا جماع مراد ہے لہذا حکم دنیوی مراد نہیں لیا جائے گا۔ ہمارے نزدیک تو اس لئے کہ اس صورت میں عموم مشترک لازم آئیگا اور امام شافعی کے نزدیک اس لئے کہ عموم مجاز لازم آئے گا اور یہ ان کے نزدیک جائز نہیں ہے ۔ حاصل کلام یہی ہے جواز اعمال نیت پر موقوف نہیں اسلئے احناف کے نزدیک وضو میں نیت فرض نہیں ہے امام شافعی کے نز د یک وضو میں نیت فرض ہے لیکن جو باقی عبادات مقصودہ ہیں وہ نیت کے بغیر صحیح نہیں ہوں گی۔ حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کا فرمان رفع عن امتى الخطاً والنسيان اس میں بھی حقیقت کو چھوڑا گیا ہے محل کلام کی دلالت کی وجہ سے کیونکہ اس کے ظاہر سے تو یہ معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ اس است سے خطا اور نسیان ہو ہی نہیں سکتا۔ حالانکہ یہ بات غلط ہے اس لئے یہاں مجازی معنی مراد ہو گا یعنی خطاء اور نسیان کی بناء پر آخرت میں گناہ نہیں ہو گا اگر چہ دنیا کا حکم اس پر بہر صورت مرتب ہو گا لہذا اگر خطاء روزے کی حالت میں کچھ کھا پی لیا تو روزہ فاسد ہو جائے گا اور خطاء نماز کی حالت میں کلام کیا تو نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی۔
The fifth qarinah is the indication of the complete speech, i.e., the context in which the speech occurs indicates that here the literal meaning is not intended but figurative. For example, the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Actions are by intentions.' Its literal meaning is that no act is valid without intention. However, many acts do occur without intention. Therefore, a figurative meaning will be taken here, i.e., 'reward of actions is by intentions' or 'the judgment of actions is by intentions.' That is, the reward of actions in the Hereafter will be based on intention. And judgment can be of two types: (1) worldly judgment (2) otherworldly judgment. Here otherworldly judgment, i.e., reward, is intended by consensus, so worldly judgment is not intended. According to us (Hanafis), because in this case general commonality (umum mushtarak) would be necessary, and according to Imam Shafi'i, because general metaphorical (umum majaz) would be necessary, and that is not permissible according to him. The conclusion is that the validity of actions is not dependent on intention. Therefore, according to the Hanafis, intention is not obligatory (fard) in wudu; according to Imam Shafi'i, intention is obligatory in wudu. But the remaining intended acts of worship (al-'ibadat al-maqsudah) are not valid without intention. The saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'My nation is pardoned for mistakes and forgetfulness.' In this too, the literal meaning is abandoned due to the indication of the context, because from its apparent meaning it seems that this nation cannot make mistakes or forget at all. However, that is false; therefore, a figurative meaning will be intended, i.e., there will be no sin in the Hereafter due to mistake and forgetfulness, although the worldly ruling will still apply. So if someone eats or drinks mistakenly while fasting, the fast becomes invalid; and if one speaks mistakenly during prayer, the prayer becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage discusses the fifth qarinah: the indication of the complete speech (dalalat al-kalam al-kamil). The author explains that the overall context in which a statement is made can override its literal meaning. Two major hadiths are analyzed. First, the famous hadith 'Actions are by intentions' (innama al-a'malu binniyat). Literally, it suggests that no action is valid without intention, but there are many actions (e.g., eating, sleeping) that are valid without intention. Therefore, the context shows that it refers to the reward (thawab) in the Hereafter, not to the worldly validity (sihhah) of actions. The author notes a difference between Hanafis and Imam Shafi'i: Hanafis argue that taking the literal meaning would lead to umum mushtarak (general commonality) which is problematic, while Shafi'i argues it would lead to umum majaz (general metaphor) which he deems impermissible. The practical result is that in wudu (ablution), intention is not fard according to Hanafis (because wudu's validity does not depend on it), but it is fard according to Shafi'is. However, both agree that for other intentional acts of worship (like prayer, fasting), intention is essential. The second hadith is 'The nation is pardoned for mistakes and forgetfulness' (rufi'a 'an ummati al-khata' wa al-nisyan). Literally, it could be taken to mean that mistakes and forgetfulness never occur, which is false. The context indicates it means no sin in the Hereafter, though worldly rulings (like invalidity of fast or prayer) still apply. This analysis demonstrates the Hanafi method of ta'wil (interpretive construction) based on contextual evidence, balancing textual literalness with practical reality and other legal principles (like istihsan).

Key Terms
عموم مشترک (umum mushtarak): A general term that applies to multiple referents equally; taking the literal meaning would lead to an impossible commonality.
عموم مجاز (umum majaz): A general metaphorical interpretation; Shafi'i considers this impermissible because it would undermine clarity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis consider intention not obligatory in wudu despite the hadith 'innama al-a'malu binniyat'?
A) Because wudu is not an act of worship.
B) Because the hadith is weak.
C) Because the hadith refers to reward in the Hereafter, not worldly validity, and wudu can be valid without intention.
D) Because Hanafis follow a different hadith.

Key Takeaway
The fifth qarinah shows that when literal meaning leads to impossibility or contradiction, the context indicates a figurative interpretation, distinguishing worldly validity from otherworldly reward.

Tahrim Mudaf ila al-A'yan: Literal vs Metaphorical Prohibition
Analyzing whether prohibition attributed to entities like wine is literal or metaphorical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the literalness of prohibition attributed to entities, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the division of prohibition into two types.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, prohibition attributed to entities is literal, not metaphorical, because it is a more emphatic form akin to negation.

The classification of prohibition (tahrim) is a foundational topic in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. This lesson tackles a specific dispute: when the Quran or hadith attributes prohibition directly to an entity, such as in 'Forbidden to you are your mothers,' does this mean the entity itself is prohibited, or is it a metaphor for prohibiting the action? The Hanafi school asserts the literal reading, grounded in a nuanced division of prohibition types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed text forms
Recognize that the dispute centers on phrases like Quran 4:23 (hurrimat 'alaykum ummahatukum) and the hadith (hurrimat al-khamr li-'aynihi). These attribute prohibition directly to entities.
The term 'tahrim mudaf ila al-a'yan' refers to this grammatical construction.
2
State the opposing argument
Some scholars (Shafi'is) argue that prohibition is an attribute of actions, not substances. Therefore, phrases like 'your mothers are forbidden' must be metaphorical, meaning the act of marrying them is forbidden.
They rely on the principle that entities cannot literally be prohibited.
3
Present the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond by distinguishing two types of prohibition: tahrim al-fi'l (directed at the act) and tahrim al-'ayn (directed at the entity). The latter is literal because it carries greater emphasis, like negation (nafy).
This division is the core Hanafi proof.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafis draw an analogy to linguistic negation: 'nafy' is stronger than 'laysa'. Similarly, entity-directed prohibition is stronger than act-directed prohibition, making it literal.
This shows the school's method of using linguistic intensification as evidence.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference may affect the severity of sin or penalties. For example, the prohibition of wine as an entity implies it is inherently impure and forbidden in all contexts, not just when consumed.
This highlights why the dispute matters for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والتحريم المضاف إلى الأعيان كالممحارم والخمر حقيقة عندنا خلافاً للبعض بعض لوگوں کا خیال ہے جب تحریم کی نسبت اعیان کی طرف کی جائے جیسا کہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے اس قول میں حرمت علیکم امھا تکم.... الخ اور اس حدیث میں حرمت الخمر لعینھا تو یہ باں مجاز مراد ہو گا یعنی حرمت علیکم نکاح امھا تکم اور حرمت شرب الخمر لعینھا کیونکہ خمر تو عین ہے وہ حرمت کو قبول نہیں کرتا کیونکہ حالت اور حرمت فعل کے اوصاف ہیں ہم کہتے ہیں کہ حرمت اپنی حقیقت پر ہے اس لئے کہ حرمت کی دو قسمیں ہیں: (1) دو حرمت جو فعل کی طرف متوجہ ہو تو فعل ممنوع عنہ بن جائے گا اور بندہ ممنوع ۔ (۲) وہ حرمت جو گل کی طرف متوجہ ہوتی ہے تو فعل ممنوع بن جاتا ہے اور بندہ ممنوع عنہ اور منع کرنے میں اس میں دونوں وجہوں سے زیادہ مبالغہ پایا جاتا ہے اس لئے کہ یہ تو بمنز رفی کے بن گئی اور نفی میں نہیں سے زیادہ مبالغہ پایا جاتا ہے۔
His saying: 'And prohibition added to entities like mothers and wine is literal (haqiqah) according to us, contrary to some.' Some people think that when prohibition is attributed to entities (a'yan), as in Allah's saying 'Forbidden to you are your mothers...' and in this hadith 'Wine is forbidden in its essence,' then it will be metaphorical (majaz), i.e., 'Forbidden to you is the marriage of your mothers' and 'The drinking of wine is forbidden in its essence,' because wine is an entity and it does not accept prohibition, as states and prohibitions are attributes of acts. We say that prohibition is upon its literal meaning because prohibition is of two types: (1) Prohibition that is directed toward the act, so the act becomes that which is prohibited (mamnu' 'anhu) and the servant is prohibited (mamnu'). (2) Prohibition that is directed toward the entity, so the act becomes prohibited and the servant becomes that which is prohibited (mamnu' 'anhu). And in prohibition, there is greater exaggeration in the latter due to both aspects, because it is like negation (nafy), and in negation there is greater exaggeration than in 'not' (laysa).
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific usul dispute regarding the interpretation of Quranic and prophetic phrases that attribute prohibition directly to entities (a'yan), such as 'Forbidden to you are your mothers' (Quran 4:23) and 'Wine is forbidden in its essence' (hadith). Some scholars (likely Shafi'is) argue that prohibition is an attribute of actions, not entities, so such phrases are metaphorical (majaz): the actual prohibition applies to the act (e.g., marrying mothers, drinking wine). The Hanafi position is that such prohibition is literal (haqiqah). The author presents the Hanafi argument that there are two types of prohibition: one directed at the act (tahrim al-fi'l) and one directed at the entity (tahrim al-'ayn). The latter is more emphatic because it implies the entity itself is inherently prohibited, akin to negation (nafy), which is stronger than the simple negation 'not' (laysa). This reflects the Hanafi method of using linguistic intensification (mubalaghah) as evidence for the literal meaning. The practical difference may be in legal consequences, such as the severity of sin or the applicability of penalties. The passage highlights the Hanafi commitment to the literal wording when possible, and their nuanced understanding of the categories of prohibition.

Key Terms
تحریم مضاف الی الاعیان (tahrim mudaf ila al-a'yan): Prohibition that is grammatically attributed to entities (nouns denoting substances or persons), e.g., 'hurrimat 'alaykum ummahatukum'.
تحریم الفعل (tahrim al-fi'l): Prohibition directed at the action itself, e.g., 'do not drink wine'.
تحریم العین (tahrim al-'ayn): Prohibition directed at the entity itself, e.g., 'wine is forbidden in its essence'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi justification for considering 'tahrim al-mudaf ila al-a'yan' as literal (haqiqah)?
A) They argue that entities can be prohibited just like acts.
B) They claim there are two types of prohibition: one for acts and one for entities, with the latter being more emphatic.
C) They follow a different Quranic reading.
D) They reject the existence of metaphorical language in revelation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school considers 'tahrim mudaf ila al-a'yan' as literal because they posit a second type of prohibition that mirrors negation, making it more emphatic.

The Particle Waw and Its Implications in Talaq
Explains meaning of waw and its application in triple divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the particle waw as absolute conjunction and apply it to derive the ruling in conditional triple divorce, distinguishing the roles of linguistic meaning and speech implication.

Core Idea
Waw is for absolute conjunction; its temporal meaning comes from context, not the particle itself.

In this lesson, we examine the dispute over the Arabic particle waw (وَ). The Hanafi school holds it indicates absolute conjunction without sequence or simultaneity, while the Shafi'i school sees it as sequential. This disagreement has practical consequences in the law of divorce. We will trace the proofs and see how the same particle leads to different rulings based on the implication of the speech rather than the word alone.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on waw
Identify the Hanafi claim: waw is for absolute conjunction (مطلق العطف) and does not inherently indicate sequence or simultaneity.
This is the default linguistic view.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and its evidence
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i argues waw indicates sequence. Note the cited Quranic verses (Safa and Marwa; command to bow and prostrate) and hadith.
These texts are used to support the sequential meaning.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to Shafi'i evidence
Examine how Hanafis counter: sequence in the examples is from external context (order of revelation, or other proofs), not from waw itself. The Quran also uses waw without sequence (e.g., 'and prostrate and bow').
This shows that context, not the particle, determines order.
4
Apply to triple divorce scenario
Consider the conditional statement: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and divorced and divorced.' Understand that Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on the implication (muqtada) of the speech: Abu Hanifa sees separation (افتراق) → one divorce; Sahibayn see togetherness (اجتماع) → three divorces.
This difference is not about the particle waw alone but about the overall intent.
5
Note the consensus when condition is delayed
If the condition comes at the end ('You are divorced and divorced and divorced if you enter the house'), all agree on three divorces because the suspension changes the implication.
This highlights the role of sentence structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ويتصل بماذكر ناحروف المعاني فالواؤ لمطلق العطف من غير تعرض لمقارنة ولا ترتيب . حقیقت اور مجاز کے ساتھ حروف معانی بھی متصل ہوتے ہیں کیونکہ حروف معانی اگر اپنے معنی موضوع لہ میں استعمال ہوں تو حقیقت ہو نگے اور اگر غیر معنی موضوع لہ میں استعمال ہو ں تو مجاز ہو نگے ان میں واو کو مقدم کیا۔ السواف : واد مطلق عطف کیلئے آتا ہے یعنی مطلق شرکت کیلئے تو اگر مفرد کا مفرد پر عطف ہو تو شرکت صرف محکوم علیہ یا صرف محکوم بہ میں ہوگی اور اگر جملہ کا عطف جملہ پر ہوتو شرکت ثبوت اور وجود میں ہوگی ۔ لیکن بعض لوگ کہتے ہیں کہ واؤ مقارنت اور اجتماع پر دلالت کرتی ہے اور امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ واؤ ترتیب پر دلالت کرتی ہے ان کی دلیل ان الصفا والمروۃ اور حضور کا ارشادن بنداً بما بعد اللہ اور دوسری جگہ ہے وار کفو واسجدوا اور ظاہر ہے کہ صفار کو مردہ پر اور رکوع کو مجود پر مقدم کرنا ضروری ہے۔ پہلی دلیل کا جواب یہ ہو سکتا ہے کہ ہم نے ترتیب وحی متلو سے جان لی ہے اور اس آیت کا حوالہ اس لئے دیا کیا ہے کہ تقدیم فی الذکر اہتمام سے خالی نہیں ہے۔ اور دوسری دلیل کا جواب یہ ہے کہ قرآن میں دوسری جگہ آتا ہے واسجدی وار کھی حالانکہ بجد داتور کوٹ سے مقدم نہیں ہوتا۔ قوله : وفى قوله لغير المؤطوءة ان دخلت الدار فأنت طالق وطالق وطالق انما تطلق واحدة عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله لأن موجب هذا الكلام الافتراق فلا يتغير با لو اؤ وقالا مو جبه الاجتماع فلا يتغير بالواؤ. اعتراض اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ اگر کوئی شخص اپنی غیر مدخولہ بیوی سے کہے کہ ان دخلت الدار فانت طالق وطالق وطالق تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک ایک اور صاحبین کے نزدیک تین طلاقیں واقع ہونگی تو معلوم ہوا کہ امام صاحب کے نز و یک واوتر تیب کیلئے ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک واو مقارنت کیلئے ہے۔ جواب .... یہ ترتیب اور مقارنت و ادت ذہن میں نہیں آتی ہے بلکہ کلام کے متقضی سے ذہن میں آتی ہے اس لئے کہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک اس کلام کا متقضی افتراق ہے کیونکہ اگر ایسے نہ ہوتا تو وہ یوں کہتا ان دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ثلاثا جب اس نے ثلاث نہیں کہا تو معلوم ہوا کہ اس کا مقتنہائش اور مقصد افتراق ہے تو ان میں سے ہر ایک طلاق الگ الگ واقع ہوئی لہذا جب پہلی واقع ہوگئی تو اب دوسری اور تیسری کیلئے محل ہی باقی نہیں رہا۔ لہذادہ دونوں لغو ہو گئیں۔ جب کہ صاحبین کے نزدیک کلام کا منتقصی افتراق نہیں بلکہ اجتماع ہے کیونکہ اگر ایسا نہ ہوتا تو تینوں طلاقوں کو ایک شرط کیساتھ معلق نہ کرتا لیکن جب اس نے معلق کر لیا تو تینوں اکھٹی واقع ہو جائیں گی۔ نکتہ یہ اختلاف اس وقت ہے جب کہ شرط کو مقدم کرے۔ اور اگر اس نے شرط کو مؤخر کر دیا اور یوں کہا انت طالق وطالق و طالق ان دخلت الار تو پر بالاتفاق تین طلاقیں ہو جائیں گی۔ کیونکہ کلام کے آخر میں ایسی چیز ہے جو کہ اول حصہ کو تبدیل کر دیگی وہ ہے معلق کرنا۔ قوله : واذا قال لغير المؤطونة أنت طالق وطالق وطا لق انما تبين بواحدة لأن الأول وقع قبل التكلم بالثانى وا لثالث فسقطت ولا يته لفوت محل التصرف اعتراض ... اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ جب کوئی شخص اپنی غیر مدخولہ بیوی کو بغیر شرط کے تین طلاقیں غیر شرط
He said: Connected to what was mentioned are the particles of meaning. So the waw is for absolute conjunction without indicating simultaneity or sequence. With reality and metaphor, particles of meaning are also connected because particles of meaning, if used in their original meaning, are real, and if used in a non-original meaning, are metaphorical. Among them, waw is given precedence. The explanation: Waw comes for absolute conjunction, i.e., for absolute participation. If one noun is conjoined to another, participation is only in the subject or only in the predicate. If a sentence is conjoined to a sentence, participation is in affirmation and existence. But some people say that waw indicates simultaneity and togetherness. Imam al-Shafi'i says that waw indicates sequence. Their evidence is the verse of Safa and Marwa and the Prophet's saying 'bind what is after God' and elsewhere 'and bow and prostrate'. It is obvious that Safa should be preceded over Marwa and bowing over prostration. The answer to the first evidence is that we know the sequence from the order of revelation, and this verse is mentioned because precedence in mention is not devoid of emphasis. The answer to the second evidence is that in another place in the Quran it comes 'and prostrate and bow' although bowing is not preceded by prostration. He said: And in his statement to a non-consummated wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and divorced and divorced', according to Abu Hanifa, she is divorced only one divorce, because the implication of this speech is separation, so it does not change by the waw. And the two said: Its implication is togetherness, so it does not change by the waw. Objection: It is objected that if a man says to his non-consummated wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and divorced and divorced', according to Imam Abu Hanifa, one divorce occurs, and according to the two companions, three divorces occur. So it is known that according to Imam, waw is for sequence, and according to the two companions, waw is for simultaneity. Answer: Sequence and simultaneity are not brought to mind by the wording, but by the implication of the speech. Because according to Imam, the implication of this speech is separation. For if it were not so, he would have said: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced thrice.' When he did not say thrice, it is known that his intent and purpose is separation. So each one of these divorces occurred separately. Therefore, when the first occurred, there remained no place for the second and third. Thus, both are invalid. Whereas according to the two companions, the implication of the speech is not separation but togetherness, because if it were not so, he would not have suspended all three divorces on one condition. But when he suspended them, they all occur together. Note: This difference is when he presents the condition. If he delays the condition and says: 'You are divorced and divorced and divorced if you enter the house', then by agreement three divorces occur, because at the end of the speech there is something that changes the first part, and that is the suspension. He said: And when he says to a non-consummated wife: 'You are divorced and divorced and divorced', she becomes separated by one, because the first occurred before speaking the second and third, so they are dropped due to the loss of the place of disposition. Objection: An objection is made that when a man gives three divorces without a condition to his non-consummated wife.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute regarding the meaning of the Arabic particle waw (وَ). It begins by stating the Hanafi position that waw is for absolute conjunction (مطلق العطف) and does not inherently indicate simultaneity (مقارنة) or sequence (ترتيب). It then presents the Shafi'i view that waw indicates sequence, with evidence from Quranic verses (Safa and Marwa, and command to bow and prostrate) and a hadith. The Hanafi response counters that the sequence in those examples is understood from external context (e.g., the order of revelation) or that the evidence is not conclusive because the Quran itself can present actions in non-sequential order. The discussion then shifts to a fiqh application: a man says to his non-consummated wife, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and divorced and divorced.' According to Imam Abu Hanifa, only one divorce occurs, while according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), three divorces occur. This is not because they disagree on the meaning of waw per se, but because they derive different implications from the speech. Abu Hanifa holds that the speaker's intent is separation (افتراق) — he could have said 'thrice' but didn't, so each divorce is independent; thus after the first, no place remains for the others. The Sahibayn hold that the speaker's intent is togetherness (اجتماع) — he suspended all three on one condition, so they all occur together. The passage notes that this disagreement arises only when the condition is mentioned first; if the condition is delayed (e.g., 'You are divorced and divorced and divorced if you enter the house'), there is consensus that three divorces occur. Finally, the text cites a scenario without a condition: a man says 'You are divorced and divorced and divorced' to a non-consummated wife; again, only one divorce occurs because the first takes effect before the second is spoken. This aligns with the authority of Zahir al-Riwayah (the core Hanafi texts). The commentary highlights the usul principle that the legal effect of a particle depends on the context and intent, not a fixed linguistic meaning.

Key Terms
الواو (al-waw): The Arabic letter waw used as a conjunction; the text defines it as for absolute conjunction (مطلق العطف) without indication of sequence or simultaneity.
حروف المعاني (huruf al-ma'ani): Letters/particles that carry meaning (like waw, fa, thumma). They are considered in terms of haqiqah (literal) and majaz (metaphorical) usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a man says to his non-consummated wife 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and divorced and divorced', how many divorces occur?
A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
Waw is for absolute conjunction; the legal effect of a sentence depends on its overall implication, not on the particle alone.

The Waw in Divorce: Conjunction or Sequence?
Sharh refuting a claim about waw and sequence in divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that waw indicates mere conjunction (without sequence) in divorce contexts and derive the ruling that only one divorce occurs in 'ant taliq wa taliq wa taliq'.

Core Idea
Waw does not create sequence; the first divorce ends marital authority instantly.

A common misconception holds that the word 'and' (waw) in a triple divorce statement implies a sequence of three divorces. However, Hanafi usul al-fiqh teaches that the conjunction does not carry such force. This lesson unpacks the reasoning: once the first divorce is pronounced, the husband's authority to divorce is exhausted, so no further divorces can occur. The same result holds even without waw, proving that sequence is not the operative factor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Matn's Claim
The base text (matn) claimed that waw indicates sequence (tartib) because when a husband says 'ant taliq wa taliq wa taliq,' three divorces occur.
2
Locate the Sharh's Objection
The commentary (sharh) refutes this: the real cause for only one divorce is not sequence but the immediate end of the husband's authority after the first divorce.
3
Trace the Reasoning (Ta'lil)
Apply the logic: as soon as the first divorce is pronounced, the woman becomes bainoonah (irrevocably divorced), removing the 'subject' (mahal) for any subsequent divorce. Hence, the second and third pronouncements are legally ineffective.
The same logic applies even if waw is omitted: 'ant taliq taliq taliq' also results in one divorce.
4
Determine the Hanafi Ruling
Thus, in Hanafi fiqh, a triple pronouncement with waw results in a single irrevocable divorce, not three. The waw functions as a mere conjunction (atf) without sequence.
This ruling is the mufta bihi (operative) position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دیدے یعنی یوں کہے انت طالق و طلاق و طالق تو ہمارے علماء ثالثہ کے نزدیک واقع ہوتی ہے اس سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ واو ترتیب کیلئے ہوتی ہے۔ جواب ..... جب پہلی طلاق پر تکلم کیا تو شوہر کا اختیار ختم ہو گیا کیونکہ اب دوسری اور تیسری کیلئے محل ہی باقی نہ رہا اس لئے ایک ہی طلاق واقع ہو گئی اور عورت بائنہ ہوگئی ۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر وہ بغیر واو کے انت طالق طلاق طلاق کہتا تو بھی ایک ہی طلاق سے عورت بائنہ ہو جاتی۔
I.e., if he says: 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced' then according to our three imams it occurs. From this it became known that waw is for sequence. Answer: When he spoke the first divorce, the husband's authority ended because no place remained for the second and third. Therefore only one divorce occurred and the woman becomes bainoonah. That is why if he says without waw 'you are divorced, divorced, divorced', then also from one divorce the woman becomes bainoonah.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the meaning of waw in a triple divorce statement. The matn claimed that waw indicates sequence (tarteeb) based on the occurrence of three divorces. The sharh objects that the real reason is not sequence but the exhaustion of marital authority. Once the first divorce is pronounced, the marriage ends (bainoonah), so no further divorces can take effect. Thus, only one divorce falls, and the same applies even without waw. This shows that waw does not necessarily imply sequence in divorce.

Key Terms
واو (wāw): The conjunction 'and'; here debated whether it implies sequence or mere conjunction.
ترتیب (tartīb): Sequence; the idea that one event follows another in order.
بائنہ (bā'inah): A woman who has been divorced irrevocably (ba'in), ending the marriage immediately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The waw indicates simultaneity, so all three are read as one.
B) The first divorce ends the husband's marital authority, leaving no subject for further divorces.
C) The second and third are invalid due to lack of proper intention.
D) The woman's consent is required for each subsequent divorce.

Key Takeaway
Waw in divorce statements does not create sequence; the first divorce extinguishes the husband's authority, so only one divorce occurs.
